水仙号上的黑鬼 | The Nigger of the Narcissus
<<
>>
前言 CHAPTER PREFACE
一部作品，无论多么卑微，要想成为艺术品，它的每一行都应具有成为艺术品的理由。艺术大概可以这样定义：通过揭示隐藏在每个现象下的多样或惟一的真实，给可视世界以最高公正的一种一心一意的尝试。艺术是一种企求，想从世界的形式、色彩、光亮和阴影中，从事物的种种现象和生活的种种现实中，发现各自主要、持久和根本的东西——一种具有启发作用和令人信服的品质——它们存在的实质。艺术家和思想家、科学家一样，探求真理，表达呼声。因受到世界不同方面的影响，思想家一头扎进概念，科学家则钻入事实——他们从那儿钻出后，立即呼吁：要充分利用我们人类最擅长的能力，以防止人生旅途中的风险。他们以权威的身份提到我们的常识、提到我们的智力、提到我们求安或求乱的愿望；经常提到我们的偏见、有时提到我们的恐惧、时常提到我们的自私——可总是提到我们的盲信。我们充满敬意地聆听他们讲话，因为他们关心的都是重大问题：培育我们的心智、关照我们的身体、实现我们的抱负、完善我们的方法、颂扬我们伟大的奋斗目标。 A work that aspires, however humbly, to the condition of art should carry its justification in every line.And art itself may be defined as a single-minded attempt to render the highest kind of justice to the visible universe, by bringing to light the truth, manifold and one, underlying its every aspect.It is an attempt to find in its forms, in its colours, in its light, in its shadows, in the aspects of matter and in the facts of life what of each is fundamental, what is enduring and essential—their one illuminating and convincing quality—the very truth of their existence.The artist, then, like the thinker or the scientist, seeks the truth and makes his appeal.Impressed by the aspect of the world the thinker plunges into ideas, the scientist into facts—whence, presently, emerging they make their appeal to those qualities of our being that fit us best for the hazardous enterprise of living.They speak authoritatively to our common-sense, to our intelligence, to our desire of peace or to our desire of unrest; not seldom to our prejudices, sometimes to our fears, often to our egoism—but always to our credulity.And their words are heard with reverence, for their concern is with weighty matters: with the cultivation of our minds and the proper care of our bodies, with the attainment of our ambitions, with the perfection of the means and the glorification of our precious aims.
艺术家却并非如此。 It is otherwise with the artist.
艺术家面对同样难以解释的现象时，会深入到自己内心深处，在这个充满紧张与冲突的孤独的世界里，如果他有才华和运气的话，他会找到他的呼吁方式。他呼吁的是我们潜在的能力：我们天性的那部分；由于人类危险的生存条件，这些能力不得不隐藏在坚韧、刚强的本性里面，未曾显现——仿佛被钢盔罩着的易受伤害的肉体一样。他的呼吁不那么响亮，但更深沉；不那么清楚，但更动人——而且更快被遗忘。然而它的的影响却是经久不衰。一代代人的智慧随之改变，必然会摒弃一些观点、质疑一些事实、推翻一些理论。但是，艺术家呼唤的是我们身上不依赖于智慧的东西；这种东西与生俱来，而非后天获取——因此更具永恒性。他提到我们感知欢喜和惊奇的能力，提到我们关于人生秘密的感受；提到我们对同情、美和痛苦的感受；提到对众生潜在的友爱之情——提到一种微妙而强大的信念，即人类心灵息息相通，这种信念把无数颗寂寞的心连在了一起；提到人们在梦想、欢乐、痛苦、志向、幻觉、愿望、恐惧方面的心灵相通，这种沟通把所有人——死去的、活着的和即将出生的——连在了一起。 Confronted by the same enigmatical spectacle the artist descends within himself, and in that lonely region of stress and strife, if he be deserving and fortunate, he finds the terms of his appeal.His appeal is made to our less obvious capacities: to that part of our nature which, because of the warlike conditions of existence, is necessarily kept out of sight within the more resisting and hard qualities—like the vulnerable body within a steel armour.His appeal is less loud, more profound, less distinct, more stirring—and sooner forgotten.Yet its effect endures forever.The changing wisdom of successive generations discards ideas, questions facts, demolishes theories.But the artist appeals to that part of our being which is not dependent on wisdom; to that in us which is a gift and not an acquisition—and, therefore, more permanently enduring.He speaks to our capacity for delight and wonder, to the sense of mystery surrounding our lives; to our sense of pity, and beauty, and pain; to the latent feeling of fellowship with all creation—and to the subtle but invincible conviction of solidarity that knits together the loneliness of innumerable hearts, to the solidarity in dreams, in joy, in sorrow, in aspirations, in illusions, in hope, in fear, which binds men to each other, which binds together all humanity—the dead to the living and the living to the unborn.
只有类似这样一串思想，或者干脆说是感受，才能一定程度上说明下面故事的创作目的：即为什么从众多迷惘、纯朴、默默无闻、备受漠视的人群中，选出这么几个人，描写他们卑微人生中不平静的插曲。因为，如果上面所谈的信念有一点道理的话，那么，很显然，世上没有哪个光明的地方或黑暗的角落不值得投以惊奇或同情的一瞥，哪怕是瞬间一瞥也好。因此，这个动机就可用来证明这部作品的创作价值；但这篇序言只公开谈谈所做的努力还不能止笔，因为谈得不够全面。小说——如果要想成为艺术——必须能够激发情感。事实上，小说像绘画、音乐，像所有艺术一样，是一种情感对其他无数种情感的呼唤，各种情感微妙而强大的力量赋予故事中的事件真实含义，创造出当时当地的道德与情感气氛。这种呼唤若要产生效力，必须通过各种感觉来表达；事实上，也无法通过其他途径，因为情感，无论是一个人的或是集体的，都不能通过说理得以表达。因此，所有艺术主要诉诸感觉；当艺术目的借助文字表达时，若其最高愿望是触及情感反应的秘密之源，那么，它必须诉诸各种感觉。它必须力求达到雕塑的造型、绘画的色彩、音乐的神奇启发性——这是最高的艺术。只有坚定不移地追求形式和内容的完美结合，只有毫不松懈，永不气馁地关注句子的结构和声音，才能接近造型、色彩之美，才能在平淡无奇的文字表面赋予千百年来被人们随意使用，磨得面目全非的古老词语的神奇联想之光。 It is only some such train of thought, or rather of feeling, that can in a measure explain the aim of the attempt, made in the tale which follows, to present an unrestful episode in the obscure lives of a few individuals out of all the disregarded multitude of the bewildered, the simple and the voiceless.For, if any part of truth dwells in the belief confessed above, it becomes evident that there is not a place of splendour or a dark corner of the earth that does not deserve, if only a passing glance of wonder and pity.The motive then, may be held to justify the matter of the work; but this preface, which is simply an avowal of endeavour, cannot end here—for the avowal is not yet complete.Fiction—if it at all aspires to be art—appeals to temperament.And in truth it must be, like painting, like music, like all art, the appeal of one temperament to all the other innumerable temperaments whose subtle and resistless power endows passing events with their true meaning, and creates the moral, the emotional atmosphere of the place and time.Such an appeal to be effective must be an impression conveyed through the senses; and, in fact, it cannot be made in any other way, because temperament, whether individual or collective, is not amenable to persuasion.All art, therefore, appeals primarily to the senses, and the artistic aim when expressing itself in written words must also make its appeal through the senses, if its highest desire is to reach the secret spring of responsive emotions.It must strenuously aspire to the plasticity of sculpture, to the colour of painting, and to the magic suggestiveness of music—which is the art of arts.And it is only through complete, unswerving devotion to the perfect blending of form and substance; it is only through an unremitting never-discouraged care for the shape and ring of sentences that an approach can be made to plasticity, to colour, and that the light of magic suggestiveness may be brought to play for an evanescent instant over the commonplace surface of words: of the old, old words, worn thin, defaced by ages of careless usage.
真诚努力地完成这项具有创新性的任务，不遗余力地沿着这条路走下去，不怕跌倒、困顿和指责是散文工作者应尽的职责。只要无愧于心，对于那些满心追求功名利禄，而又明确要求得到启迪、慰藉和快乐的人，还有那些要求立刻受到启智、鼓励，感到恐惧、震惊或陶醉的人，他的回答必然是：我努力完成的任务是用文字的力量让你去倾听、去感受——最重要的是，让你“看到”。别无其他，仅此而已。要是我成功了，你就可以在我书中找到你需要的东西：鼓励、安慰、恐惧、陶醉——你需要的一切——还有，也许你忘记要求的：对事实的惊鸿一瞥。凭借一时之勇，从匆匆逝去的残酷时光里抢出一个生活片段，这仅是这项任务的开端。要审慎、忠实地对待这项任务，就要毫不犹豫，不加区分、勇敢无畏地把救出的片段以真诚的心态呈现在读者眼前。去展现它的震动、色彩、形式；通过它的运动和色彩来揭示事实的本质——揭示其鼓舞人心的秘密：每个令人信服的瞬间所蕴含的紧张和激情。在这样专心致志的尝试中，如果你既有天赋又幸运的话，你的作品也许就能达到真诚无暇的程度，最终你所呈现的悔恨、同情、恐惧或欢乐必然会唤起读者心中彼此相连的感受；在人类的神秘起源、辛勤劳作、欢乐、希望或无常命运上，这种感受是彼此相连的，它把人与人连系在了一起，把人类与斑斓的世界连系在了一起。显然，坚持上述信念的人，姑且不论正确与否，他都不会忠实于创作技巧的任何一种临时原则。这些原则中最持久的东西——真实，是任何一种原则都不能完全掩盖的——应当留下来作为他最珍贵的财富，而所有这些原则：现实主义、浪漫主义、自然主义，甚至未被文坛正式接受的伤感主义（像贫穷一样，伤感主义是极其难以消除的），所有这些神灵，经过短暂的相伴之后，必然都会舍他而去——即使他刚刚步入神殿门槛——让他去琢磨他的良知，让他去体会他工作的艰辛。在这种心神不安的孤独情形下，为艺术而艺术的最高呼声也逐渐失去了它那明显不道德的声音，这声音一度多么令人激动。这种声音越来越远。它不再是一种疾呼，听起来像是耳语，总是听不太懂，但偶尔也能起到微弱的鼓舞作用。 The sincere endeavour to accomplish that creative task, to go as far on that road as his strength will carry him, to go undeterred by faltering, weariness or reproach, is the only valid justification for the worker in prose.And if his conscience is clear, his answer to those who in the fulness of a wisdom which looks for immediate profit, demand specifically to be edified, consoled, amused; who demand to be promptly improved, or encouraged, or frightened, or shocked, or charmed, must run thus:—My task which I am trying to achieve is, by the power of the written word to make you hear, to make you feel—it is, before all, to make you—see—.That—and no more, and it is everything.If I succeed, you shall find there according to your deserts: encouragement, consolation, fear, charm—all you demand—and, perhaps, also that glimpse of truth for which you have forgotten to ask. To snatch in a moment of courage, from the remorseless rush of time, a passing phase of life, is only the beginning of the task.The task approached in tenderness and faith is to hold up unquestioningly, without choice and without fear, the rescued fragment before all eyes in the light of a sincere mood.It is to show its vibration, its colour, its form; and through its movement, its form, and its colour, reveal the substance of its truth—disclose its inspiring secret: the stress and passion within the core of each convincing moment.In a single-minded attempt of that kind, if one be deserving and fortunate, one may perchance attain to such clearness of sincerity that at last the presented vision of regret or pity, of terror or mirth, shall awaken in the hearts of the beholders that feeling of unavoidable solidarity; of the solidarity in mysterious origin, in toil, in joy, in hope, in uncertain fate, which binds men to each other and all mankind to the visible world.It is evident that he who, rightly or wrongly, holds by the convictions expressed above cannot be faithful to any one of the temporary formulas of his craft.The enduring part of them—the truth which each only imperfectly veils—should abide with him as the most precious of his possessions, but they all: Realism, Romanticism, Naturalism, even the unofficial senti-mentalism (which like the poor, is exceedingly difficult to get rid of,) all these gods must, after a short period of fellowship, abandon him—even on the very threshold of the temple—to the stammerings of his conscience and to the outspoken consciousness of the difficulties of his work.In that uneasy solitude the supreme cry of Art for Art itself, loses the exciting ring of its apparent immorality.It sounds far off.It has ceased’ to be a cry, and is heard only as a whisper, often incomprehensible, but at times and faintly encouraging.
有时，我们躺在路旁的树荫下，悠闲地舒展着身体，观看远处田间劳作者的举动，过上一会儿，我们就会百无聊赖地去想，这个人到底在忙什么。我们看他活动着身体，舞动着双臂，弯下腰，站起来，迟疑下一步干什么，然后重复这些动作。倘若明白了他劳动的目的，兴许会给我们的无聊时光增添一丝乐趣。倘若知道他在使劲搬石头，挖沟或刨树根，我们就会带着更实在的兴趣看他劳动，也会宽恕他劳作时发出的噪声打破了田园的宁静；如果我们怀着兄弟般的情意，我们还会原谅他的失败。因为我们明白他的目的，毕竟，这老兄尽力了，也许他力气有限——也许他知识不够。我们原谅了他，继续赶路——并且忘了这事。 Sometimes, stretched at ease in the shade of a roadside tree, we watch the motions of a labourer in a distant field, and after a time, begin to wonder languidly as to what the fellow may be at.We watch the movements of his body, the waving of his arms, we see him bend down, stand up, hesitate, begin again.It may add to the charm of an idle hour to be told the purpose of his exertions.If we know he is trying to lift a stone, to dig a ditch, to uproot a stump, we look with a more real interest at his efforts; we are disposed to condone the jar of his agitation upon the restfulness of the landscape; and even, if in a brotherly frame of mind, we may bring ourselves to forgive his failure.We understood his object, and, after all, the fellow has tried, and perhaps he had not the strength—and perhaps he had not the knowledge.We forgive, go on our way—and forget.
艺术工作者同样如此。艺术是漫长的，生命是短暂的，因而，成功遥遥无期。所以，当我们怀疑是否有力气走那么远的时候，我们不妨可以聊几句目的——艺术的目的，像生命一样，催人奋进，却异常艰难——层层迷雾遮掩住了它。艺术的目的不在获取成功结论的清晰逻辑里，也不在揭开称为自然法则的某个无情秘密中。艺术的目的同样伟大，却更难企及。 And so it is with the workman of art.Art is long and life is short, and success is very far off. And thus, doubtful of strength to travel so far, we talk a little about the aim—the aim of art, which, like life itself, is inspiring, difficult—obscured by mists—It is not in the clear logic of a triumphant conclusion; it is not in the unveiling of one of those heartless secrets which are called the Laws of Nature.It is not less great, but only more difficult.
让那些忙于经纶事务的人们停下来，喘口气，逼那些沉醉于自己远大目标的人们瞥一眼周围的形、色、光、影；使他们止住脚步看一眼，叹一声，笑一下——这就是目标，难以企及，瞬间即逝，并且只有少数人才能完成。然而有时候，有才能的幸运儿即便是如此艰巨的任务也照样能够完成。任务一旦完成，看吧——所有生活的真实就会尽收眼底：一瞬风景，一声叹息，一个微笑——然后回归到永恒的平静。 To arrest, for the space of a breath, the hands busy about the work of the earth, and compel men entranced by the sight of distant goals to glance for a moment at the surrounding vision of form and colour, of sunshine and shadows; to make them pause for a look, for a sigh, for a smile—such is the aim, difficult and evanescent, and reserved only for a very few to achieve.But sometimes, by the deserving and the fortunate, even that task is accomplished.And when it is accomplished—behold!—all the truth of life is there: a moment of vision, a sigh, a smile—and the return to an eternal rest.
1897年约瑟夫·康拉德 1897.0J. C.
“水仙号”上的黑鬼 THE NIGGER OF THE “NARCISSUS”
第一章 CHAPTER ONE
“水仙号”大副——贝克先生，大跨一步从明亮的船舱来到黑暗的后甲板。值夜班的人在他头顶上方，沿着船尾楼的边沿敲了两下钟。九点了。贝克先生大声问上面那人：“所有船员都上船了吗，诺尔斯？” Mr. Baker, chief mate of the ship—Narcissus—, stepped in one stride out of his lighted cabin into the darkness of the quarter-deck.Above his head, on the break of the poop, the night-watchman rang a double stroke.It was nine o’clock.Mr. Baker, speaking up to the man above him, asked:—“Are all the hands aboard, Knowles?”
那人一瘸一拐地走下扶梯，然后慢悠悠地答道： The man limped down the ladder, then said reflectively:—
“我想都上来了，先生。所有的老伙计都到了，许多新船员也来了……他们一定都在那边呢。” “I think so, sir.All our old chaps are there, and a lot of new men has come....They must be all there.”
“通知水手长把大家召集到船尾，”贝克先生接着说道，“叫个年轻人送一盏亮些的灯来。我要清点一下人数。” “Tell the boatswain to send all hands aft,” went on Mr. Baker; “and tell one of the youngsters to bring a good lamp here.I want to muster our crowd.”
船尾主甲板一片黑暗，但轮船前部的中间，两道耀眼的灯光从前甲板下面的水手舱敞开的门里射出，刺破了笼罩着轮船的静谧夜空。那边传来一阵阵嗡嗡声，同时，船左右两弦明亮的过道里一下子人影绰绰，黑黑的，毫无立体感，好像薄铁皮做成的人像。轮船一切准备停当，就等起航。木匠把最后一块楔子打入主舱口的木板中，撂下锤子，不慌不忙地擦着脸，这时，钟响了五下。甲板已经打扫干净，锚机上好油准备起锚；粗大的拖索打着长长的圈躺在主甲板一侧，一端向上，挂在船头，迎接拖船的到来。明天，拖船将打破清早的清澈、凉爽与宁静，拍打着水花，冒着热气和浓烟，轰鸣而来。船长还在岸上，他一直在招募新船员，好配齐船上的所有工作人员；一天的工作结束了，船上的高级职员回去休息了，他们乐得享受一小会儿自由时间。天黑不久，几个被允许到岸上去的船员和新招的船员乘坐岸边小船开始返回；驾驶小船的是身着白衣的亚洲人；他们还没等把船划到舷梯旁，就大声嚷嚷着要船钱。于是，狂乱刺耳、喋喋不休的东方语言与醉醺醺的海员的蛮横话语交织在一起，海员用粗话大声斥责船家的无耻勒索和卑鄙企图。他们为了五个安那到半个卢比这一点点小钱争吵不休，把东方这布满群星的、美丽而宁静的夜晚搅得粉碎；在孟买港乘船的人没有谁不知道“水仙号”的新水手正在上船。 The main deck was dark aft, but halfway from forward, through the open doors of the forecastle, two streaks of brilliant light cut the shadow of the quiet night that lay upon the ship.A hum of voices was heard there, while port and starboard, in the illuminated doorways, silhouettes of moving men appeared for a moment, very black, without relief, like figures cut out of sheet tin. The ship was ready for sea. The carpenter had driven in the last wedge of the mainhatch battens, and, throwing down his maul, had wiped his face with great deliberation, just on the stroke of five.The decks had been swept, the windlass oiled and made ready to heave up the anchor; the big tow-rope lay in long bights along one side of the main deck, with one end carried up and hung over the bows, in readiness for the tug that would come paddling and hissing noisily, hot and smoky, in the limpid, cool quietness of the early morning.The captain was ashore, where he had been engaging some new hands to make up his full crew; and, the work of the day over, the ship’s officers had kept out of the way, glad of a little breathing-time.Soon after dark the few liberty-men and the new hands began to arrive in shore-boats rowed by white-clad Asiatics, who clamoured fiercely for payment before coming alongside the gangway-ladder.The feverish and shrill babble of Eastern language struggled against the masterful tones of tipsy seamen, who argued against brazen claims and dishonest hopes by profane shouts.The resplendent and bestarred peace of the East was torn into squalid tatters by howls of rage and shrieks of lament raised over sums ranging from five annas to half a rupee; and every soul afloat in Bombay Harbour became aware that the new hands were joining the—Narcissus—.
渐渐地，吵闹声平息下来。此刻，小船不再是三、四只簇拥在一起拍打着海水同时驶来，而是一只只驶向大船，人们低声讨价还价，嗡嗡响作一团。这时，一个黑影肩上扛着一个长袋子，摇摇晃晃地爬着舷梯，大声说道：“一个铜子也不会多给。见鬼去吧！”这打断了那片嗡嗡声。前甲板下面的水手舱里堆放着一些绳捆索绑的箱子和一捆捆铺盖，新来的水手在它们之间穿梭或是站立着，他们正在结识老水手。这些老水手坐在双层床上，挑剔而友好地端详着未来的船友。水手舱里高高地挂着两盏灯，发出强烈的光芒；水手上岸时戴的圆帽被远远地推到后脑勺上，或者滚落在甲板上的锁链中；敞开的白色领口伸展在红色脸庞两侧；白色袖口中粗壮的胳膊挥舞着；叽里咕噜声响个不停，中间夹杂着哄然的笑声和嘶哑的叫声。“喂，小子，就睡这张铺吧！……别这样！……你上次呆的是哪条船？……我知道那条船……三年前，在普及特海峡……这张铺漏水，我告诉你！……过来；让我们晃晃箱子！……可带了一瓶酒，你们这些岸上的阔少爷？……给我们一点烟丝……我知道那条船；她的船长喝酒喝死啦……他是个花花公子！……喜欢在身上擦一下润肤露，他的确如此！……别嚷嚷啦！……闭上嘴，你们这帮家伙！……我告诉你们，你们登上了一条破船，他们每花一分钱，你们这些可怜虫就得卖一份力！……” Gradually the distracting noise had subsided.The boats came no longer in splashing clusters of three or four together, but dropped alongside singly, in a subdued buzz of expostulation cut short by a “Not a pice more!You go to the devil!” from some man staggering up the accommodation-ladder—a dark figure, with a long bag poised on the shoulder.In the forecastle the newcomers, upright and swaying amongst corded boxes and bundles of bedding, made friends with the old hands, who sat one above another in the two tiers of bunks, gazing at their future shipmates with glances critical but friendly.The two forecastle lamps were turned up high, and shed an intense hard glare; shore-going round hats were pushed far on the backs of heads, or rolled about on the deck amongst the chain-cables; white collars, undone, stuck out on each side of red faces; big arms in white sleeves gesticulated; the growling voices hummed steady amongst bursts of laughter and hoarse calls.“Here, sonny, take that bunk!...Don’t you do it!...What’s your last ship?...I know her....Three years ago, in Puget Sound....This here berth leaks, I tell you!...Come on; give us a chance to swing that chest!...Did you bring a bottle, any of you shore toffs?...Give us a bit of ’baccy....I know her; her skipper drank himself to death....He was a dandy boy!...Liked his lotion inside, he did!...No!...Hold your row, you chaps!...I tell you, you came on board a hooker, where they get their money’s worth out of poor Jack, by—!...”
一个小个子，名叫克雷克，绰号贝尔法斯特，肆意诋毁着这艘船，他一贯胡编乱扯，好给新船员提供一些闲来琢磨的素材。阿奇侧身坐在衣箱上，双膝收拢，腾开过道；他稳稳当当地穿针引线，用一块白布补一条蓝裤子。穿黑色立领短上衣的人与赤脚光膀穿花衬衣、露着毛茸茸胸脯的人混在一起，在水手舱中间彼此碰来撞去。这群人摇摇摆摆，晃晃悠悠，喷云吐雾，争执不休。人人抢着说话，说一句骂一声。一个俄裔芬兰人身穿带有粉红色条纹的黄衬衫，正用恍惚的目光，透过乱蓬蓬的头发，凝视着上方。两个大个子年轻人长着白净的娃娃脸——两个斯堪的纳维亚人——相互帮忙打开铺盖，悄无声息，面对这种穷开心的无聊咒骂的热闹场面安详地微笑着。老辛格尔顿，船上年龄最大的一级水手，坐在甲板一侧，头顶正对着空中挂着的那两盏灯，上身脱得精光，结实的胸部和硕大的二头肌满是刺青，活像吃人族的首领。他白皙的皮肤凸显在蓝红相间的纹身之间，闪着缎子般的光，光背靠在船头斜桅的根部，手里拿着一本书，书离他那张黑黝黝的大脸约有一臂之遥。他戴着眼镜，留着令人起敬的花白胡须，俨然是一位博学而又野蛮的长老。他这个蛮人智慧的化身，在这样一个亵渎神灵的混乱境地，仍能保持宁静的心境。他全神贯注；翻动书页时，庄重好奇的表情便由他那布满皱纹的面容显现出来。他正在读《佩勒姆》。鲍沃尔·李敦的作品在南行船只的船舱里深受欢迎，真是令人惊奇的怪现象。在这些生活在黑暗漂泊环境中的大孩子们的单纯心灵里，他那精雕细琢、怪诞离奇的语句，究竟会唤起什么样的思绪呢？他们质朴、天真的灵魂在书中华丽的词藻里会发现什么意义呢？是刺激？——是忘却？——是安慰？太神秘了！是不可理解带来的魅力？还是不可能的事情带来的吸引力？或者这些生活在社会规范之外的人们受了他的故事的蛊惑？就像这个华丽的世界被不可思议地揭露开来，充盈了丑行与污秽，布满了污垢与饥饿、痛苦与荒淫。这一切从四面八方汇入不朽的大海，这就是他们所了解的生活全部，所看到的四周陆地的全部——这些大海的终身囚徒啊！真是神秘呀！辛格尔顿从十二岁起就登上了南行的船，在过去的四十五年中，（我们根据他的履历推算）他在岸上的时间不超过四十个月——老辛格尔顿，用他那长期养成的温和性情自豪地说道，一天又一天，他从一艘船上结算完工钱，再到另一艘船上工作，通常忙得分不清白天黑夜——老辛格尔顿一动不动地坐着，不顾嘈杂的说话声和叫喊声，一字一句慢慢读着《佩勒姆》，完全为情节所深深吸引，简直到了痴迷地步。他均匀地呼吸着。每当他用硕大黝黑的双手翻动书页时，他那白净粗壮胳膊上的肌肉便会随着光滑的皮肤微微晃动。白色的胡须遮住了嘴唇，唇上烟草色唾液顺着胡须流淌下来，双唇微动，似在低语。他昏花的双眼透过闪光的黑边眼镜凝视着。对面，在他面部的同一水平面上，船上的猫像蹲伏的怪兽坐在绞盘滚筒上，眨着绿色的眼睛望着它的老友。它似乎想纵身一跳，越过坐在辛格尔顿脚边的一位普通海员弓着的脊背，直接跳到老人膝上。查利年纪轻轻，身材单薄，脖颈很长。透过旧衬衣，他的脊背宛如一列起伏的小山。他的脸就像街上野孩子的脸——早熟、聪慧、略带讥讽，又薄又宽的嘴唇两侧有两道深深向下的皱纹——低低地垂向他那瘦骨嶙峋的膝部。他正用一节旧绳子学打绳头结。他凸出的额头上浸出了点点小汗珠，他用忙碌的眼角瞥着老水手，鼻腔里不时发出呼哧呼哧的响声。老水手根本就没有注意这位一脸迷惑的对工作喃喃抱怨的小伙子。 A little fellow, called Craik and nicknamed Belfast, abused the ship violently, romancing on principle, just to give the new hands something to think over.Archie, sitting aslant on his sea-chest, kept his knees out of the way, and pushed the needle steadily through a white patch in a pair of blue trousers.Men in black jackets and stand-up collars, mixed with men bare-footed, bare-armed, with coloured shirts open on hairy chests, pushed against one another in the middle of the forecastle.The group swayed, reeled, turning upon itself with the motion of a scrimmage, in a haze of tobacco smoke.All were speaking together, swearing at every second word.A Russian Finn, wearing a yellow shirt with pink stripes, stared upwards, dreamy-eyed, from under a mop of tumbled hair.Two young giants with smooth, baby faces—two Scandinavians—helped each other to spread their bedding, silent, and smiling placidly at the tempest of good-humoured and meaningless curses.Old Singleton, the oldest able seaman in the ship, set apart on the deck right under the lamps, stripped to the waist, tattooed like a cannibal chief all over his powerful chest and enormous biceps.Between the blue and red patterns his white skin gleamed like satin; his bare back was propped against the heel of the bowsprit, and he held a book at arm’s length before his big, sunburnt face.With his spectacles and a venerable white beard, he resembled a learned and savage patriarch, the incarnation of barbarian wisdom serene in the blasphemous turmoil of the world.He was intensely absorbed, and as he turned the pages an expression of grave surprise would pass over his rugged features.He was reading “Pelham.” The popularity of Bulwer Lytton in the forecastles of Southern-going ships is a wonderful and bizarre phenomenon.What ideas do his polished and so curiously insincere sentences awaken in the simple minds of the big children who people those dark and wandering places of the earth?What meaning can their rough, inexperienced souls find in the elegant verbiage of his pages?What excitement?—what forgetfulness?—what appeasement?Mystery! Is it the fascination of the incomprehensible?—is it the charm of the impossible?Or are those beings who exist beyond the pale of life stirred by his tales as by an enigmatical disclosure of a resplendent world that exists within the frontier of infamy and filth, within that border of dirt and hunger, of misery and dissipation, that comes down on all sides to the water’s edge of the incorruptible ocean, and is the only thing they know of life, the only thing they see of surrounding land—those life-long prisoners of the sea?Mystery!Singleton, who had sailed to the southward since the age of twelve, who in the last forty-five years had lived (as we had calculated from his papers) no more than forty months ashore—old Singleton, who boasted, with the mild composure of long years well spent, that generally from the day he was paid off from one ship till the day he shipped in another he seldom was in a condition to distinguish daylight—old Singleton sat unmoved in the clash of voices and cries, spelling through “Pelham” with slow labour, and lost in an absorption profound enough to resemble a trance.He breathed regularly.Every time he turned the book in his enormous and blackened hands the muscles of his big white arms rolled slightly under the smooth skin.Hidden by the white moustache, his lips, stained with tobacco-juice that trickled down the long beard, moved in inward whisper.His bleared eyes gazed fixedly from behind the glitter of black-rimmed glasses.Opposite to him, and on a level with his face, the ship’s cat sat on the barrel of the windlass in the pose of a crouching chimera, blinking its green eyes at its old friend.It seemed to meditate a leap on to the old man’s lap over the bent back of the ordinary seaman who sat at Singleton’s feet.Young Charley was lean and long-necked.The ridge of his backbone made a chain of small hills under the old shirt.His face of a street-boy—a face precocious, sagacious, and ironic, with deep downward folds on each side of the thin, wide mouth—hung low over his bony knees.He was learning to make a lanyard knot with a bit of an old rope.Small drops of perspiration stood out on his bulging forehead; he sniffed strongly from time to time, glancing out of the corners of his restless eyes at the old seaman, who took no notice of the puzzled youngster muttering at his work.
喧闹声越来越大。在闷热的船舱里，矮个子贝尔法斯特就像因为暴怒而沸腾起来一样，十分滑稽。他的眼珠跳舞似的来回转动；脸涨得通红，就像戴着面具，非常可笑，打着呵欠的嘴像黑洞，样子十分古怪。他对面是一个衣服脱了一半的男人，双手捧腹，笑得前仰后合，满眼是泪。其他人都用好奇的目光注视着他。蜷曲着身子坐在上铺的人们抽着短烟斗，他们褐色的光脚晃来晃去，下面的人懒散地坐在衣箱上听着，带着傻傻的或是讥讽的微笑。一些挤眉弄眼的人把头从白色的床沿上探了出来，但身子却隐没在昏暗的床里面，床铺就像刷过白灰、灯光照亮的停尸房中盛放棺材的狭窄壁龛。嗡嗡声越来越响。阿奇抿着嘴唇挤了进来，似乎要把身体缩到一个更小的空间里，手里一针一针不停地缝着什么，勤勉而沉默。贝尔法斯特就像伊斯兰教的托钵僧得了神谕一般，尖声叫道：“……于是我对他说，‘先生，对不起。’我对那艘船上的二副说，‘先生，对不起，商务局的人一定是喝醉了才发给你执照的！’‘你说什么，你——’他叫道，像一头发疯的公牛冲向我……穿着一身白衣服；我举起焦油桶，对着他那倒霉的漂亮脸蛋和漂亮短上衣猛泼下去……嘴里喊道：‘接住吧！’‘不管咋说，我是个水手，你这个爱打小报告、爱巴结船长、无用、多余的桥墩子，你！我就是这样的人！’我叫道……可惜，你们没有见到他那又蹦又跳的狼狈样，伙计们！满身是焦油，睁不开眼，简直是！所以……” The noise increased.Little Belfast seemed, in the heavy heat of the forecastle, to boil with facetious fury.His eyes danced; in the crimson of his face, comical as a mask, the mouth yawned black, with strange grimaces.Facing him, a half-undressed man held his sides, and, throwing his head back, laughed with wet eyelashes.Others stared with amazed eyes.Men sitting doubled up in the upper bunks smoked short pipes, swinging bare brown feet above the heads of those who, sprawling below on sea-chests, listened, smiling stupidly or scornfully.Over the white rims of berths stuck out heads with blinking eyes; but the bodies were lost in the gloom of those places, that resembled narrow niches for coffins in a whitewashed and lighted mortuary.Voices buzzed louder.Archie, with compressed lips, drew himself in, seemed to shrink into a smaller space, and sewed steadily, industrious and dumb.Belfast shrieked like an inspired Dervish:—“...So I seez to him, boys, seez I, ‘Beggin’ yer pardon, sorr’, seez I to that second mate of that steamer—‘beggin’ your-r-r pardon, sorr, the Board of Trade must ’ave been drunk when they granted you your certificate!’ ‘What do you say, you———!’ seez he, comin’ at me like a mad bull...all in his white clothes; and I up with my tar-pot and capsizes it all over his blamed lovely face and his lovely jacket....‘Take that!’ seez I.‘I am a sailor, anyhow, you nosing, skipper-licking, useless, sooperfloos bridge-stanchion, you!That’s the kind of man I am!’ shouts I....You should have seed him skip, boys!Drowned, blind with tar, he was!So...”
“别听他瞎吹！他根本就没有泼什么焦油，我也在场！”有人大声嚷嚷道。两个挪威人并排坐在衣箱上，长相相似，神态安详，宛如一双栖息在树枝上的情侣鸟，圆圆的眼睛天真地注视着大家；但那位俄裔芬兰人在这雷鸣般的叫声和哄堂的笑声中却一动不动，显得疲惫乏力、无精打采，仿佛没有脊椎的聋子。阿奇挨着他，对着缝衣针笑。一个胸膛宽阔、目光迟钝的新船员趁着喧闹声临时平息的间隙，小心翼翼地对贝尔法斯特说：“我真纳闷，有像你这样的家伙呆在船上，还有船员能够活下来！我想，要是你过去把他们都驯服了，他们现在也不至于这么坏，小子。” “Don’t ’ee believe him!He never upset no tar; I was there!” shouted somebody.The two Norwegians sat on a chest side by side, alike and placid, resembling a pair of love-birds on a perch, and with round eyes stared innocently; but the Russian Finn, in the racket of explosive shouts and rolling laughter, remained motionless, limp and dull, like a deaf man without a backbone.Near him Archie smiled at his needle.A broad-chested, slow-eyed newcomer spoke deliberately to Belfast during an exhausted lull in the noise:—“I wonder any of the mates here are alive yet with such a chap as you on board!I conclude they ain’t that bad now, if you had the taming of them, sonny.”
“不坏！不坏！”贝尔法斯特尖叫道。要不是我们抱成团……不坏！他们可真是不坏，只要他们没逮着机会丧心病狂地做坏事……” “Not bad!Not bad!” screamed Belfast.“If it wasn’t for us sticking together....Not bad!They ain’t never bad when they ain’t got a chawnce, blast their black ’arts....”
他飞沫四溅，挥舞着双臂，突然龇牙咧嘴，从衣兜里掏出一片黑色烟叶，怪模怪样地狠狠咬了一块。另一个新船员——长着一双机灵的眼睛，一副瘦削蜡黄的面孔，一直坐在船舱中间柜子的阴影里，微微张着嘴巴倾听着——此刻他像鸭子一样呱呱地叫了起来：“喂，不管怎么说，这是返航回家。坏也罢，好也罢，反正我觉着像睡觉一样容易——只要能到家就行。我会维护自己的权利的！我会让他们见识见识！”所有人都把目光转向了他。只有那位普通海员和猫没有理会。他站在那里，双手叉腰，个头矮小，长着白色睫毛。看样子他似乎早已领略过人世间的所有屈辱和愤恨。他好像曾经被人拳打脚踢，扔到污泥中；他仿佛被人撕扯唾弃，遭到污言秽语谩骂过……然而，他仍能笑脸面对周围的人，仍然觉得自己是安全的。在破毡帽的重压下，他的耳朵耷拉着。黑大衣尾部已经磨破，布条在他后腿处荡来荡去。他解开仅存的两个扣子，谁都看得到他里面没穿衬衣。想不出谁还会穿如此破烂的衣服，他穿着就像是偷来的，这也是他应受的罪。他的脖子又细又长，眼皮红红的，稀疏的胡须挂在下巴上，肩膀瘦削耷拉着，仿佛鸟儿折断的翅膀。身子左侧满是泥巴，可见最近在泥沟里睡过。不久前，由于一时疏忽犯傻，他竟混进一艘美国船，要不是溜得快，他这无用的躯壳免不了一顿毒打。无奈只得在当地海滨流浪了两周，讨点酒喝，忍饥挨饿，睡垃圾堆，在太阳下四处瞎转：真是一位来自梦魇世界的惊人访客。突然周围一阵沉默，他站在那里傻笑，令人生厌。干净雪白的水手舱倒成了他的避难所；在这里他可以懒散度日；可以打滚、闲躺、混吃——并且诅咒吃的食物；他可以展示偷懒、行骗、乞讨的才能；他必定能找到欺骗和欺负的对象——并且因此而得到回报。他们都了解他。这个不祥的幸存者证明了撒谎和无耻是永远可行的，难道世间还有什么地方不知道这个人吗？一位沉默寡言的长臂老水手，长着钩形的手指，一直仰卧在铺上抽烟，这时转过脸来冷冷地打量着他，然后，从他头顶上对着门猛啐了一长条雪白的唾沫。谁都知道他！他这个人一不会掌舵，二不会结绳，黑夜里只会偷懒；在桅杆高处，他只会用双臂和双腿发疯似地紧抱桅杆，诅咒狂风、雪雨、黑暗；别人工作时，他则会咒骂大海。当全体船员被召唤时，他总是最后一个出去，最先一个回来。他多半事情不会干，会干的又不愿干。然而，他却是慈善家和追逐私利的旱鸭子的宠儿。好一个值得同情和赞赏的人啊，对自己的权利一清二楚，而对勇气、毅力、心照不宣的信念和把全体船员凝聚在一起的不言而喻的忠诚浑然不知。这个生长在贫民窟中，无人管束，过着放荡不羁生活的家伙，对海上严酷的劳作充满了鄙视与痛恨。 He foamed, whirling his arms, then suddenly grinned and, taking a tablet of black tobacco out of his pocket, bit a piece off with a funny show of ferocity.Another new hand—a man with shifty eyes and a yellow hatchet face, who had been listening open-mouthed in the shadow of the midship locker—observed in a squeaky voice:—“Well, it’s a ‘omeward trip, anyhow.Bad or good, I can do it on my ’ed—s‘long as I get ’ome.And I can look after my rights!I will show ’em!”All the heads turned towards him.Only the ordinary seaman and the cat took no notice.He stood with arms akimbo, a little fellow with white eyelashes.He looked as if he had known all the degradations and all the furies.He looked as if he had been cuffed, kicked, rolled in the mud; he looked as if he had been scratched, spat upon, pelted with unmentionable filth...and he smiled with a sense of security at the faces around.His ears were bending down under the weight of his battered felt hat. The torn tails of his black coat flapped in fringes about the calves of his legs.He unbuttoned the only two buttons that remained and every one saw that he had no shirt under it.It was his deserved misfortune that those rags which nobody could possibly be supposed to own looked on him as if they had been stolen.His neck was long and thin; his eyelids were red; rare hairs hung about his jaws; his shoulders were peaked and drooped like the broken wings of a bird; all his left side was caked with mud which showed that he had lately slept in a wet ditch.He had saved his inefficient carcass from violent destruction by running away from an American ship where, in a moment of forgetful folly, he had dared to engage himself; and he had knocked about for a fortnight ashore in the native quarter, cadging for drinks, starving, sleeping on rubbish-heaps, wandering in sunshine: a startling visitor from a world of nightmares.He stood repulsive and smiling in the sudden silence.This clean white forecastle was his refuge; the place where he could be lazy; where he could wallow, and lie and eat—and curse the food he ate; where he could display his talents for shirking work, for cheating, for cadging; where he could find surely some one to wheedle and some one to bully—and where he would be paid for doing all this.They all knew him.Is there a spot on earth where such a man is unknown, an ominous survival testifying to the eternal fitness of lies and impudence?A taciturn long-armed shellback, with hooked fingers, who had been lying on his back smoking, turned in his bed to examine him dispassionately, then, over his head, sent a long jet of clear saliva towards the door.They all knew him!He was the man that cannot steer, that cannot splice, that dodges the work on dark nights; that, aloft, holds on frantically with both arms and legs, and swears at the wind, the sleet, the darkness; the man who curses the sea while others work.The man who is the last out and the first in when all hands are called.The man who can’t do most things and won’t do the rest.The pet of philanthropists and self-seeking landlubbers.The sympathetic and deserving creature that knows all about his rights, but knows nothing of courage, of endurance, and of the unexpressed faith, of the unspoken loyalty that knits together a ship’s company.The independent offspring of the ignoble freedom of the slums full of disdain and hate for the austere servitude of the sea.
有人对他大声问道：“你叫什么名字？”“唐金。”他答道，兴致勃勃地环顾四周，毫无羞愧之色。“你是干什么的？”另外一个人问道。“啊，和你一样当水手，老兄。”他以一种听起来很豪爽其实很鲁莽的语气答道。“我敢说你那鬼样子比烧伤的消防员还寒碜。”有人自信地低声评论道。查利抬起头，用无礼的声音尖声叫道：“他是个人，是个水手。”——他用手背抹了一把鼻涕，又埋头摆弄他那节绳子。几个人哈哈大笑。其他人迷惑不解地睁大了眼。那个衣衫破烂的新水手被激怒了：“这是水手舱里欢迎新水手的得体方式吗？”他愤怒地叫道。“你们是人还是一群吃人的凶残野人？”“不要因为一句话就甩掉衬衣大动干戈。”贝尔法斯特叫着，跳到前面，神情暴躁、凶狠，但又有几分友善。“难道那家伙眼瞎了吗？”穿破烂衣服的人不依不饶地问道，左右看看，故作吃惊。“难道他看不见我没穿衬衣吗？” Some one cried at him: “What’s your name?”—“Donkin,” he said, looking round with cheerful effrontery.—“What are you?” asked another voice.—“Why, a sailor like you, old man,” he replied, in a tone that meant to be hearty but was impudent.—“Blamme if you don’t look a blamed sight worse than a broken-down fireman,” was the comment in a convinced mutter.Charley lifted his head and piped in a cheeky voice: “He is a man and a sailor”—then wiping his nose with the back of his hand bent down industriously over his bit of rope.A few laughed.Others stared doubtfully.The ragged newcomer was indignant—“That’s a fine way to welcome a chap into a fo‘c’sle,” he snarled.“Are you men or a lot of ’artless canny-bals?”—“Don’t take your shirt off for a word, shipmate,” called out Belfast, jumping up in front, fiery, menacing, and friendly at the same time.—“Is that ‘ere bloke blind?” asked the indomitable scarecrow, looking right and left with affected surprise.“Can’t ’ee see I ’aven’t got no shirt?”
他两臂交叉，晃着挂在骨架上的破烂衣衫，样子十分滑稽。 He held both his arms out crosswise and shook the rags that hung over his bones with dramatic effect.
“因为啥原因呢？”他继续大声说道，“狠心的美国佬要踏破我的肚子，因为我像个好人一样要争取自己的权利。我是英国人，我确实是。他们要揍我，我只得逃。就是因为这。难道你们就没见过遭难的人吗？呀！这是什么破船？我现在可是个穷光蛋。我一无所有。没有包，没有床，没有毛毯，没有衬衣——除了我身上穿的这身衣服外，没有一件多余的破烂衣服。但我有胆量，敢与美国佬斗。在座的‘哪位’能给你们的朋友匀出一条旧裤子？” “‘Cos why?” he continued very loud.“The bloody Yankees been tryin’ to jump my guts out ‘cos I stood up for my rights like a good ’un.I am an Englishman, I am.They set upon me an‘ I ’ad to run.That’s why.A‘n’t yer never seed a man ’ard up?Yah! What kind of blamed ship is this?I’m dead broke.I ’aven’t got nothink.No bag, no bed, no blanket, no shirt—not a bloomin‘ rag but what I stand in.But I ’ad the ‘art to stand up agin’ them Yankees.‘As any of you ’art enough to spare a pair of old pants for a chum?”
他知道该如何去征服这伙人纯洁的天性。刹那间，众人争相给予他同情，尽管有点滑稽、轻蔑或粗鲁；第一个同情的表示是扔向他的一条毛毯，当时他就站在那里，雪白的四肢从他那奇妙的黑色破布中裸露出来，表明他与其他人的同类关系。接着是一双旧鞋，落在他那沾满泥的脚旁。又有人吆喝一声：“看下边！”一条卷着的帆布裤子，沾满了厚厚的焦油，打在了他的肩上。一阵仁爱之风在他们困惑的心中掀起了同情的波澜。他们为减轻船友的痛苦鼎力相助，就连自身也深为感动。许多声音嚷嚷道：“我们要把你打扮得整整齐齐，老伙计。” 还有一些人低语道：“从没见过穿着这么寒碜的人……可怜的叫花子……我有一件旧衬衫……这你用得着吗？……拿去吧，伙计……”这些友好的低语声传遍了水手舱。他用光脚四下扒东西，把它们拢到一堆，看看周围是否还有人送。冷漠的阿奇敷衍似地把自己没有顶的旧布帽捐到这堆衣物上。老辛格尔顿沉浸在小说祥和的意境中，继续往下读，对眼前发生的事毫不理会。查利年轻聪明但缺乏同情心，他尖声叫道：“你的新制服需要铜扣子的话，我可以送你两颗。”接受大家施舍的那个肮脏家伙冲年轻人挥了挥拳头。“我非叫你把水手舱打扫干净不可，小崽子，”他凶狠地咆哮道，“你不用害怕。我不过是叫你学会对一位一级水手要有礼貌，你这浑小子。”　他恶狠狠地瞪着眼，发现辛格尔顿合上了书；他那双警觉的小眼在各个床铺之间来回巡视。“就睡那边靠门的那张床吧——那张床不错。”贝尔法斯特建议道。他接受了建议，把脚边的施舍品卷成一团抱在胸前，然后仔细地打量着那位俄裔芬兰人，只见那芬兰人站在一边，目光迷茫，也许正凝视着经常出现在他同族人脑海中的一种奇异幻景吧。“让开，外国佬。”挨过美国人揍的那家伙说道。芬兰人没有动——根本没有听见。“滚开，该死的家伙。”另一位叫道，用臂肘将他推到一边。“滚远点，你这该死的又聋又哑的傻瓜。滚开。”那个人趔趔趄趄，好不容易才站稳，默默地望着讲话人。“你们这些可恶的外国人应当睡下铺，”和善的唐金对着水手舱发表高见，“你要是不叫他们呆在一边，他们就会跟你挤来挤去。”他把全部家当撂到那张空空荡荡的铺上后，脸上再次露出狡黠的神情，思忖着下一步行动的危险性，然后跳到呆立沉思的芬兰人面前。“我来教你振作起来，”他大叫道，“我来给你捅眼睛，你这个大傻瓜。”这会儿，多数人都上床了，水手舱地板上空空荡荡，只有他们两人。穷鬼唐金的举动引起了大家的兴趣。他衣衫褴褛地在惊讶的芬兰人面前舞拳弄脚，隔着一段距离，对着对方严肃、镇定的面容摆出架势。一两个人怂恿着叫道：“开始，动手呀！”而他们自己却舒舒服服地坐在床上观战。其他人叫道：“不要胡闹啦……去用被子蒙住头睡觉吧！”又是一阵喧闹声。突然，上面传来绞盘棒重重敲击甲板的声音，就像发射的小炮穿过，震得水手舱隆隆作响。接着门外便响起水手长的声音，拖长的腔调里带着几分威严：“下面的人，你们听见没有？船尾集合！船尾集合，清点人数！” He knew how to conquer the naive instincts of that crowd.In a moment they gave him their compassion, jocularly, contemptuously, or surlily; and at first it took the shape of a blanket thrown at him as he stood there with the white skin of his limbs showing his human kinship through the black fantasy of his rags.Then a pair of old shoes fell at his muddy feet.With a cry:—“From under,” a rolled-up pair of canvas trousers, heavy with tar stains, struck him on the shoulder.The gust of their benevolence sent a wave of sentimental pity through their doubting hearts.They were touched by their own readiness to alleviate a shipmate’s misery.Voices cried:—“We will fit you out, old man.” Murmurs:“Never seed seech a hard case.... Poor beggar....I’ve got an old singlet....Will that be of any use to you?...Take it, matey....”Those friendly murmurs filled the forecastle.He pawed around with his naked foot, gathering the things in a heap and looked about for more.Unemotional Archie perfunctorily contributed to the pile an old cloth cap with the peak torn off. Old Singleton, lost in the serene regions of fiction, read on unheeding.Charley, pitiless with the wisdom of youth, squeaked:—“If you want brass buttons for your new unyforms I’ve got two for you.” The filthy object of universal charity shook his fist at the youngster.—“I’ll make you keep this ‘ere fo’c’sle clean, young feller,” he snarled viciously.“Never you fear.I will learn you to be civil to an able seaman, you ignerant ass.”He glared harmfully, but saw Singleton shut his book, and his little beady eyes began to roam from berth to berth.—“Take that bunk by the door there—it’s pretty fair,” suggested Belfast.So advised, he gathered the gifts at his feet, pressed them in a bundle against his breast, then looked cautiously at the Russian Finn, who stood on one side with an unconscious gaze, contemplating, perhaps, one of those weird visions that haunt the men of his race.—“Get out of my road, Dutchy,” said the victim of Yankee brutality.The Finn did not move—did not hear.“Get out, blast ye,” shouted the other, shoving him aside with his elbow.“Get out, you blanked deaf and dumb fool.Get out.” The man staggered, recovered himself, and gazed at the speaker in silence.—“Those damned furriners should be kept under,” opined the amiable Donkin to the forecastle.“If you don’t teach ‘em their place they put on you like anythink.” He flung all his worldly possessions into the empty bed-place, gauged with another shrewd look the risks of the proceeding, then leaped up to the Finn, who stood pensive and dull.—“I’ll teach you to swell around,” he yelled.“I’ll plug your eyes for you, you blooming square-head.” Most of the men were now in their bunks and the two had the forecastle clear to themselves.The development of the destitute Donkin aroused interest.He danced all in tatters before the amazed Finn, squaring from a distance at the heavy, unmoved face.One or two men cried encouragingly: “Go it, Whitechapel!” settling themselves luxuriously in their beds to survey the fight.Others shouted: “Shut yer row!...Go an’ put yer ’ed in a bag!...” The hubbub was recommencing.Suddenly many heavy blows struck with a handspike on the deck above boomed like discharges of small cannon through the forecastle.Then the boatswain’s voice rose outside the door with an authoritative note in its drawl:—“D’ye hear, below there?Lay aft!Lay aft to muster all hands!”
这时，出现了一阵令人吃惊的安静。接着，这些人从他们的铺位上一跃而起，扑通扑通跳了下去，水手舱甲板上随处都是他们的光脚。帽子从凌乱的毛毯里扒了出来。有些人打着呵欠，扣着腰带上的扣子。抽了一半的烟斗，匆忙地在木器上磕一磕，然后塞到枕头下。人们大声抱怨：“什么事？难道就不能让我们歇上一会儿？”唐金叫嚷道：“要是这条船有这种规矩，我们非把它改过来不可……你们不用管我……我会很快……”大家谁也没有理会他。他们三三两两，踉踉跄跄走出舱门，就像商船水手一般，连一道门槛也过不稳，真是一群旱鸭子。倡导改变规矩的人紧随其后。辛格尔顿费劲地穿上他的外衣，走在最后，他个子高高的，颇具长者之风，老运动员的身躯上高高地顶着一颗饱经风霜的圣人脑袋。只有查利还呆在那明亮空旷的地方，坐在两排铁链中间，那铁链一直延伸到前面狭窄的阴暗处。他急促地使劲拉绳索，好把结子打完。突然他站了起来，把绳子投向猫，追着黑猫向前猛跑。小猫从容地摆脱了追赶，跳上制链机，把尾巴直直地竖起来，仿佛一根小旗杆。 There was a moment of surprised stillness.Then the forecastle floor disappeared under men whose bare feet flopped on the planks as they sprang clear out of their berths.Caps were rooted for amongst tumbled blankets.Some, yawning, buttoned waistbands.Half-smoked pipes were knocked hurriedly against woodwork and stuffed under pillows.Voices growled:—“What’s up?...Is there no rest for us?”—Donkin yelped:“If that’s the way of this ship, we’ll ’ave to change all that....You leave me alone....I will soon....”None of the crowd noticed him.They were lurching in twos and threes through the doors, after the manner of merchant Jacks who cannot go out of a door fairly, like mere landsmen.The votary of change followed them.Singleton, struggling into his jacket, came last, tall and fatherly, bearing high his head of a weather-beaten sage on the body of an old athlete.Only Charley remained alone in the white glare of the empty place, sitting between the two rows of iron links that stretched into the narrow gloom forward.He pulled hard at the strands in a hurried endeavour to finish his knot.Suddenly he started up, flung the rope at the cat, and skipped after the black tom which went off leaping sedately over chain compressors, with its tail carried stiff and upright, like a small flag pole.
走出明亮温暖的水手舱，海员们便被宁静清澈的夜色所笼罩，和煦的微风扑面吹来；桅杆顶端，透过一层发亮的薄雾，天空挂满了点点繁星。对着城镇的一侧，黑色的海水与明亮的光线融合在一起，随着海水的轻轻荡漾，光线也微微起伏，宛如长在海边的海藻。远处还有其他一排排的灯，呈直线排列，好像是在高楼间进行阅兵表演；而港口的另一侧，影影绰绰的小山高高地弓起它们的脊背，山上布满了点点星光，犹如空中落下的火花。远处，毕古拉大道附近，船坞闸门上的电灯高高地挂在灯杆顶端，发出昏黄清冷的光，仿佛是邪恶的卫星囚禁起来的幽灵。黑暗笼罩的停泊处，下了锚的船只在锚灯微光的照耀下静寂地漂浮着，时隐时现，模糊不清，庞大无比，仿佛被人们遗弃于此，纪念长眠之人的奇特建筑。 Outside the glare of the steaming forecastle the serene purity of the night enveloped the seamen with its soothing breath, with its tepid breath flowing under the stars that hung countless above the mastheads in a thin cloud of luminous dust.On the town side the blackness of the water was streaked with trails of light which undulated gently on slight ripples, similar to filaments that float rooted to the shore.Rows of other lights stood away in straight lines as if drawn up on parade between towering buildings; but on the other side of the harbour sombre hills arched high their black spines, on which, here and there, the point of a star resembled a spark fallen from the sky. Far off, Byculla way, the electric lamps at the dock gates shone on the end of lofty standards with a glow blinding and frigid like captive ghosts of some evil moons.Scattered all over the dark polish of the roadstead, the ships at anchor floated in perfect stillness under the feeble gleam of their riding-lights, looming up, opaque and bulky, like strange and monumental structures abandoned by men to an everlasting repose.
在舱门前，贝克先生正在点名。 他们磕磕绊绊摇摇晃晃走过主桅时，看见船尾处贝克那张宽宽的圆脸，面前展着一张白纸，肩旁站着一位昏昏欲睡的船员，眼皮耷拉着，举起的一只胳膊悬在空中，擎着一盏明亮的球形灯。光光的脚底走过甲板的拖拉声还没消失，大副便开始点名了。他用严肃的语气清清楚楚地点着每个船员的名字，这样的点名适合于不安的寂寞，卑微迷茫的奋斗，以及对些许困顿和繁琐义务的无奈忍受。大副每叫到一个名字，被叫的人就要答道：“是，先生！”或者“在！”接着，人群中便有一个人头凸显在黑暗的右弦壁上方，光着脚走到亮处，然后，静悄悄地大跨两步，走进后甲板左船舷的阴影里。他们应答的声音各不相同：有的浑厚模糊，有的响亮清晰；还有人认为点名本身就是对他们情感的伤害，所以用受伤的语调回答。因为商船上的纪律不是很讲究，等级观念淡漠，面对冷漠浩瀚的大海和苛刻的工作要求，大家认为彼此是平等的。贝克先生不紧不慢地往下点：“汉森——坎贝尔——史密斯——沃米博。嗨，喂，沃米博。你干嘛不答应？老是得叫你两次。”芬兰人终于发出了粗鲁的咕哝声，跨过光亮处，身上穿着奇装异服，脸上带着梦游者的表情。大副加快了点名速度：“克雷克——辛格尔顿——唐金……哦，上帝呀！”他突然禁不住惊叫起来，这时，那个衣衫褴褛的家伙出现在灯光下。那人站着不动；狰狞地一笑，露出灰白的牙龈和长长的上齿。“我有什么问题吗，大副先生？”他问道，故作简洁的语气充满了傲慢。甲板两侧传来压低的嗤笑声。“没什么。过去吧。”贝克先生吼道，蓝色的眼睛却一直注视着这位新水手。唐金突然消失在光亮中，钻进了点过名的黑乎乎的人群里，有人轻拍他的背，他听到别人的低语奉承：　“他才不害怕呢，他今后还敢嘲弄他们哩，走着瞧，看他敢不敢……会经常上演滑稽木偶戏的……你没看见大副见到他时那副吃惊样儿？……唉！ 真要命，要是我曾经……”最后一个人走了过来，人群出现了一阵沉默，大副凝视看名单。“十六，十七。”他低声自语道。“我看还差一位，水手长。”他大声说道。他胳膊肘旁站着的这位西部大个老乡，皮肤黝黑，留着胡须，宛如西班牙巨人，只听他瓮声瓮气地说道：“前面一个人也没留，先生。我各处都查看过了。他人不在船上，但也许天亮前回来。”“哦。他也许会回来，也许不会，”大副答道，“怎么也认不出他的姓。整个一团黑……就这样吧，伙计们。下去吧。” Before the cabin door Mr. Baker was mustering the crew.As they stumbled and lurched along past the mainmast, they could see aft his round, broad face with a white paper before it, and beside his shoulder the sleepy head, writh dropped eyelids, of the boy, who held, suspended at the end of his raised arm, the luminous globe of a lamp.Even before the shuffle of naked soles had ceased along the decks, the mate began to call over the names.He called distinctly in a serious tone befitting this roll-call to unquiet loneliness, to inglorious and obscure struggle, or to the more trying endurance of small privations and wearisome duties.As the chief mate read out a name, one of the men would answer: “Yes, sir!” or “Here!” and, detaching himself from the shadowy mob of heads visible above the blackness of starboard bulwarks, would step bare-footed into the circle of light, and in two noiseless strides pass into the shadows on the port side of the quarterdeck.They answered in divers tones: in thick mutters, in clear, ringing voices; and some, as if the whole thing had been an outrage on their feelings, used an injured intonation: for discipline is not ceremonious in merchant ships, where the sense of hierarchy is weak, and where all feel themselves equal before the unconcerned immensity of the sea and the exacting appeal of the work.Mr. Baker read on steadily:—“Hansen—Campbell—Smith—Wamibo.Now, then, Wamibo.Why don’t you answer?Always got to call your name twice.”The Finn emitted at last an uncouth grunt, and, stepping out, passed through the patch of light, weird and gaudy, with the face of a man marching through a dream.The mate went on faster:—“Craik—Singleton—Donkin....O Lord!” he involuntarily ejaculated as the incredibly dilapidated figure appeared in the light.It stopped; it uncovered pale gums and long, upper teeth in a malevolent grin.—“Is there any-think wrong with me, Mister Mate?” it asked, with a flavour of insolence in the forced simplicity of its tone.On both sides of the deck subdued titters were heard.—“That’ll do.Go over,” growled Mr. Baker, fixing the new hand with steady blue eyes.And Donkin vanished suddenly out of the light into the dark group of mustered men, to be slapped on the back and to hear flattering whispers:—“He ain’t afeard, he’ll give sport to ‘em, see if he don’t.... Reg’lar Punch and Judy show....Did ye see the mate start at him?...Well! Damme, if I ever!...” The last man had gone over, and there was a moment of silence while the mate peered at his list.—“Sixteen, seventeen,” he muttered.“I am one hand short, bo’sen,” he said aloud.The big west-countryman at his elbow, swarthy and bearded like a gigantic Spaniard, said in a rumbling bass:—“There’s no one left forward, sir.I had a look round.He ain’t aboard, but he may, turn up before daylight.”—“Ay.He may or he may not,” commented the mate, “can’t make out that last name.It’s all a smudge....That will do, men.Go below.”
清晰而静止的人群开始躁动，分散开来，向前走去。 The distinct and motionless group stirred, broke up, began to move forward.
“等等！”一个深沉响亮的声音叫道。 “Wait!” cried a deep, ringing voice.
大家站住了。打着呵欠已经开始往回走的贝克先生，也张着嘴回过头来。终于忍不住发起了脾气，他脱口而出：“怎么回事？”谁叫的‘等等’？什么……” All stood still.Mr. Baker, who had turned away yawning, spun round open-mouthed.At last, furious, he blurted out:—“What’s this?Who said ‘Wait’?What....”
这时，他注意到舷栏上站着一个高个子。那人走下来，穿过人群，迈着沉重的大步，径直来到船尾光亮处。那响亮的声音再次坚决地说道：“等等！” 灯光照亮了来人的全身。他个子很高。甲板上方，滑动垫木上的救生艇的阴影遮住了他的头。他的眼白和牙齿熠熠闪光，可面部却不分明。他两手硕大，似乎还戴着手套。 But he saw a tall figure standing on the rail.It came down and pushed through the crowd, marching with a heavy tread towards the light on the quarterdeck.Then again the sonorous voice said with insistence:—“Wait!”The lamplight lit up the man’s body.He was tall.His head was away up in the shadows of lifeboats that stood on skids above the deck.The whites of his eyes and his teeth gleamed distinctly, but the face was indistinguishable.His hands were big and seemed gloved.
贝克先生勇敢地迎了上去。“你是谁？你怎么敢……”他问道。 Mr. Baker advanced intrepidly.“Who are you?How dare you...” he began.
提灯的年轻人像其他人一样吃惊，举起灯去照那人的脸。是个黑人。人群中发出了惊讶的嗡嗡声——声音微弱，听起来像是压低成了一个词“黑鬼”——沿着甲板消失在夜色中。黑人似乎没有听见。他站立不稳，来回踏了几步，身体才保持住平衡。过了一会儿，他镇定地说道：“我叫韦特——詹姆斯·韦特。” The boy, amazed like the rest, raised the light to the man’s face.It was black.A surprised hum—a faint hum that sounded like the suppressed mutter of the word “Nigger”—ran along the deck and escaped out into the night.The nigger seemed not to hear.He balanced himself where he stood in a swagger that marked time.After a moment he said calmly:—“My name is Wait—James Wait.”
“哦！”贝克先生应和道。随后，默默克制了几秒钟，他的脾气还是发作了。“啊！你叫韦特。那又怎么着？你想干什么？你来这儿大声嚷嚷，什么意思？” “Oh!” said Mr. Baker.Then, after a few seconds of smouldering silence, his temper blazed out.“Ah! Your name is Wait.What of that?What do you want?What do you mean, coming shouting here?”
那黑人沉着，冷静，身材高大，气度非凡。人们走上前来，全都站在他身后。他比里面最高的还要高出半头。他说：“我是这条船上的船员。”他发音清楚，语气柔和，表达准确。他那深沉响亮的语调毫不费劲地传遍了甲板。此人天生高傲，但又真诚谦虚，好像六英尺三的身高使他能够洞察人类极大的愚蠢，但下定决心对此不去苛求。他继续解释道：“船长今早雇了我。我没能早点上船。我登上舷梯时看到大家都在船尾，并且一眼就看到您在点名。我禁不住报出了自己的名字。我以为您的名单上会有我的名字，您会明白的。结果，您误会了。”　他突然止住不讲了。他周围的傻瓜们显得十分尴尬。他说的一点没错，并且乐意原谅别人。他那倨傲的话语停止了，静静地站着，喘着粗气，周围站满了白人。他仰起头，望着闪耀的灯——头上清晰地凸现出灯光与阴影的轮廓——一个充满活力而又有些变形的头，长着一张饱受折磨、无精打采的脸——一张凄惨凶狠的脸：仿佛一副悲伤、神秘、令人讨厌的面具，罩住了黑人的灵魂。 The nigger was calm, cool, towering, superb.The men had approached and stood behind him in a body.He overtopped the tallest by half a head.He said: “I belong to the ship.”He enunciated distinctly, with soft precision.The deep, rolling tones of his voice filled the deck without effort.He was naturally scornful, unaffectedly condescending, as if from his height of six foot three he had surveyed all the vastness of human folly and had made up his mind not to be too hard on it.He went on:—“The captain shipped me this morning.I couldn’t get aboard sooner.I saw you all aft as I came up the ladder, and could see directly you were mustering the crew.Naturally I called out my name.I thought you had it on your list, and would understand.You misapprehended.”He stopped short.The folly around him was confounded.He was right as ever, and as ever ready to forgive.The disdainful tones had ceased, and, breathing heavily, he stood still, surrounded by all these white men.He held his head up in the glare of the lamp—a head vigorously modelled into deep shadows and shining lights—a head powerful and misshapen with a tormented and flattened face—a face pathetic and brutal: the tragic, the mysterious, the repulsive mask of a nigger’s soul.
贝克先生恢复了平静，凑近纸仔细看。“哦，不错；是这样。好了，韦特。把你的行李提到前面去。”他说。 Mr. Baker, recovering his composure, looked at the paper close.“Oh, yes; that’s so. All right, Wait.Take your gear forward,” he said.
突然，黑人的眼睛剧烈翻动，眼睛全部变白。他用一只手撑着腰，连咳两声，咳嗽声十分巨大，像金属撞击,似空谷传响；又如两声爆炸响彻在苍穹之下；整个苍穹发出了回声，铁质的船舷似乎也在共振，然后，他与其他船员一起大步向前走去。漫步在船舱门前的高级职员听到他说：“你们当中谁帮我提一下行李好吗？我有一个箱子和一个包。”他说话的声音洪亮，语调平稳，每句话全船人都能听到，他彬彬有理的请求，谁都不会拒绝。　人们扛着重物快速行走的声音霎时传到了前面，而黑大个儿却在主升降口处与几个矮个儿船员一起悠闲地走着。这时，又有人听到他问：“你们的厨师是位黑人先生吗？”得知厨师不巧也是白人时，他非常失望与不满，嘴里发出“啊！哼！”之声，算是对这一信息的回应。然而，当大家一同向水手舱走去时，他屈尊将头伸进厨房的门，用低沉的声音大声叫道：“晚上好，大师傅！”这声音震得锅盆乱响。昏暗的灯光下，厨子坐在煤箱上打瞌睡，面前放着船长的晚饭。厨子好像被鞭子抽了一下，跳了起来，狂奔到甲板上，看到几个人的背影笑着向前走去。后来，一提起这次航行，他都会说：“那可怜的家伙把我吓坏了。我还以为撞见鬼了。”　这位厨师跟本船船长航海已达七年之久。他是一个认真严肃的人，家里有妻子和三个孩子，平均每十二个月与家人团聚一次。在陆地上时，每个礼拜日他都要带家人去两次教堂。在海上，每天晚上睡觉时他都把灯开得通亮，嘴里噙着烟斗，手里拿着打开的《圣经》。经常有人夜里不得不替他把灯熄灭，取下他手中的书和齿间的烟斗。“因为，”贝尔法斯特常常生气地抱怨道，“你这个傻厨子，总有一天夜里会吞下你那老掉牙的土烟斗，那时，我们可就没有厨子啦。”“啊！小子，我随时听从上帝的召唤……希望你们都能这样。”对方和蔼平静地回答道，听起来有些迂腐，可确实感人。贝尔法斯特站在厨房门外，气得直跺脚。“你这个圣人笨蛋！我才不想让你死呢。”他怒吼着，扬起头，面带愠色，脸上肌肉颤动，但目光依然柔和。“发什么急呀？你个呆头呆脑早该死的异教徒，魔鬼马上会找你的。为我们想想吧……为我们……为我们！” 他跺着脚，向旁边吐了一口唾沫，忿忿不平地走开了；而另一位踱了出来，手里掂着锅，热烘烘的，满身油污，可镇静自如，面带居高临下、充满自信的微笑，望着那位气哼哼的“怪异小个子”的背影。他们俩是知心朋友。 Suddenly the nigger’s eyes rolled wildly, became all whites.He put his hand to his side and coughed twice, a cough metallic, hollow, and tremendously loud; it resounded like two explosions in a vault; the dome of the sky rang to it, and the iron plates of the ship’s bulwarks seemed to vibrate in unison, then he marched off forward with the others.The officers lingering by the cabin door could hear him say: “Won’t some of you chaps lend a hand with my dunnage?I’ve got a chest and a bag.” The words, spoken sonorously, with an even intonation, were heard all over the ship, and the question was put in a manner that made refusal impossible.The short, quick shuffle of men carrying something heavy went away forward, but the tall figure of the nigger lingered by the main hatch in a knot of smaller shapes.Again he was heard asking: “Is your cook a coloured gentleman?” Then a disappointed and disapproving “Ah! h’m!” was his comment upon the information that the cook happened to be a mere white man.Yet, as they went all together towards the forecastle, he condescended to put his head through the galley door and boom out inside a magnificent “Good evening, doctor!” that made all the saucepans ring.In the dim light the cook dozed on the coal locker in front of the captain’s supper.He jumped up as if he had been cut with a whip, and dashed wildly on deck to see the backs of several men going away laughing.Afterwards, when talking about that voyage, he used to say:—“The poor fellow had scared me. I thought I had seen the devil.”The cook had been seven years in the ship with the same captain.He was a serious-minded man with a wife and three children, whose society he enjoyed on an average one month out of twelve.When on shore he took his family to church twice every Sunday.At sea he went to sleep every evening with his lamp turned up full, a pipe in his mouth, and an open Bible in his hand.Some one had always to go during the night to put out the light, take the book from his hand, and the pipe from between his teeth.“For”—Belfast used to say, irritated and complaining—“some night, you stupid cookie, you’ll swallow your ould clay, and we will have no cook.”—“Ah! sonny, I am ready for my Maker’s call...wish you all were,” the other would answer with a benign serenity that was altogether imbecile and touching.Belfast outside the galley door danced with vexation.“You holy fool!I don’t want you to die,” he howled, looking up with furious, quivering face and tender eyes.“What’s the hurry?You blessed wooden-headed ould heretic, the divvle will have you soon enough.Think of Us...of Us...of Us!” And he would go away, stamping, spitting aside, disgusted and worried; while the other, stepping out, saucepan in hand, hot, begrimed and placid, watched with a superior, cock-sure smile the back of his “queer little man” reeling in a rage.They were great friends.
在二副的陪同下，贝克先生懒洋洋地靠在后甲板升降口，呼吸着夜里潮湿的空气。“那些来自西印度的黑人体格壮、块头大——其中一些……咳！……可不是吗？克赖顿先生，刚才那黑人就又壮实又高大。让他拉拉绳索试试看。嗨？咳！我想把他派到我的班上。”　二副是个英俊、文雅的年轻人，长着刚毅的面庞和健壮的身材，此刻正静静地谈着自己的想法。从他的语调里可以感到一丝尖酸，贝克先生非常亲切地与他讨论着。“得了，得了，年轻人，”他说，话语间嘟嘟囔囔,“得了！别太贪啦。在整个航行中，你已经得到大个子芬恩来做看守了。我做事最讲公道。你可以要那两个年轻的斯堪的纳维亚人，而我……咳！……我要那个黑人，还有那……咳！那个穿黑外套的厚脸皮小贩似的家伙。我要让他……咳！……让他规规矩矩，不然的话，我就……咳！……我就不叫贝克啦。咳！咳！咳！” Mr. Baker, lounging over the after-hatch, sniffed the humid night in the company of the second mate.—“Those West India niggers run fine and large—some of them...Ough!...Don’t they?A fine, big man that, Mr. Creighton.Feel him on a rope.Hey?Ough!I will take him into my watch, I think.” The second mate, a fair, gentlemanly young fellow, with a resolute face and a splendid physique, observed quietly that it was just about what he expected.There could be felt in his tone some slight bitterness which Mr. Baker very kindly set himself to argue away.“Come, come, young man,” he said, grunting between the words.“Come!Don’t be too greedy.You had that big Finn in your watch all the voyage.I will do what’s fair.You may have those two young Scandinavians and I...Ough!...I get the nigger, and will take that....Ough! that cheeky costermonger chap in a black frock-coat.I’ll make him....Ough!...make him toe the mark, or my....Ough!....name isn’t Baker.Ough! Ough!Ough!”
他连哼三声——非常吓人。他学会了一种把戏，一句话说到半截和结尾都要哼上一声。这哼哼声既微妙又管用，与他那凶狠的话语、脖子像牛似的肥大身驱、晃荡摇摆的步态、皱巴巴的大脸、专注的目光以及略含讽刺的嘴巴无不匹配。但他这哼哼的威力早已在船员中大打折扣。大家都喜欢他；贝尔法斯特——此人深受大家喜爱，他也清楚这一点——当着面就敢模仿贝克的样子。查利——可就谨慎得多——模仿他那摇摆的步态。他的一些话语也成了经典，在水手舱里天天被引用。他受欢迎的程度简直无以复加。此外，所有人都会乐意承认，在适当的场合，他会“按传统的大西洋方式严厉训斥船员”。 He grunted thrice—ferociously.He had that trick of grunting so between his words and at the end of sentences.It was a fine, effective grunt that went well with his menacing utterance, with his heavy, bull-necked frame, his jerky, rolling gait; with his big, seamed face, his steady eyes, and sardonic mouth.But its effect had been long ago discounted by the men.They liked him; Belfast—who was a favourite, and knew it—mimicked him, not quite behind his back.Charley—but with greater caution—imitated his rolling gait.Some of his sayings became established, daily quotations in the forecastle.Popularity can go no farther!Besides, all hands were ready to admit that on a fitting occasion the mate could “jump down a fellow’s throat in a reg’lar Western Ocean style.”
现在他要发布最后的命令啦。“咳！你，诺尔斯！四点钟，叫醒所有人。我要…….咳！……在拖船到来之前把锚链收短。小心候着船长回来。我要和衣睡一会儿……咳！……看见拖船一到就叫醒我。咳！咳！老头子一上船肯定有事要吩咐，” 他叮嘱克赖顿，“好了，晚安……咳！明天可长着呢……咳!……现在还是睡觉为好。咳！咳！” Now he was giving his last orders.“Ough! You, Knowles!Call all hands at four.I want...Ough!...to heave short before the tug comes.Look out for the captain.I am going to lie down in my clothes....Ough!...Call me when you see the boat coming.Ough!Ough! The old man is sure to have something to say when he gets aboard,” he remarked to Creighton.“Well, good-night....Ough! A long day before us to-morrow....Ough!...Better turn in now.Ough! Ough!”
黑暗的甲板上闪过一道光，然后是砰的关门声，贝克先生进了他那整洁的船舱。年轻的克赖顿背靠弦栏站着，望着朦胧的东方夜空。夜空中，他仿佛看到了一条长长的乡间小路，路旁树叶婆娑，阳光闪耀。他看到古树的枝桠摇曳着在空中舒展开来，形成圆拱，缝隙间透出英格兰天空柔和亲切的碧蓝。一位衣着轻盈的姑娘打着阳伞微笑着，似乎从温柔的天际走出，跨过圆拱。 Upon the dark deck a band of light flashed, then a door slammed, and Mr. Baker was gone into his neat cabin.Young Creighton stood leaning over the rail, and looked dreamily into the night of the East.And he saw in it a long country lane, a lane of waving leaves and dancing sunshine.He saw stirring boughs of old trees outspread, and framing in their arch the tender, the caressing blueness of an English sky. And through the arch a girl in a light dress, smiling under a sunshade, seemed to be stepping out of the tender sky.
在轮船另一端，水手舱里只亮着一盏灯，整个船舱即将在朦胧虚幻中睡去，不时传来震耳的鼾声和急促的叹息声。双层床张着黑洞洞的大嘴，好像是游尸居住的坟墓。到处挂着俗丽的印花棉布帷幔，拉下了一半，表明这是奢靡之人的居所。一条惨白而毫无生气的腿悬在床沿上。一条胳膊直直地伸着，乌黑的手掌向外，粗大的手指半曲着。两人轻微的鼾声，此起彼伏，似乎在滑稽地争论。辛格尔顿又脱光了衣服——这位老人深受痱子之苦——站在门口，让风吹拂脊背，交叉的两臂放在裸露的刺有纹身的胸膛上。他的头触到了上面甲板的横梁。黑人半裸着身子，正忙着解开捆箱子的绳子，把被褥放到一个上铺上。他穿着袜子走来走去，高高的个子却悄无声息，一双背带拍打着小腿。在支柱和斜桅的阴影里，唐金在啃一块船上的硬面包，他坐在甲板上，两脚上翘，眼睛乱转；他紧抓面包，送到嘴边，面带怒色，使劲猛咬。面包屑洒落在他叉开的双腿间。然后他站了起来。 At the other end of the ship the forecastle, with only one lamp burning now, was going to sleep in a dim emptiness traversed by loud breathings, by sudden short sighs.The double row of berths yawned black, like graves tenanted by uneasy corpses.Here and there a curtain of gaudy chintz, half drawn, marked the resting-place of a sybarite.A leg hung over the edge very white and lifeless.An arm stuck straight out with a dark palm turned up, and thick fingers half closed.Two light snores, that did not synchronise, quarrelled in funny dialogue.Singleton stripped again—the old man suffered much from prickly heat—stood cooling his back in the doorway, with his arms crossed on his bare and adorned chest.His head touched the beam of the deck above.The nigger, half undressed, was busy casting adrift the lashing of his box, and spreading his bedding in an upper berth.He moved about in his socks, tall and noiseless, with a pair of braces beating about his calves.Amongst the shadows of stanchions and bowsprit, Donkin munched a piece of hard ship’s bread, sitting on the deck with upturned feet and restless eyes; he held the biscuit up before his mouth in the whole fist and snapped his jaws at it with a raging face.Crumbs fell between his outspread legs.Then he got up.
“水桶在哪儿？”他压低嗓门问道。 “Where’s our water-cask?” he asked in a contained voice.
辛格尔顿什么也没说，用拿着燃着的短烟斗的大手指了指。唐金俯身就着水桶喝水，水溅了起来，他转过身发现黑人正回头用镇静高傲的眼神打量他。他侧着身走上前。 Singleton, without a word, pointed with a big hand that held a short smouldering pipe.Donkin bent over the cask, drank out of the tin, splashing the water, turned round and noticed the nigger looking at him over the shoulder with calm loftiness.He moved up sideways.
“这就是给人吃的晚饭呀，”他忿忿地咕哝道，“我家里的狗都不愿吃。难道你我只配吃这些？这还是大船水手舱哩！……盘子里连一点肉末都没有。我看了所有的橱柜……” “There’s a blooming supper for a man,” he whispered bitterly.“My dorg at ‘ome wouldn’t ’ave it. It’s fit enouf for you an‘ me. ’Ere’s a big ship’s fo’c’sle!...Not a blooming scrap of meat in the kids.I’ve looked in all the lockers....”
黑人瞪大了眼睛，好像有人突然用外语跟自己打招呼似的。唐金换了语气：“给咱点儿烟草吧，伙计，”他低声说道，显得十分亲密，“打从上月，我就没有烟可抽可嚼啦。我要急疯了。快点，老伙计！” The nigger stared like a man addressed unexpectedly in a foreign language.Donkin changed his tone:—“Giv‘ us a bit of ’baccy, mate,” he breathed out confidentially, “I ‘aven’t ’ad smoke or chew for the last month.I am rampin’ mad for it. Come on, old man!”
“少套近乎。”黑人说。唐金吓了一跳，在旁边一个箱子上坐下，十分惊讶。“我们不曾在一起呆过。”詹姆斯·韦特用深沉的声音低声说道，“这是你要的烟草。”然后，沉默了一会儿，他问道：“什么船？”“黄金之州号。”唐金模模糊糊地低声答道，嘴里不停地嚼着烟草。黑人低声吹着口哨。“逃的？”他简洁地问道。唐金点点头：一侧的腮帮子高高鼓起。“自然我要逃，”他咕哝道，“他们在这个部位的过道上踢死了一个外国佬，然后要对我下手。我就只好溜掉。”“把行李都落在了船上？”“可不是么，行李和钱，”唐金答道，微微提高了嗓门，“我什么都没了。没有衣服，没有铺盖。这儿一位罗圈腿的爱尔兰小伙子送给了我一条毛毯。我想今晚我只好裹着前中桅的支索帆睡觉啦。” “Don’t be familiar,” said the nigger.Donkin started and sat down on a chest near by, out of sheer surprise.“We haven’t kept pigs together,” continued James Wait in a deep undertone.“Here’s your tobacco.”Then, after a pause, he inquired:—“What ship?”—“—Golden State—,” muttered Donkin indistinctly, biting the tobacco.The nigger whistled low.—“Ran?” he said curtly.Donkin nodded: one of his cheeks bulged out.“In course I ran,” he mumbled.“They booted the life hout of one Dago chap on the passage ‘ere, then started on me.I cleared hout ’ere.—” “Left your dunnage behind?”—“Yes, dunnage and money,” answered Donkin, raising his voice a little; “I got nothink.No clothes, no bed.A bandy-legged little Hirish chap ‘ere ’as give me a blanket.Think I’ll go an’ sleep in the fore topmast staysail to-night.”
他在甲板上走着，背上披着毛毯的一角。辛格尔顿瞥都没瞥，把身子移到一边，让他过去。黑人脱下岸上穿的衣服，换上干干净净的工作服，坐在箱子上，一只胳膊伸在双膝上。打量了辛格尔顿一阵后，他平淡地问道：“这是哪种船？很不错吧？啊？” He went on deck trailing behind his back a corner of the blanket.Singleton, without a glance, moved slightly aside to let him pass.The nigger put away his shore togs and sat in clean working clothes on his box, one arm stretched over his knees.After staring at Singleton for some time he asked without emphasis:—“What kind of ship is this?Pretty fair?Eh?”
辛格尔顿毫无反应。过了好久，板着脸说道：“船！……船都不错。关键是船上的人！” Singleton didn’t stir.A long while after he said, with unmoved face:—“Ship!...Ships are all right.It is the men in them!”
他在无比的静寂中不停地吸着烟。半个世纪一来，他一直在聆听雷鸣般的涛声，不经意间，他那沧桑的双唇流露出无限的智慧。小猫在起锚机上打着呼噜。詹姆斯·韦特大声咳嗽起来，好像一阵飓风吹来，前后摇摆站立不稳，猛然倒在箱子上瞪着眼喘气。好几个人被吵醒了。一位躺在铺上无精打采地抱怨道：“咋回事！这么吵！”“我胸部受了点凉。”韦特喘着气说。“你管这叫受凉！”那人气愤地说，“恐怕比这严重吧……”“噢！你是这么想的。”黑人坦率地说，语气中再次充满了鄙夷与讽刺。他爬上床铺，又开始不停地咳嗽，同时环顾着舱里的一切。没有谁再发出抗议。他躺在枕头上，咳嗽声再次响起，像一个人在睡梦中受到压迫一般。 He went on smoking in the profound silence.The wisdom of half a century spent in listening to the thunder of the waves had spoken unconsciously through his old lips.The cat purred on the windlass.Then James Wait had a fit of roaring, rattling cough, that shook him, tossed him like a hurricane, and flung him panting with staring eyes headlong on his sea-chest.Several men woke up. One said sleepily out of his bunk: “’Struth! what a blamed row!”—“I have a cold on my chest,” gasped Wait.—“Cold!you call it,” grumbled the man; “should think ’twas something more....”—“Oh! you think so,” said the nigger upright and loftily scornful again.He climbed into his berth and began coughing persistently while he put his head out to glare all round the forecastle.There was no further protest.He fell back on the pillow, and could be heard there wheezing regularly like a man oppressed in his sleep.
辛格尔顿站在门口，面向灯光，背朝黑暗。水手们都进入了梦乡，他独自一人站在这昏暗空旷的舱里显得更加魁梧，高大，苍老；苍老得如同时间老人，可惜，时间老人未能降临到船上，来到这静如坟墓的舱里耐心检阅睡眠——抚慰者——的短暂胜利。然而，他只是时间的孩子，被吞噬被遗忘的一代的寂寞残存者。他站在那里，身体硬朗如故，似乎一直无忧无虑；好一个任劳任怨的人，拥有丰富、虚幻的过去，而没有什么未来，有过孩子般的冲动和成年人的热情，但这些却早已在他那刺有纹身的胸膛中泯灭。能够理解他沉默的人都已经离开了人世——这些人知道如何生存在生活的藩篱之外和死亡的威逼之中。他们曾经身体强壮，就像现在那些没有忧虑没有希望的人一样强壮。他们急躁而坚韧，狂暴而专注，任性而忠诚。好心人过去非把这些人说成是每吃一口饭就抱怨一番；干起话来总担心自己的性命。但事实上，这些人知道什么是艰辛、贫困、残暴、堕落——但不知什么是恐惧，内心中没有怨恨。这些人虽然难以驾驭，但却容易激励；虽然默默无闻，但从心底里蔑视那些意志脆弱，哀叹命运多舛的人。这些人的命运不同一般，非其莫属；在他们看来，能够承担这种命运是精选出来的少数人的特权！他们一辈子少言寡语，但工作少不了他们，他们不懂得爱情的甜蜜或家庭的安逸——面对死亡也不会因坟墓的狭窄黑暗而恐惧。他们是神秘大海永远的孩子。他们的接班人便是不满陆地生活的大孩子。这些后继者不怎么顽皮，但也不够天真；不怎么世俗，但也缺乏信仰；他们要是学会了讲话，同时也学会了抱怨。但是他们的前辈刚强少语；他们不被关注，俯身劳作，坚韧不拔，就像女像石柱，夜晚托起了金碧辉煌光彩夺目的圣殿。他们现在要走了——但这无关紧要。大海和大地对于自己的孩子无信无义：一个真理，一个信仰，一代人走了——被遗忘了，但这无关紧要！也许只有个别相信真理，承认信仰——或者爱惜他人的人，情况有所不同。 Singleton stood at the door with his face to the light and his back to the darkness.And alone in the dim emptiness of the sleeping forecastle he appeared bigger, colossal, very old; old as Father Time himself, who should have come there into this place as quiet as a sepulchre to contemplate with patient eyes the short victory of sleep, the consoler.Yet he was only a child of time, a lonely relic of a devoured and forgotten generation.He stood, still strong, as ever unthinking; a ready man with a vast empty past and with no future, with his childlike impulses and his man’s passions already dead within his tattooed breast.The men who could understand his silence were gone—those men who knew how to exist beyond the pale of life and within sight of eternity.They had been strong, as those are strong who know neither doubts nor hopes.They had been impatient and enduring, turbulent and devoted, unruly and faithful.Well-meaning people had tried to represent those men as whining over every mouthful of their food; as going about their work in fear of their lives.But in truth they had been men who knew toil, privation, violence, debauchery—but knew not fear, and had no desire of spite in their hearts.Men hard to manage, but easy to inspire; voiceless men—but men enough to scorn in their hearts the sentimental voices that bewailed the hardness of their fate.It was a fate unique and their own; the capacity to bear it appeared to them the privilege of the chosen!Their generation lived inarticulate and, indispensable, without knowing the sweetness of affections or the refuge of a home—and died free from the dark menace of a narrow grave.They were the everlasting children of the mysterious sea.Their successors are the grown-up children of a discontented earth.They are less naughty, but less innocent; less profane, but perhaps also less believing; and if they have learned how to speak they have also learned how to whine.But the others were strong and mute; they were effaced, bowed and enduring, like stone caryatides that hold up in the night the lighted halls of a resplendent and glorious edifice.They are gone now—and it does not matter.The sea and the earth are unfaithful to their children: a truth, a faith, a generation of men goes—and is forgotten, and it does not matter!Except, perhaps, to the few of those who believed the truth, confessed the faith—or loved the men.
一阵微风吹来。一阵强风吹来，一直停泊在海水中的船开始摇荡，突然，起锚机和锚链管之间松弛的锚链叮当作响，向前滑动了一英寸，缓缓离开甲板，人们非常吃惊，觉得钢铁里似乎一直潜伏着未知的生命。锚链管里相互摩擦的链环发出一种声音，传遍了全船，这声音像负重者的轻轻呻吟和叹息。拉力传到了起锚机上，锁链就像拉紧的绳子，开始振动——螺旋制动机的手柄也猛然轻微振动。辛格尔顿走上前去。 A breeze was coming.The ship that had been lying tide-rode swung to a heavier puff; and suddenly the slack of the chain cable between the windlass and the hawse-pipe clinked, slipped forward an inch, and rose gently off the deck with a startling suggestion as of unsuspected life that had been lurking stealthily in the iron.In the hawse-pipe the grinding links sent through the ship a sound like a low groan of a man sighing under a burden.The strain came on the windlass, the chain tautened like a string, vibrated—and the handle of the screw-brake moved in slight jerks.Singleton stepped forward.
直到那时，他就一直站在那儿，看似沉思，实则什么也没想，看似沉着冷静，实则毫无希望，脸上既严肃又茫然——俨然是神秘大海六十岁的孩子。他整个一生的思想可以用这六个词表达，但眼前这些物体的震动对于他的生存来说不亚于跳动的心脏，在他那严肃沧桑的脸上唤起了一道警觉的光芒。灯光来回摇摆，这位老人紧蹙着浓密的眉头，仿佛站在疯狂的萨拉班德舞蹈的阴影中，一动不动专心致志地注视着制动机。不久，船体听从了船锚的召唤，微微向前，放松了拉紧的索链。索链松弛下来，低低地垂着，经过一阵轻微的前后摇摆，砰的一声落在船体坚硬的木板上。辛格尔顿牢牢抓住高高的控制杆，身子使劲前倾，将制动机又转了半圈。他又恢复了平衡，大口喘着气，盯着这台威力巨大，外观紧凑的机器。它蹲卧在脚下的甲板上，像一只安静的巨兽——一只令人惊奇而听话的动物。 Till then he had been standing meditative and unthinking, reposeful and hopeless, with a face grim and blank—a sixty-year-old child of the mysterious sea. The thoughts of all his lifetime could have been expressed in six words, but the stir of those things that were as much part of his existence as his beating heart called up a gleam of alert understanding upon the sternness of his aged face.The flame of the lamp swayed, and the old man, with knitted and bushy eyebrows, stood over the brake, watchful and motionless in the wild saraband of dancing shadows.Then the ship, obedient to the call of her anchor, forged ahead slightly and eased the strain.The cable relieved, hung down, and after swaying imperceptibly to and fro dropped with a loud tap on the hard wood planks.Singleton seized the high lever, and, by a violent throw forward of his body, wrung out another half-turn from the brake.He recovered himself, breathed largely, and remained for a while glaring down at the powerful and compact engine that squatted on the deck at his feet like some quiet monster—a creature amazing and tame.
“你……坚持住！”他摆出主人的架子，透过乱蓬蓬的白胡子对制动机叫道。 “You...hold!” he growled at it masterfully in the incult tangle of his white beard.
第二章 CHAPTER TWO
次日黎明时分，“水仙号”起航了。 Next morning, at daylight, the—Narcissus—went to sea.
一层薄薄的轻雾模糊了视野。出了港湾，平静无垠的大海，熠熠发光，宛如宝石镶嵌的地板，海面空旷，犹如天空。像通常一样，短小乌黑的拖船迎风拖拉，然后松开绳索，停下机器，在船尾部盘旋一阵；与此同时，细长的大船缓慢前移，上面挂着一些中桅下帆。松弛的高层帆布在微风吹拂下，鼓成柔软的圆形，宛如朵朵白云被纵横交错的绳子网住。然后，帆脚索拉紧，帆桁吊了起来，轮船变成了一座孤独的金字塔，洁白闪亮，在阳光照射下的薄雾里滑行。拖船直接调头向岸上驶去。二十六双眼睛注视着她那又低又宽的船尾费力地在平滑的水面上爬行，两边桨轮飞转，剧烈地拍打着水花。她就像一只巨大的生长在水里的黑色甲虫，因受不了太阳强光照射，试图逃到远方阴凉的陆地上，可怎么使劲也跑不快。她在天空中留下了一团久久不散的黑烟，水里留下了两行瞬间即逝的泡沫。在她停泊的地方，一团圆形的黑色烟灰留了下来，漂浮在水面上——仿佛动物栖息后留下的肮脏印迹。 A slight haze blurred the horizon.Outside the harbour the measureless expanse of smooth water lay sparkling like a floor of jewels, and as empty as the sky.The short black tug gave a pluck to windward, in the usual way, then let go the rope, and hovered for a moment on the quarter with her engines stopped; while the slim, long hull of the ship moved ahead slowly under lower topsails.The loose upper canvas blew out in the breeze with soft round contours, resembling small white clouds snared in the maze of ropes.Then the sheets were hauled home, the yards hoisted, and the ship became a high and lonely pyramid, gliding, all shining and white, through the sunlit mist.The tug turned short round and went away towards the land.Twenty-six pairs of eyes watched her low broad stern crawling languidly over the smooth swell between the two paddle-wheels that turned fast, beating the water with fierce hurry.She resembled an enormous and aquatic black beetle, surprised by the light, overwhelmed by the sunshine, trying to escape with ineffectual effort into the distant gloom of the land.She left a lingering smudge of smoke on the sky, and two vanishing trails of foam on the water.On the place where she had stopped a round black patch of soot remained, undulating on the swell—an unclean mark of the creature’s rest.
“水仙号”向南独行，在起伏的海面上，在移动的太阳下，巍然屹立，光彩照人。白色的浪花抚摸着船舷；闪光的海水拍打着船体；陆地渐渐远去，鸟儿展着一动不动的翅膀叽叽喳喳飞过摇摆的桅顶。不久，陆地看不见了，鸟儿飞走了；抬眼西望，一艘驶向孟买的阿拉伯尖顶单帆船，像直立的三角形出现在天边一线，梦幻似地徘徊片刻，便消失得无影无踪。轮船驶过，留下无尽而笔直的波痕，波痕延伸开来，与一整天难熬的孤独相伴。浓密的乌云下，夕阳在海面上燃烧，发出深红色的光芒。傍晚时分，狂风从身后袭来，霎时融入了一阵哗哗啦啦的暴雨之中。阵雨过后，从桅冠到吃水线，轮船处处熠熠发光，只是帆篷颜色有些发暗。在季风吹拂下，轮船轻快地行驶着，甲板已经收拾干净，准备过夜；随着轮船的行进，波涛单调的刷刷声不绝于耳，中间夹杂着船尾安排值夜班的点名声，高处滑轮短促的吱呀声，还有时断时续的海风呼啸声。 The—Narcissus—left alone, heading south, seemed to stand resplendent and still upon the restless sea, under the moving sun.Flakes of foam swept past her sides; the water struck her with flashing blows; the land glided away slowly fading; a few birds screamed on motionless wings over the swaying mastheads.But soon the land disappeared, the birds went away; and to the west the pointed sail of an Arab dhow running for Bombay, rose triangular and upright above the sharp edge of the horizon, lingered and vanished like an illusion.Then the ship’s wake, long and straight, stretched itself out through a day of immense solitude.The setting sun, burning on the level of the water, flamed crimson below the blackness of heavy rain clouds.The sunset squall, coming up from behind, dissolved itself into the short deluge of a hissing shower.It left the ship glistening from trucks to water-line, and with darkened sails.She ran easily before a fair monsoon, with her decks cleared for the night; and, moving along with her, was heard the sustained and monotonous swishing of the waves, mingled with the low whispers of men mustered aft for the setting of watches; the short plaint of some block aloft; or, now and then, a loud sigh of wind.
贝克先生走出船舱，身后门还没顾上关，就猛然叫第一个人的名字。他准备到甲板上值夜班。按航海惯例，返航途中大副负责值第一个夜班——从八点到半夜。贝克先生听到最后一声“到，先生！”　便闷闷不乐地说：“负责掌舵的和瞭望的可以休息啦”；说完他便拖着沉重的脚步爬上了通向船尾楼的梯子，来到上风处。不一会儿，克赖顿先生轻轻地吹着口哨走下尾楼，来到船舱。门阶上，乘务员穿着拖鞋，懒洋洋地躺着想心事，衬衣的袖子直卷到胳肢窝处。 Mr. Baker, coming out of his cabin, called out the first name sharply before closing the door behind him.He was going to take charge of the deck.On the homeward trip, according to an old custom of the sea, the chief officer takes the first night-watch—from eight till midnight.So Mr. Baker, after he had heard the last “Yes, sir!” said moodily, “Relieve the wheel and look-out”; and climbed with heavy feet the poop ladder to windward.Soon after Mr. Creighton came down, whistling softly, and went into the cabin.On the doorstep the steward lounged, in slippers, meditative, and with his shirt-sleeves rolled up to the armpits.
主甲板上，厨子锁了厨房门，与年轻的查利为一双袜子在争论。在船中间的黑暗处，只听厨师激动地说：“你这个人就不配别人待你好。我把你的袜子烤干了，而你现在却抱怨烧了洞——还骂脏话！当着我的面！我要不是基督徒——你当然不是，你这小无赖——我会敲碎你的头……滚开！”人们三三两两沿着船舷腰部或站立沉思或默然走动。返航途中头一个繁忙的日子就这样恢复了常态，回到了枯燥的平静之中。船尾处高高的尾楼上，贝克先生踱步沉思，嘴里不住发出哼哼唧唧的声音。船头，负责瞭望的水手直挺挺地站在两个锚爪之间，没完没了地哼着同一支曲子，目不转睛、恪尽职守，茫然地注视着前方。清澈的夜晚，繁星布满了空旷的苍穹。大海上，闪烁的群星好像有了生命；它们环绕在疾驶的行船四周；比人们凝视的眼睛还要明亮，像人们的心灵一样难以揣测。航程已经开始，这艘大船宛如离开陆地的一块碎片，孤独而轻快地向前行驶，就像一颗小行星。船的周围，深邃的大海和天空在遥不可及的远方交汇。无限的寂寞环绕着她，寂寞不断变化而又始终如一，一直那么单调，一直那么威严。不时也有其他白点游动，承载着生命，出现在远方——又消失了；专注于奔赴自己的命运去了。太阳整天俯视着她，每天早上，怀着永恒的好奇心，睁着炽热的圆眼升起来。她有自己的未来；那些踩在她甲板上的人赋予了她生命；就像把她抛弃在大海上的陆地一样，她也有难以忍受的遗憾和希望的重负。怯懦的真理和无畏的谎言占据了她；就像陆地一样，她没有自知之明，徒有华丽外表——已被人类判定为卑贱的命运。途中令人敬畏的寂寞，使得她这次朝圣般航行的卑鄙使命也变得高尚起来。仿佛被追求某一高尚事业的勇气所指引，她吐着泡沫，向南行驶。无垠大海的微笑缩短了时间的长度。日子一天接一天过去了，宛如灯塔的闪光，灿烂、迅速，而黑夜则暗藏玄机、短暂仓促，仿佛飞逝的梦。 On the main deck the cook, locking up the galley doors, had an altercation with young Charley about a pair of socks.He could be heard saying impressively, in the darkness amidships: “You don’t deserve a kindness.I’ve been drying them for you, and now you complain about the holes—and you swear, too!Right in front of me!If I hadn’t been a Christian—which you ain’t, you young ruffian—I would give you a clout on the head....Go away!” Men in couples or threes stood pensive or moved silently along the bulwarks in the waist.The first busy day of a homeward passage was sinking into the dull peace of resumed routine.Aft, on the high poop, Mr. Baker walked shuffling and grunted to himself in the pauses of his thoughts.Forward, the look-out man, erect between the flukes of the two anchors, hummed an endless tune, keeping his eyes fixed dutifully ahead in a vacant stare.A multitude of stars coming out into the clear night peopled the emptiness of the sky. They glittered, as if alive above the sea; they surrounded the running ship on all sides; more intense than the eyes of a staring crowd, and as inscrutable as the souls of men.The passage had begun, and the ship, a fragment detached from the earth, went on lonely and swift like a small planet.Round her the abysses of sky and sea met in an unattainable frontier.A great circular solitude moved with her, ever changing and ever the same, always monotonous and always imposing.Now and then another wandering white speck, burdened with life, appeared far off—disappeared; intent on its own destiny.The sun looked upon her all day, and every morning rose with a burning, round stare of undying curiosity.She had her own future; she was alive with the lives of those beings who trod her decks; like that earth which had given her up to the sea, she had an intolerable load of regrets and hopes.On her lived timid truth and audacious lies; and, like the earth, she was unconscious, fair to see—and condemned by men to an ignoble fate.The august loneliness of her path lent dignity to the sordid inspiration of her pilgrimage.She drove foaming to the southward, as if guided by the courage of a high endeavour.The smiling greatness of the sea dwarfed the extent of time.The days raced after one another, brilliant and quick like the flashes of a lighthouse, and the nights, eventful and short, resembled fleeting dreams.
人们各就各位，每半小时响一次的钟声支配着他们操不完心的生活。不管夜里还是白天，舵轮旁总能看到一名水手的头和双肩，在星光或阳光的映衬下，在旋转的舵柄上方显得非常沉稳。面孔在交替变换。有年轻的面孔，有长着胡须的面孔，有黝黑的面孔：有的恬静，有的郁闷，但大家在海上都亲如兄弟；用同样关注的目光密切注视着罗盘或帆篷。　船长阿利斯顿神情严肃，脖子上围着一条红色的旧围巾，整天呆在尾楼里。夜里，他经常走进周围的黑暗中，就像坟上的鬼魂，警觉地默默站在群星下，长长的睡衣像旗子一样不停地摆动——然后，一声不响，又回到舱里。他出生在彭特兰湾的海滨。年轻时曾在彼得黑德捕鲸船上当过鱼叉手。一谈起那段时光，他那不安的灰色眼神就变得呆滞冰冷，就像若隐若现的浮冰。后来，为了改变境况，他去了东印度做生意。自从“水仙号”建成后，他一直当船长。他爱这艘船，但驾驶起来毫不留情；因为他内心的抱负是，有朝一日要让她完成一次非凡的快速航行，使她载入航海史册。他提起自己主人的名字时总是面带嘲笑，平时很少与船员讲话，指责别人错误时轻声细语，但句句切中要害。他的头发呈铁灰色，面容刻板，就像皮质舞鞋的颜色。他这辈子都是每天六点刮胡子，但有一回（在毛里求斯西南八十英里处被狂暴的飓风所困），一连三日都没有刮。除了不宽恕人类的上帝，他无所畏惧，只希望有一座小房子，房子周围有一片空地——在内地的乡间——远离大海，在那儿度过余生。 The men had shaken into their places, and the half-hourly voice of the bells ruled their life of unceasing care.Night and day the head and shoulders of a seaman could be seen aft by the wheel, outlined high against sunshine or starlight, very steady above the stir of revolving spokes.The faces changed, passing in rotation.Youthful faces, bearded faces, dark faces: faces serene, or faces moody, but all akin with the brotherhood of the sea; all with the same attentive expression of eyes, carefully watching the compass or the sails.Captain Allistoun, serious, and with an old red muffler round his throat, all day long pervaded the poop.At night, many times he rose out of the darkness of the companion, such as a phantom above a grave, and stood watchful and mute under the stars, his night-shirt fluttering like a flag—then, without a sound, sank down again.He was born on the shores of the Pentland Firth.In his youth he attained the rank of harpooner in Peterhead whalers.When he spoke of that time his restless grey eyes became still and cold, like the loom of ice. Afterwards he went into the East Indian trade for the sake of change.He had commanded the—Narcissus—since she was built.He loved his ship, and drove her unmercifully; for his secret ambition was to make her accomplish some day a brilliantly quick passage which would be mentioned in nautical papers.He pronounced his owner’s name with a sardonic smile, spoke but seldom to his officers, and reproved errors in a gentle voice, with words that cut to the quick.His hair was iron-grey, his face hard and of the colour of pump-leather.He shaved every morning of his life—at six—but once (being caught in a fierce hurricane eighty miles southwest of Mauritius) he had missed three consecutive days.He feared naught but an unforgiving God, and wished to end his days in a little house, with a plot of ground attached—far in the country—out of sight of the sea.
他，这个小小世界的主宰者，很少走下他那威严的高高在上的船尾楼。他下面——他脚下，不妨说——一群平凡的人过着忙碌而无足轻重的生活。沿着主甲板，贝克先生嘴里哼哼叽叽，样子十分凶狠，其实并无恶意；他要求我们不懈地工作，因为他花钱雇我们——他曾说——就是干活的。甲板上工作的船员身体健壮，心满意足——海员一到远海，大都如此。在离最近的陆地一千英里的任何一处，上帝带给人类的真正安宁便开始了；万能的主把信使派往那里，不是用暴怒的方式去惩罚罪恶、放纵和愚蠢，而是像慈父般地去磨炼这些淳朴的心灵——这些天真的心灵对生活一无所知，嫉妒和贪婪对它们的搏动也毫无影响。 He, the ruler of that minute world, seldom descended from the Olympian heights of his poop.Below him—at his feet, so to speak—common mortals led their busy and insignificant lives.Along the main deck, Mr. Baker grunted in a manner bloodthirsty and innocuous; and kept all our noses to the grindstone, being—as he once remarked—paid for doing that very thing.The men working about the deck were healthy and contented—as most seamen are, when once well out to sea. The true peace of God begins at any spot a thousand miles from the nearest land; and when He sends there the messengers of His might it is not in terrible wrath against crime, presumption, and folly, but paternally, to chasten simple hearts—ignorant hearts that know nothing of life, and beat undisturbed by envy or greed.
傍晚时分，打扫过的甲板呈现出十分宁静的景象，就像陆地上的秋天。夕阳渐渐落下，外面斗篷似地裹着一层温暖的云。远处，备用桅杆的尽头，水手长和木匠各自手臂交叉着坐在一起；两人关系亲密，个个膀大腰圆，身强力壮。他俩旁边那位矮胖的修帆工——他曾在海军干过——一边衔着烟斗喷云吐雾，一边讲着关于海军将军们子虚乌有的故事。三三两两的人们在这有限的空间里踱来踱去，毫不费力地保持着一致的步伐和身体的平衡。宽敞的猪圈里，猪在哼哼唧唧地叫着。贝尔法斯特若有所思地把胳膊肘靠在猪圈的围栏上，以无声的冥想与猪进行交流。船员们敞开衬衣，露着太阳晒黑的胸脯，坐在通往水手舱的阶梯上，下面垫着系船的绳索。前桅旁，几个人围坐在一起，议论着绅士的特征。一位说：“有钱就行。”另一位坚持说：“不对，是他们的说话方式。”　瘸子诺尔斯一瘸一拐地走上前来，脸也没洗（他在水手舱里可是有名的邋遢人），狡黠地笑着，几颗黄黄的尖牙露了出来。他巧妙地解释说他“曾见过他们中一些人的裤子。”裤子的后面——他观察到——由于长期在办公室坐着磨得比纸还薄，而其它部位看起来非常完好，可以再穿多年。这都是表面现象。“当个绅士并不难，”他说，“只要一辈子有份干净工作就行。”他们无休止地争论着，像孩子一样固执己见；他们重复着自己的真知灼见，彼此争得面红耳赤；此时，微风吹拂着前桅上的帆篷，形成了一个巨大的凹洞，漂扬在他们裸露的头顶上，微风也轻柔地吹拂着他们乱蓬蓬的头发，像是亲昵的爱抚。 In the evening the cleared decks had a reposeful aspect, resembling the autumn of the earth.The sun was sinking to rest, wrapped in a mantle of warm clouds.Forward, on the end of the spare spars, the boatswain and the carpenter sat together with crossed arms;
