他们齐声叫喊：“吉米！吉米！”水手长在上面用低沉的声音叫道：“你。韦特！” 一阵沉默过后，贝尔法斯特乞求似地叫道：“吉米，吉米，亲爱的，你还活着吧？” 水手长说：“再叫一次！一齐喊，伙计们！” 于是大家一起使劲喊。沃米博的叫声像狗的狂吠。贝尔法斯特用一个铁块猛敲舱壁一侧。一切声响戛然而止。耳畔尖叫声和捶打声，微弱而清晰——仿佛合唱后的独唱。他还活着。他在我们下面急切地叫着敲着，好像过早地把活人装进了棺材。我们动手解救他。我们不顾一切，向那堆讨厌的东西发起进攻，有重的、尖的、难拿的。水手长爬到一边去找一根飘飞的绳头；沃米博被训斥声喝退：“别跳！别过来，糊涂蛋！”他依然呆在我们上面睁大眼睛观察。只见他两眼炯炯有神，牙齿熠熠发光，头发东倒西歪；活像一个疯疯癫癫的弱智野人，幸灾乐祸地旁观一个极其狼狈的落难者。水手长恳请大伙“搭把手”，于是，一条绳子放了下去。我们把各种杂物捆紧，只见它们晃晃悠悠被拉了上去，再也没被看见过。把东西扔到海里去的激动情绪支配了我们。我们拼命去救人，手被划破了，彼此粗鲁地招呼着。吉米不停的嚎叫声让人心烦；他像受刑的女人一样，扯着嗓子针刺一般尖叫，中间也不换气；他拳打脚踢，砰砰作响。他那极度的恐惧压榨着我们的心，我们感到非常痛苦，不想救他，想离开这井一般幽深、树一般摇摆的地方，逃到他听不见我们的去处，回到无比安静的船尾楼，在那儿我们宁愿被动等死。我们高喊着让他“看在上帝的份上，闭嘴”。 可他却叫得更凶了。他一定认为我们听不见。也许他自己听着也很微弱。我们想象着他蜷缩在上铺边沿，黑暗中气急败坏地用两个拳头捶着木板，张着大嘴不住地叫喊。这真是痛苦难耐的时刻。一片乌云掠过太阳，无情地将门口遮得暗淡许多。船的每次震动都会带来痛苦。我们四处攀爬，找不到喘息的地方，感到异常恶心。水手长对呆在下面的我们叫道：“搭把手！搭把手！你们要不快来帮忙，我们马上就会被冲走！”海浪一连三次越过高高的船侧，把满桶满桶的水直泼我们的头顶。吉米被这次冲击吓坏了，暂时停止了叫喊——也许是在等待船的沉没——接着又开始了他那凄惨的嚎叫，仿佛这阵恐惧给他增添了活力。地板上钉子洒了一地，有几英寸厚。真是可怕。世界上任何一颗没钉牢的钉子，仿佛都溜进了木匠的工作间。它们堆在那儿，各式各样，是七次航海积攒下来的。有镀锡平头铁钉，铜钉（像针一样尖）；抽水泵上用的钉，大头，就像小个铁蘑菇；无头钉（可怕）；法国钉，又亮又细。这些钉子厚厚地堆在一起，比刺猬还扎人。我们犹豫着想找一把铲子，这时，下面的吉米仿佛有人要剥他的皮似地大叫起来。我们呻吟着，把指头伸进钉子堆中，刺得生疼，摇摇手，钉子和血滴纷纷落下。我们把盛满各种钉子的帽子递给水手长，他好像在举行一个神秘的和平仪式，使劲将它们撒到愤怒的大海里。 Together they shouted: “Jimmy!Jim!” From above the boatswain contributed a deep growl: “You.Wait!” In a pause, Belfast entreated: “Jimmy, darlin’, are ye aloive?” The boatswain said: “Again!All together, boys!” All yelled excitedly.Wamibo made noises resembling loud barks.Belfast drummed on the side of the bulkhead with a piece of iron.All ceased suddenly.The sound of screaming and hammering went on thin and distinct—like a solo after a chorus.He was alive.He was screaming and knocking below us with the hurry of a man prematurely shut up in a coffin.We went to work.We attacked with desperation the abominable heap of things heavy, of things sharp, of things clumsy to handle.The boatswain crawled away to find somewhere a flying end of a rope; and Wamibo, held back by shouts:—“Don’t jump!...Don’t come in here, muddle-head!”—remained glaring above us—all shining eyes, gleaming fangs, tumbled hair; resembling an amazed and half-witted fiend gloating over the extraordinary agitation of the damned.The boatswain adjured us to “bear a hand,” and a rope descended.We made things fast to it and they went up spinning, never to be seen by man again.A rage to fling things overboard possessed us.We worked fiercely, cutting our hands and speaking brutally to one another.Jimmy kept up a distracting row; he screamed piercingly, without drawing breath, like a tortured woman; he banged with hands and feet.The agony of his fear wrung our hearts so terribly that we longed to abandon him, to get out of that place deep as a well and swaying like a tree, to get out of his hearing, back on the poop where we could wait passively for death in incomparable repose.We shouted to him to “shut up, for God’s sake.” He redoubled his cries.He must have fancied we could not hear him.Probably he heard his own clamour but faintly.We could picture him crouching on the edge of the upper berth, letting out with both fists at the wood, in the dark, and with his mouth wide open for that unceasing cry.Those were loathsome moments.A cloud driving across the sun would darken the doorway menacingly.Every movement of the ship was pain.We scrambled about with no room to breathe, and felt frightfully sick.The boatswain yelled down at us:—“Bear a hand!Bear a hand!We two will be washed away from here directly if you ain’t quick!” Three times a sea leaped over the high side and flung bucketfuls of water on our heads.Then Jimmy, startled by the shock, would stop his noise for a moment—waiting for the ship to sink, perhaps—and began again, distressingly loud, as if invigorated by the gust of fear.At the bottom the nails lay in a layer several inches thick.It was ghastly.Every nail in the world, not driven in firmly somewhere, seemed to have found its way into that carpenter’s shop.There they were, of all kinds, the remnants of stores from seven voyages.Tin-tacks, copper tacks (sharp as needles); pump nails with big heads, like tiny iron mushrooms; nails without any heads (horrible); French nails polished and slim.They lay in a solid mass more inabordable than a hedgehog.We hesitated, yearning for a shovel, while Jimmy below us yelled as though he had been flayed.Groaning, we dug our fingers in, and very much hurt, shook our hands, scattering nails and drops of blood.We passed up our hats full of assorted nails to the boatswain, who, as if performing a mysterious and appeasing rite, cast them wide upon a raging sea.
我们终于接近了舱壁。舱壁是由结实的木板做的。她——“水仙号”这艘船——每个细节都做得十分精细。我们想，没有哪艘船的舱壁比她的更结实了。随后我们意识到，匆忙中我们把所有工具都扔进了大海。荒唐的小个子贝尔法斯特想用自己的体重推倒舱壁，像羚羊一样两脚腾空，嘴里骂着克莱德的造船工人干活太实在。他也捎带着骂起了整个英国北部、地球上其他地方、海洋——以及他所有的伙伴。当他重重地把脚跟放下来时，发誓说，他将永远永远不会同那些连“膝盖与臂肘都分不清”的傻瓜交往了。他咚咚地撞着，简直要把吉米最后一点魂也吓掉。我们能够听到那个惹人生气又令人同情的家伙在木板下窜来窜去。他已经喊破了嗓子，现在只能痛苦地发出吱吱呀呀的沙哑声。他的头或背这儿蹭一下，那儿蹭一下，让人难以捉摸。他躲避那无形的打击时，嘴里发出吱吱的声音。这种声音比嗷嗷直叫还凄惨。突然阿奇取出一根撬杠。这是他原来放在那儿的；还有一把小斧头。我们兴奋得欢呼起来。他猛劲砸下去，小木片在我们眼前乱飞。水手长在上面大叫：“当心！小心点。别把人砸死了。手脚轻点！”　沃米博欣喜若狂，低着头，拼命地给我们加油：“嘿！砸死他！嘿！嘿！”　我们担心他会滑到我们中间来误伤了谁，便连忙恳请水手长“把那该死的芬兰人推下船去”。然后大家一起对着下面的木板大声叫喊：“别站在下面！向前去。” 接着听听有没有动静。我们只听到头顶上低沉呜咽的风声，混杂着海浪的咆哮声和嘶嘶声。轮船仿佛被绝望压垮了，无精打采地摇荡着，我们的头随着她那不自然的起伏而摇摆。贝尔法斯特嚷嚷道：“为了上帝的爱，吉米，你到底在哪儿？……敲一敲！吉米亲爱的！……敲一敲！你这头残忍的黒兽！敲呀！”他悄无声息，就像墓中的死人；我们则像站在坟墓上的人，几乎要流出眼泪——可我们还感到烦恼、紧张、疲劳；想罢手不干，离开这里，找个地方躺下，在那里，我们能够看清危险，平静呼吸。阿奇叫道：“给我腾点地方！”我们蜷曲在他身后，护着头，他在木板接缝处一下接一下地砸着。舱壁咔嚓一下断开了。突然，撬杠从断开的长方形窟窿滑进了一半。就差半英寸没有捅到吉米的头。阿奇连忙抽回撬杠，而那无耻的黑鬼冲到窟窿前，凑上嘴唇，用几乎听不见的微弱声音说“救救我”；他把脑袋伸向窟窿，发疯似地想从一英寸宽三英寸长的小孔中钻出来。在如此混乱的情形下，我们简直被他那不可思议的举止吓呆了。把他赶走似乎不可能。即使阿奇，最终也失去了耐心。“你要是不滚开，我就用撬杠捅你的头。”他大声训斥道，语气十分坚决。他说到做到，他那认真的神态似乎对吉米产生了影响。他突然消失了，于是我们对板壁连撬带扯，那急切的样子就像对付不共戴天的仇敌，一心想把他撕得粉碎。木板裂开了，咔嚓一声坍倒了下来。贝尔法斯特把头和肩探下去，不怀好意地乱摸一通。“我摸到他啦！摸到啦。”他嚷嚷道。“哦！你瞧！……他溜了；我抓住了他！……使劲拉我的腿！……拉呀！”沃米博不住地狂叫。水手长发布指令：“抓住他的头发，贝尔法斯特；直接往上拉，你们两个一块儿！……使劲拉呀！” 我们使劲去拉。我们陡然把贝尔法斯特拽出来，非常不耐烦地将他丢在一边。他坐在那儿，脸色发紫，绝望地啜泣着说：“我咋才能抓住他那该死的短毛？”　突然，吉米的脑袋和肩膀露了出来。他卡在了中间，翻着眼睛，把唾沫吐在了我们脚边。我们急不可耐，疯狂地向他扑去，扯掉了他背上的衬衣，揪住了他的耳朵，对着他大口喘气；仿佛有人松开了他的双腿，一下子他跑到了我们手中。接下来，我们采用同样的动作，一鼓作气，把他推到上面。他发出刺耳的呼吸声，乱踢我们仰着的脸，紧紧抱住他头顶上的两双手臂，身体扭动着向上升起。由于上升急速，他那轻如气囊的身体，简直要从我们手中飞走。我们汗流浃背，攀爬着绳子，进到刺骨的冷风中，大家喘着气，仿佛跳进了冰水中。我们面部发烧，浑身哆嗦，冷气直透骨髓。我们觉得，以前狂风从来没有那么猛烈，大海从来没有那么疯狂，阳光从来没有那么无情和充满讥讽，轮船的处境从来没有那么绝望和可怕。她的每次震颤都是凶兆，预示着她那苦难航程的结束和我们灾难的来临。我们跌跌撞撞离开房门，船的突然摇摆让我们大吃一惊，纷纷摔倒在地，滚作一团。我们觉得船舱侧壁比玻璃还光，比冰还滑。除了一个偶尔用于闩门的长铜钩外，没有其他任何可以依附的东西。沃米博牢牢拉住铜钩，我们拉住沃米博，同时紧紧抓住吉米。他现在彻底瘫软了。似乎连握紧拳头的气力也没有了。我们忘记了恐惧，牢牢抓住吉米。谁也不担心沃米博会松手（我们知道这个野人比船上其他任何三人加起来力气都大），可我们担心铜钩会脱落，我们还深信不疑船已打定主意，最终要翻入大海。然而她并没有翻。一个大浪向我们袭来。水手长结结巴巴地说：“上来，走开。大海平静啦。大家快到船尾去，不然我们会在这儿完蛋的。”我们围着吉米站了起来。我们恳求他稳住不倒，至少要抓紧。他瞪着一双凸出的眼睛，鱼儿一样默不作声，所有的犟劲不复存在了。他不愿站稳；甚至不愿搂住我们的脖子；他简直就是一张黑皮，里面松松散散地装了些棉花和毛绒；他的胳膊和腿仿佛脱了臼，软软的，荡来荡去；他的头滚来滚去；下唇耷拉着，又大又重。我们紧紧护着他，心中充满烦恼与沮丧；我们一群人蹒蹒跚跚，为他遮挡风雨；在死亡的边缘，我们跌跌撞撞，做着神秘可笑的手势，尴尬的样子仿佛一群醉汉围着一具偷来的死尸。 We got to the bulkhead at last.Those were stout planks.She was a ship, well finished in every detail—the—Narcissus—was.They were the stoutest planks ever put into a ship’s bulkhead—we thought—and then we perceived that, in our hurry, we had sent all the tools overboard.Absurd little Belfast wanted to break it down with his own weight, and with both feet leaped straight up like a springbok, cursing the Clyde shipwrights for not scamping their work.Incidentally he reviled all North Britain, the rest of the earth, the sea—and all his companions.He swore, as he alighted heavily on his heels, that he would never, never any more associate with any fool that “hadn’t savee enough to know his knee from his elbow.” He managed by his thumping to scare the last remnant of wits out of Jimmy.We could hear the object of our exasperated solicitude darting to and fro under the planks.He had cracked his voice at last, and could only squeak miserably.His back or else his head rubbed the planks, now here, now there, in a puzzling manner.He squeaked as he dodged the invisible blows.It was more heartrending even than his yells.Suddenly Archie produced a crowbar.He had kept it back; also a small hatchet.We howled with satisfaction.He struck a mighty blow and small chips flew at our eyes.The boatswain above shouted:—“Look out!Look out there.Don’t kill the man.Easy does it!” Wamibo, maddened with excitement, hung head down and insanely urged us:—“Hoo!Strook’im! Hoo!Hoo!” We were afraid he would fall in and kill one of us and, hurriedly, we entreated the boatswain to “shove the blamed Finn overboard.” Then, all together, we yelled down at the planks:—“Stand from under!Get forward,” and listened.We only heard the deep hum and moan of the wind above us, the mingled roar and hiss of the seas.The ship, as if overcome with despair, wallowed lifelessly, and our heads swam with that unnatural motion.Belfast clamoured:—“For the love of God, Jimmy, where are ye?...Knock! Jimmy darlint!...Knock! You bloody black beast!Knock!” He was as quiet as a dead man inside a grave; and, like men standing above a grave, we were on the verge of tears—but with vexation, the strain, the fatigue; with the great longing to be done with it, to get away, and lie down to rest somewhere where we could see our danger and breathe.Archie shouted:—“Gi’e me room!” We crouched behind him, guarding our heads, and he struck time after time in the joint of planks.They cracked.Suddenly the crowbar went halfway in through a splintered oblong hole.It must have missed Jimmy’s head by less than an inch.Archie withdrew it quickly, and that infamous nigger rushed at the hole, put his lips to it, and whispered “Help” in an almost extinct voice; he pressed his head to it, trying madly to get out through that opening one inch wide and three inches long.In our disturbed state we were absolutely paralysed by his incredible action.It seemed impossible to drive him away.Even Archie at last lost his composure.“If ye don’t clear oot I’ll drive the crowbar thro’ your head,” he shouted in a determined voice.He meant what he said, and his earnestness seemed to make an impression on Jimmy.He disappeared suddenly, and we set to prising and tearing at the planks with the eagerness of men trying to get at a mortal enemy, and spurred by the desire to tear him limb from limb.The wood split, cracked, gave way.Belfast plunged in head and shoulders and groped viciously.“I’ve got ’im!Got ’im,” he shouted.“Oh! There!...He’s gone; I’ve got ’im!...Pull at my legs!...Pull!” Wamibo hooted unceasingly.The boatswain shouted directions:—“Catch hold of his hair, Belfast; pull straight up, you two!...Pull fair!” We pulled fair.We pulled Belfast out with a jerk, and dropped him with disgust.In a sitting posture, purple-faced, he sobbed despairingly:—“How can I hold on to ’is blooming short wool?” Suddenly Jimmy’s head and shoulders appeared.He stuck halfway, and with rolling eyes foamed at our feet.We flew at him with brutal impatience, we tore the shirt off his back, we tugged at his ears, we panted over him; and all at once he came away in our hands as though somebody had let go his legs.With the same movement, without a pause, we swung him up.His breath whistled, he kicked our upturned faces, he grasped two pairs of arms above his head, and he squirmed up with such precipitation that he seemed positively to escape from our hands like a bladder full of gas.Streaming with perspiration, we swarmed up the rope, and, coming into the blast of cold wind, gasped like men plunged into icy water.With burning faces we shivered to the very marrow of our bones.Never before had the gale seemed to us more furious, the sea more mad, the sunshine more merciless and mocking, and the position of the ship more hopeless and appalling.Every movement of her was ominous of the end of her agony and of the beginning of ours.We staggered away from the door, and, alarmed by a sudden roll, fell down in a bunch.It appeared to us that the side of the house was more smooth than glass and more slippery than ice. There was nothing to hang on to but a long brass hook used sometimes to keep back an open door.Wamibo held on to it and we held on to Wamibo, clutching our Jimmy.He had completely collapsed now.He did not seem to have the strength to close his hand.We stuck to him blindly in our fear.We were not afraid of Wamibo letting go (we remembered that the brute was stronger than any three men in the ship), but we were afraid of the hook giving way, and we also believed that the ship had made up her mind to turn over at last.But she didn’t.A sea swept over us.The boatswain spluttered:—“Up and away.There’s a lull.Away aft with you, or we will all go to the devil here.” We stood up surrounding Jimmy.We begged him to hold up, to hold on, at least.He glared with his bulging eyes, mute as a fish, and with all the stiffening knocked out of him.He wouldn’t stand; he wouldn’t even as much as clutch at our necks; he was only a cold black skin loosely stuffed with soft cotton wool; his arms and legs swung jointless and pliable; his head rolled about; the lower lip hung down, enormous and heavy.We pressed round him, bothered and dismayed; sheltering him we swung here and there in a body; and on the very brink of eternity we tottered all together with concealing and absurd gestures, like a lot of drunken men embarrassed with a stolen corpse.
我们必须得采取措施。一定把他弄到船尾。我们用绳子松松地系在他的两个胳肢窝下面，冒着生命危险，把他悬在前桅帆角索的铁角上。他一声不吭；看着既可笑又可怜，仿佛失去一半木屑的木偶；接着，我们穿过主甲板，开始了危险的旅程，小心翼翼地拖走这个可怜、瘦弱、令人憎恶的包袱。他不太重，但是即使他有一吨重的话，移动起来也不会比现在更困难。毫不夸张地说，我们是一人接一人地把他递过去的。每隔一会儿，我们就近把他系在系索栓上，喘口气，整理一下队形。要是系索栓断了，他就会无可挽回地掉进南部这片海洋，但他只得冒此风险；过了一会儿，他明白了怎么回事，便轻轻呻吟起来，费了很大劲，才哼出几句话。我们急切地侧耳细听。他抱怨我们太粗心，让他冒如此大的风险：“你看，我从那儿出来以后。”他有气无力地说着。“那儿”指的是他的小屋。并且是他自己出来的。显然我们什么也没帮他！……不要紧……我们继续对他施救，而他只能碰运气啦，因为我们别无选择；尽管当时我们比什么时候都恨他——恨他超过天底下任何事物——我们也不愿丢下他不管。迄今为止是我们救了他；而成功与否成了我们与大海之间的私事。我们的想法是对他永不放弃。如果我们（打一个不当的比喻）为救一个空桶而经历相似的艰辛和困难，那么，这只空桶对我们来说也会像吉米一样珍贵。事实上应该更为珍贵，因为我们没有理由痛恨水桶。可我们痛恨詹姆斯·韦特。我们无法摆脱心中的巨大怀疑：这个令人恐惧的黑人是在装病，面对我们的艰辛、我们的蔑视、我们的忍耐，他无动于衷，一直在装——现在不顾我们的忠诚——面对死亡，依然在装。他那不仗义的谎言令我们憎恶，同时，也唤醒了我们模糊不清、存有瑕疵的道德观念。可他却男子汉般地坚持他的劣行——真是令人吃惊。不！这不可能。他也完全处于极度痛苦之中。他感觉到那富有挑衅而又不可抗拒的死神时刻陪伴着他，结果造成了他这种坏脾气。不管谁与这专横的挚友相处都可能会发怒的。可是，我们又是什么样的人——满脑子都是这种念头！愤恨和猜疑在我们内心争斗，结果蹂躏了我们最美好的感情。因为怀疑我们憎恨他；因为不信任我们讨厌他。我们没有足够的理由鄙视他——同样，对他同情也有可能会损害自己的尊严。因此，我们尽管憎恨他，还是小心谨慎地把他从一个人的手中传到了另一个人的手中。我们大声喊道：“接好了吗？”“好啦。没问题。松手吧。” Something had to be done.We had to get him aft.A rope was tied slack under his armpits, and, reaching up at the risk of our lives, we hung him on the fore-sheet cleet.He emitted no sound; he looked as ridiculously lamentable as a doll that had lost half its sawdust, and we started on our perilous journey over the main deck, dragging along with care that pitiful, that limp, that hateful burden.He was not very heavy, but had he weighed a ton he could not have been more awkward to handle.We literally passed him from hand to hand.Now and then we had to hang him up on a handy belaying-pin, to draw a breath and reform the line.Had the pin broken he would have irretrievably gone into the Southern Ocean, but he had to take his chance of that; and after a little while, becoming apparently aware of it, he groaned slightly, and with a great effort whispered a few words.We listened eagerly.He was reproaching us with our carelessness in letting him run such risks: “Now, after I got myself out from there,” he breathed out weakly.“There” was his cabin.And he got himself out.We had nothing to do with it apparently!...No matter....We went on and let him take his chances, simply because we could not help it; for though at that time we hated him more than ever—more than anything under heaven—we did not want to lose him.We had so far saved him; and it had become a personal matter between us and the sea.We meant to stick to him.Had we (by an incredible hypothesis) undergone similar toil and trouble for an empty cask, that cask would have become as precious to us as Jimmy was.More precious, in fact, because we would have had no reason to hate the cask.And we hated James Wait.We could not get rid of the monstrous suspicion that this astounding black-man was shamming sick, had been malingering heartlessly in the face of our toil, of our scorn, of our patience—and now was malingering in the face of our devotion—in the face of death.Our vague and imperfect morality rose with disgust at his unmanly lie.But he stuck to it manfully—amazingly.No! It couldn’t be.He was at all extremity.His cantankerous temper was only the result of the provoking invincible-ness of that death he felt by his side.Any man may be angry with such a masterful chum.But, then, what kind of men were we—with our thoughts!Indignation and doubt grappled within us in a scuffle that trampled upon the finest of our feelings.And we hated him because of the suspicion; we detested him because of the doubt.We could not scorn him safely—neither could we pity him without risk to our dignity.So we hated him and passed him carefully from hand to hand.We cried, “Got him?”—“Yes.All right.Let go.”
这样，他从一个仇敌的手中晃荡着被传到了另一仇敌的手中，看起来就像一个破旧的枕头一般毫无生气。黑黑的脸上两只眼睛露出两条窄窄的白缝。气息从双唇中溢出，带着风箱般的声响。我们终于到了船尾楼的扶梯前，由于这个地方相对安全，我们这些筋疲力尽的人便挤在一起休息片刻。这时他开始喃喃自语。我们这些人真是不可救药，总是急着听听他到底说些什么。这次他怒气冲冲地嘟囔道：“你们费了不少时间来救我！我还以为你们这群机灵的家伙早已被冲到海里去啦。什么挡住了你们？嗨？害怕吗？”　我们没有回答。我们叹着气又开始把他往上拖。我们内心的秘密和热切的愿望是用拳头猛击他的脑袋；而此刻我们却温柔地移动他，似乎他是玻璃做成的…… And he swung from one enemy to another, showing about as much life as an old bolster would do. His eyes made two narrow white slits in the black face.The air escaped through his lips with a noise like the sound of bellows.We reached the poop ladder at last, and it being a comparatively safe place, we lay for a moment in an exhausted heap to rest a little.He began to mutter.We were always incurably anxious to hear what he had to say.This time he mumbled peevishly, “It took you some time to come!I began to think the whole smart lot of you had been washed overboard.What kept you back?Hey? Funk?” We said nothing.With sighs we started again to drag him up. The secret and ardent desire of our hearts was the desire to beat him viciously with our fists about the head; and we handled him as tenderly as though he had been made of glass....
我们回到船尾楼，就像流浪者漂泊多年后回到了寂寞忧伤的故人之中。他们的眼睛在眼眶中慢慢地转动，瞥了我们一眼。这时传来了窃窃私语：“你们找到了他没有？”　这些熟悉的面孔看起来既陌生又亲切；显得更加憔悴、肮脏；个个脸上交织着疲惫与渴望的表情。在我们离开期间，他们似乎瘦了许多，仿佛处在被遗弃的境地，长期忍饥挨饿。船长手腕上缠着一圈绳子，单膝跪地，身体摇摆着，面容冰冷僵硬；可他的眼睛却活灵活现，密切关注着轮船动向，不曾理会任何人，好像沉浸在了超自然的努力中。我们把詹姆斯·韦特捆在一个安全的地方。贝克先生爬过来帮忙。克赖顿先生仰面躺着，脸色异常苍白，嘴里喃喃说道：“干得不错。”然后不屑地向我们、吉米和天空瞥了一眼，慢慢地闭上了眼睛。周围不断有人动弹动弹，但多数人无动于衷，在狭小的空间里，嘴里叽叽咕咕，浑身哆哆嗦嗦。夕阳西下。太阳是那样巨大，周围没有一丝乌云，红红的，低低的，仿佛在弯腰观察他们的脸。狂风呼啸着穿过太阳长长的光芒，那光芒明亮而寒冷，直直地照在凝视着的眼睛中放大的瞳孔上，可瞳孔一眨不眨。粘连在一起的缕缕头发和纠缠在一块的胡须，因夹杂了海水的盐分全变成了灰白色。人人面色如土，眼睛下面的黑斑一直延伸到耳际，笼罩了整个凹陷面颊的低洼处。嘴唇青灰消瘦，开合困难，仿佛与牙齿粘在了一起。有人在阳光下苦笑，打着寒战。也有人悲伤不已，一动不动。查利突然发觉自己因年轻而不被当回事，颇感忿恨，眼中射出可怕的光芒。两个没长胡须的挪威人就像虚弱的孩子，呆呆地注视着什么。下风侧水天相接处，黑浪翻滚，直冲灼烧的太阳。太阳缓慢下沉，圆圆的，亮亮的，浪尖冲击着明亮光环的边缘。其中一位挪威人似乎看到了这幅美景，十分惊喜，打开了话匣子。他的声音惊动了其他人，于是，大家都跟着动了起来。他们僵硬地晃晃脑袋，或者费劲地转过身望着他，感到吃惊，恐惧，或者仍然保持着深深的沉默。他对着落日念念有词，频频点头，此时，滚滚巨浪开始掠过深红色的圆盘；数英里辽阔汹涌的海面上，高浪的阴影随着奔腾的黑暗，扫过人们的面颊。一个顶似鸡冠的卷浪，发着嘶嘶的轰鸣声破碎了，太阳像被扑灭了一样，没了踪影。絮絮叨叨的谈话声时断时续，随后与白昼一同消失了。接着是一阵叹息声。海浪破碎声过后突然出现了片刻平静，有人少气无力地说：“那个讨厌的外国佬疯了。”　一个海员腰间系着绳子，用一只张开的手，反复快速地拍打着甲板。在越来越浓的暮霭中，只见一个大个子出现在船尾，他四肢着地，匍匐前行，那动作就像一只小心翼翼的大型野兽。那是贝克先生，他正从这排人身边经过。他对谁都哼唧两声表示鼓励，同时摸摸系在他们身上的绳索。有的眯着眼睛，就像受不了热时那样喘着气；有的用梦呓般的声音机械地答道：“啊！啊！先生！”　他爬过一个又一个，嘴里不停地咕哝道：“咳！……还得帮她渡过难关。”可是谁也没料到他会突然怒吼起来，责骂诺尔斯，因为他把应急舵具上的滑索截下了一长段。“咳！——真丢人——备用舵具——难道你不比谁清楚！——咳！——还是一等水手哩！咳！”那位跛脚水手无言以对。他咕哝道：“找点绳子给我系上吧，先生。” “咳！绳子——给你系上。你是勤杂工还是水手——什么？咳！——也许马上就用得上那舵啦——咳！——对于轮船来说，它比你这一瘸一拐的行尸走肉用处大。咳！——留着吧！——留着，既然你已经用了。” The return on the poop was like the return of wanderers after many years amongst people marked by the desolation of time.Eyes were turned slowly in their sockets, glancing at us.Faint murmurs were heard, “Have you got ’im after all?” The well-known faces looked strange and familiar; they seemed faded and grimy; they had a mingled expression of fatigue and eagerness.They seemed to have become much thinner during our absence, as if all these men had been starving for a long time in their abandoned attitudes.The captain, with a round turn of a rope on his wrist, and kneeling on one knee, swung with a face cold and stiff; but with living eyes he was still holding the ship up, heeding no one, as if lost in the unearthly effort of that endeavour.We fastened up James Wait in a safe place.Mr. Baker scrambled along to lend a hand.Mr. Creighton, on his back, and very pale, muttered, “Well done,” and gave us, Jimmy and the sky, a scornful glance, then closed his eyes slowly.Here and there a man stirred a little, but most of them remained apathetic, in cramped positions, muttering between shivers.The sun was setting.A sun enormous, unclouded and red, declining low as if bending down to look into their faces.The wind whistled across long sunbeams that, resplendent and cold, struck full on the dilated pupils of staring eyes without making them wink.The wisps of hair and the tangled beards were grey with the salt of the sea.The faces were earthy, and the dark patches under the eyes extended to the ears, smudged into the hollows of sunken cheeks.The lips were livid and thin, and when they moved it was with difficulty, as though they had been glued to the teeth.Some grinned sadly in the sunlight, shaking with cold.Others were sad and still.Charley, subdued by the sudden disclosure of the insignificance of his youth, darted fearful glances.The two smooth-faced Norwegians resembled decrepit children, staring stupidly.To leeward, on the edge of the horizon, black seas leaped up towards the glowing sun.It sank slowly, round and blazing, and the crests of waves splashed on the edge of the luminous circle.One of the Norwegians appeared to catch sight of it, and, after giving a violent start, began to speak.His voice, startling the others, made them stir.They moved their heads stiffly, or turning with difficulty, looked at him with surprise, with fear, or in grave silence.He chattered at the setting sun, nodding his head, while the big seas began to roll across the crimson disc; and over miles of turbulent waters the shadows of high waves swept with a running darkness the faces of men.A crested roller broke with a loud hissing roar, and the sun, as if put out, disappeared.The chattering voice faltered, went out together with the light.There were sighs.In the sudden lull that follows the crash of a broken sea a man said wearily, “Here’s that blooming Dutchman gone off his chump.” A seaman, lashed by the middle, tapped the deck with his open hand with unceasing quick flaps.In the gathering greyness of twilight a bulky form was seen rising aft, and began marching on all fours with the movements of some big cautious beast.It was Mr. Baker passing along the line of men.He grunted encouragingly over every one, felt their fastenings.Some, with half-open eyes, puffed like men oppressed by heat; others mechanically and in dreamy voices answered him, “Aye!aye! sir!” He went from one to another grunting, “Ough!...See her through it yet;” and unexpectedly, with loud angry outbursts, blew up Knowles for cutting off a long piece from the fall of the relieving tackle.“Ough!———Ashamed of yourself———Relieving tackle———Don’t you know better!———Ough!———Able seaman!Ough!” The lame man was crushed.He muttered, “Get som’think for a lashing for myself, sir.”—“Ough!Lashing———yourself. Are you a tinker or a sailor———What?Ough!———May want that tackle directly———Ough!———More use to the ship than your lame carcass.Ough!———Keep it!———Keep it, now you’ve done it.”
他慢慢地爬着离开了，嘴里自言自语地说有些成年人“还不如孩子。”　这场争吵令人心情舒畅。可以听到有人发出低声的喝彩：“嗬……嗬。”那些一直在痛苦中打着盹的人们心惊肉跳地问道：“怎么啦？……什么事？”接下来的回答使人们感到意外的兴奋：“不知为了什么事，大副与跛脚杰克翻了脸。”“不会！”……“他能干什么坏事？”有人甚至咯咯地笑了起来。这犹如一丝希望，让人想起了过去平安的日子。吓呆了的唐金这时也突然恢复过来，开始大声嚷嚷：“听，这就是他们跟我们讲话的方式。你们为什么不揍他——你们谁都行？揍他。揍他呀！大副对我们指手画脚。我们和他一样也是人！我们现在都快下地狱了。我们在这破船上忍饥挨饿，为了他们这些黑心肠的恶棍，现在又要淹死了！揍他！”　在这渐深的夜幕下，他尖叫着，哭诉着，嚷嚷道：“揍他！揍他！”　夜越来越深，狂风不停地吼叫，生存权利被漠视给他带来的愤怒与恐惧时时刻刻折磨着他，这种折磨比黑夜中的阴影还可怕。船尾处只听贝克先生说道：“你们中没有谁能制止他——非让我去吗？”“闭嘴！”……“安静点！”几个人十分恼火地叫道，他们冻得瑟瑟发抖。“我马上赏你一记耳光，”一个暗处看不见的人用疲惫的声音说道，“我可不愿让大副费事。”他不再胡闹，失望地静静躺着。 He crawled away slowly, muttering to himself about some men being “worse than children.” It had been a comforting row. Low exclamations were heard: “Hallo...Hallo.”...Those who had been painfully dozing asked with convulsive starts, “What’s up?...What is it?”The answers came with unexpected cheerfulness: “The mate is going bald-headed for lame Jack about something or other.” “No!”....“What ’as he done?”Some one even chuckled.It was like a whiff of hope, like a reminder of safe days.Donkin, who had been stupefied with fear, revived suddenly and began to shout:—“’Ear ’im; that’s the way they tawlk to us. Vy donch ’ee ’it ’im—one ov yer?’It ’im.’It ’im!Comin’ the mate over us.We are as good men as ’ee!We’re all goin’ to ’ell now.We ’ave been starved in this rotten ship, an’ now we’re goin’ to be drowned for them black ’earted bullies!’It ’im!” He shrieked in the deepening gloom, he blubbered and sobbed, screaming:—“’It ’im!’It ’im!” The rage and fear of his disregarded right to live tried the steadfastness of hearts more than the menacing shadows of the night that advanced through the unceasing clamour of the gale.From aft Mr. Baker was heard:—“Is one of you men going to stop him—must I come along?” “Shut up!”...“Keep quiet!” cried various voices, exasperated, trembling with cold.—“You’ll get one across the mug from me directly,” said an invisible seaman, in a weary tone, “I won’t let the mate have the trouble.” He ceased and lay still with the silence of despair.
群星走出了黑色的天幕，闪烁在漆黑的大海上空；海面点缀着泡沫，黑暗翻滚的波涛里迸出了转瞬即逝的耀眼白光，与群星遥相辉映。繁星远远地躲在永恒的静谧中，把寒光射向喧嚣的地球；它们从四周包围着这艘被征服的苦难船只：比得意洋洋的暴徒的眼睛还凶残，与人类的内心世界一样无法接近。 On the black sky the stars, coming out, gleamed over an inky sea that, speckled with foam, flashed back at them the evanescent and pale light of a dazzling whiteness born from the black turmoil of the waves.Remote in the eternal calm they glittered hard and cold above the uproar of the earth; they surrounded the vanquished and tormented ship on all sides: more pitiless than the eyes of a triumphant mob, and as unapproachable as the hearts of men.
冰冷的南风在这深沉壮丽的天空下得意地狂吼。人们在难以抵挡的严寒威逼下瑟瑟发抖，仿佛要被撕成碎片。短促的呻吟刚到僵硬的嘴边就被吹跑了，没有谁能听见。有人低声抱怨“腰部以下完全麻木啦”，而闭目养神的人心里则想着胸口上还压着一块冰呢。有人用手无力地敲打着甲板——没完没了，筋疲力尽——可一点也不觉得手指疼痛，这不由令他们感到惊恐。沃米博目光恍惚，梦游一般。两个斯堪的纳维亚人牙齿打颤，不住地说着毫无意义的话语。几位消瘦的苏格兰人费了很大的劲才使下颚保持不动。几个西部老乡满脸怒气地躺在那儿，体积庞大，神情冷漠，有个人又是打呵欠又是诅咒。还有一位，呼吸时喉咙发出格格的响声。两位饱经风霜的老水手紧挨在一起，闲着无聊，低声谈论起他俩都认识的一家公寓的女老板。他们夸她具有慈母之心，做事慷慨大方；他们甚至扯到了大块牛肉和楼下厨房里的旺火。话音一到唇边就微弱得听不清了，最后化为轻轻的叹息。寒冷的夜空里突然传来一声大叫：“上帝呀！”没有人移动位置，也没有人在意这叫声。一两个人将手伸到面前，重复打着莫名其妙的手势，但多数人纹丝不动。他们身体麻木，行动不便，各种思绪梦幻般袭来，生动形象，弄得他们十分疲惫。他们不时猛然发出一声惊叫，似乎在回答梦幻中怪异的招呼；然后又陷入沉默，仿佛在回忆熟人的面孔和熟悉的事情。他们记起了遗忘船友的容貌，听到了已故船长的声音。他们回想起了煤气灯照耀下街道的喧嚣，酒吧间蒸汽弥漫的闷热，以及风平浪静时海上似火的骄阳。 The icy south wind howled exultingly under the sombre splendour of the sky.The cold shook the men with a resistless violence as though it had tried to shake them to pieces.Short moans were swept unheard off the stiff lips.Some complained in mutters of “not feeling themselves below the waist;” while those who had closed their eyes, imagined they had a block of ice on their chests.Others, alarmed at not feeling any pain in their fingers, beat the deck feebly with their hands—obstinate and exhausted.Wamibo stared vacant and dreamy.The Scandinavians kept on a meaningless mutter through chattering teeth.The spare Scotchmen, with determined efforts, kept their lower jaws still.The West-country men lay big and stolid in an invulnerable surliness.A man yawned and swore in turns.Another breathed with a rattle in his throat.Two elderly hard-weather shellbacks, fast side by side, whispered dismally to one another about the landlady of a boarding-house in Sunderland, whom they both knew.They extolled her motherliness and her liberality; they tried to talk about the joint of beef and the big fire in the downstairs kitchen.The words dying faintly on their lips, ended in light sighs.A sudden voice cried into the cold night, “O Lord!” No one changed his position or took any notice of the cry.One or two passed, with a repeated and vague gesture, their hand over their faces, but most of them kept very still.In the benumbed immobility of their bodies they were excessively wearied by their thoughts, which rushed with the rapidity and vividness of dreams.Now and then, by an abrupt and startling exclamation, they answered the weird hail of some illusion; then, again, in silence contemplated the vision of known faces and familiar things.They recalled the aspect of forgotten shipmates and heard the voice of dead and gone skippers.They remembered the noise of gaslit streets, the steamy heat of tap-rooms or the scorching sunshine of calm days at sea.
贝克先生离开他那不安全的处所，沿着船尾楼爬一下，停一会儿。他在黑暗中爬行，仿佛某些食肉动物在尸堆里徘徊。在陡峭的边沿，他倚在一根柱子的上风处，向下查看主甲板。他发现轮船似乎还能再多坚持一会儿。风已经有所变缓，他想，但海浪仍然和以前一样涌得很高。波涛恶意地泛起白沫，下风一侧的甲板浸没在嘶嘶作响的白沫里，仿佛浸没在沸腾的牛奶中，绳索在深沉震颤的音调伴奏下不停地唱着，而轮船每次向上浮动时，风都会拉长声音，在桅桁中使劲吹。贝克先生静静地观察着。他旁边的一个人嘴唇颤动，发出啵啵的声响，那声音既突然又响亮，好像寒冷凶残地刺破了他的身体。那人的嘴不停抖动：“吧——吧——吧——啵——吧——吧。”“别响啦！”贝克先生在黑暗中一边摸索，一边嚷嚷道。“别响啦！”他摸到了一条腿，不住地晃了起来。“怎么了，先生？”贝尔法斯特叫道，那声音听起来像突然惊醒的样子，“我们正在这儿照料吉米。”“是吗？咳！那就别嚷嚷啦。挨着你的是谁？”“是我——水手长，先生，”西乡人大声答道，“我们要想法保住这个可怜虫的命。”　“哦，哦！”贝克先生说，“你们能安静点吗？”“他要我们把他抬到围栏上，”水手长忿恨地接着说道，“他说我们的夹克把他捂得透不过气。”“要是把他举起来，我们会把他掉到海里去的，”另外一个人说道，“我们的手已经冻得麻木了。”“我不管。我要窒息啦！”詹姆斯·韦特叫道，声音十分清晰。“哦，别这样，孩子，”水手长绝望地说，“这么好的晚上，我们走之前，你可千万别走。”“你会见到比这更糟的。”贝克先生兴奋地说。“这可不是小孩子闹着玩哩，先生！”水手长答道。“我们后面船尾部的几位可真够倒霉的。”　“要是这些该死的木杆早就砍倒的话，这艘船就会像其他漂亮的船一样正常行驶了，我们大家也都会得到好运。”有人叹着气说道。“老头子不允许……他真关心我们。”又一个人低声说道。“关心你们！”贝克先生气愤地嚷嚷道，“他为什么要关心你们？难道你们是一群需要照顾的女乘客吗？我们在这儿是照顾船的——可你们中的一些人连这一点也不明白。咳！……你们干了什么了不起的事值得被别人关心？咳！你们中有些人受点小罪就大吵大嚷的。”“得了吧，先生。我们没有这么差劲吧，”贝尔法斯特辩解道，因哆嗦声音有些颤抖，“我们不至于……啵……”“又来了，”大副一边说着，一边去抓那黑乎乎的人影，“又来了！……哎呀，你就穿件衬衫！你干什么啦？”“我把我的雨衣和夹克都盖在了这个半死的黑鬼身上了——可他说他要闷死。”贝尔法斯特抱怨道。“我要不是半死的话，你也不会叫我黑鬼，你这爱尔兰乞丐！”詹姆斯·韦特瓮声瓮气地说，显得精力十足。“你……啵……你就是身体再好，也不是白人……天气好的话……啵……我非揍你不可……啵……一只手反绑在背后……啵……?”“我才不稀罕你的破布——我需要空气。”另一位喘着气无力地说，仿佛气力突然衰竭了。浪花席卷而过，发出哗啦哗啦的声响。人们在平静的麻木状态中受到恼人的吵闹声惊扰，他们抱怨着，低声咒骂着。贝克先生向下风处爬去，那里隐隐约约呈现出一个大水桶，有个白色的东西紧紧挨着它。“是你吗，波德莫尔？”贝克先生问道。他不得不问了两次，厨子才轻轻地咳嗽着转过头。“是啊，先生。我心里在祈祷，想早点解脱；我已经准备好了，随时等候召唤……我——”“你瞧，厨子，”贝克先生打断厨子的话，“这些人都快被冻死了。”“冻死！”厨子凄惨地说道，“不久他们都会得到足够温暖的。”“什么？”贝克先生问道，眼睛顺着甲板注视着泛着泡沫的海水发出的微光。“他们是一群恶人，”厨子郑重地继续说道，但声音有些起伏，“像这个世界上任何一艘船上的水手一样坏。现在，我——”他哆嗦着，话都几乎讲不下去了；他呆在一个毫无遮蔽的地方，穿着一件布衬衣，一条薄裤子，双膝放在鼻子下，浑身颤抖，任凭咸水滴刺骨般的敲打；他的声音听起来十分疲惫——“现在。我——随时……我的大儿子，贝克先生……一个聪明的孩子……这次航行前在岸上的最后一个礼拜天，他不愿去教堂，先生。我说，‘你去向主忏悔，不去的话，我可知道是咋回事！’你猜他干了些什么？……池塘，贝克先生——穿着他最好的衣服掉进了池塘，先生！……意外？……‘什么也救不了你，尽管你是个好学生！’我说……真是意外！……我抽了他一顿，先生，直到打得我抬不起胳膊……”他的声音有些发抖。“我抽了他一顿！”他又说了一遍，牙齿咯吱直响；过了一会儿，他悲伤地叫了一声，这声音像是呻吟，又像是打鼾。贝克先生晃了晃他的肩膀。“喂！厨子！打起精神来，波德莫尔！告诉我——厨房水箱里还有淡水没有？轮船斜得没那么厉害了，我想；我得到前面去。有点水会对他们大有好处的。喂！小心！小心！”厨子挣扎着。“你可别去，先生——你可别去！”他开始向上风爬去。“厨房……我的事！”他大声说。“厨子要疯了。”大家异口同声说道。他嚷嚷道：“我疯了，是吗？对于死，我比你们谁都有思想准备，包括长官——绝对！只要船浮一天，我就坚持做一天饭！我给你们弄咖啡去。”“厨子，你是真正的男子汉！”贝尔法斯特叫道。可是厨子已经走过上风扶梯了。他又停了片刻，回过头来对着船尾楼大声喊道：——“只要船浮着，我就坚持给大家做饭！”喊罢就消失了，好像掉到海里去了。听到这话的人们欢呼着送他离去，那欢呼声听着就像病儿的哀号。一个多小时之后，有人肯定地说道：“他这一走就再也回不来啦。”“很有可能，”水手长附和道，“就是在好天，他在甲板上走路的姿态也不比一头初航的奶牛好多少。我们应该去看看。”　可谁也没动。黑暗中，时间慢慢地一小时一小时走过，贝克先生沿着船尾楼来来回回爬了好几次。有些人恍惚觉得听到了他与船长低声商量了些什么，但那时记忆远比实际的东西生动得多，因此他们不敢肯定那低语声是现在听到的还是多年前听到的。他们也不想弄明白。悄悄话多说点少说点无关紧要。奇寒的天气激不起好奇心甚至于希望。求生欲占据了他们的整个大脑，他们抽不出片刻空闲，也无心考虑其他事情。正是求生的欲望，才使他们在面对残忍顽固的狂风和严寒时，能够保持生气、刚毅和坚韧；此时，挂满星星的穹隆般黑色夜空在船上方缓慢旋转，船儿承载着他们的忍耐与痛苦，飘过了大海狂暴的寂寞。 Mr. Baker left his insecure place, and crawled, with stoppages, along the poop.In the dark and on all fours he resembled some carnivorous animal prowling amongst corpses.At the break, propped to windward of a stanchion, he looked down on the main deck.It seemed to him that the ship had a tendency to stand up a little more.The wind had eased a little, he thought, but the sea ran as high as ever.The waves foamed viciously, and the lee side of the deck disappeared under a hissing whiteness as of boiling milk, while the rigging sang steadily with a deep vibrating note, and, at every upward swing of the ship, the wind rushed with a long-drawn clamour amongst the spars.Mr. Baker watched very still.A man near him began to make a blabbing noise with his lips, all at once and very loud, as though the cold had broken brutally through him.He went on:—“Ba—ba—ba—brrr—brr—ba—ba.”—“Stop that!” cried Mr. Baker, groping in the dark.“Stop it!”He went on shaking the leg he found under his hand.—“What is it, sir?” called out Belfast, in the tone of a man awakened suddenly; “we are looking after that ’ere Jimmy.”—“Are you?Ough!Don’t make that row then.Who’s that near you?”—“It’s me—the boatswain, sir,” growled the West-country man; “we are trying to keep life in that poor devil.”—“Aye, aye!” said Mr. Baker.“Do it quietly, can’t you?”—“He wants us to hold him up above the rail,” went on the boatswain, with irritation, “says he can’t breathe here under our jackets.”—“If we lift ’im, we drop ’im overboard,” said another voice, “we can’t feel our hands with cold.”—“I don’t care.I am choking!” exclaimed James Wait in a clear tone.—“Oh, no, my son,” said the boatswain, desperately, “you don’t go till we all go on this fine night.”—“You will see yet many a worse,” said Mr. Baker, cheerfully.—“It’s no child’s play, sir!” answered the boatswain.“Some of us further aft, here, are in a pretty bad way.”—“If the blamed sticks had been cut out of her she would be running along on her bottom now like any decent ship, an’ giv’ us all a chance,” said some one, with a sigh.—“The old man wouldn’t have it...much he cares for us,” whispered another.—“Care for you!” exclaimed Mr. Baker, angrily.“Why should he care for you?Are you a lot of women passengers to be taken care of?We are here to take care of the ship—and some of you ain’t up to that.Ough!...What have you done so very smart to be taken care of?Ough!...Some of you can’t stand a bit of a breeze without crying over it.”—“Come, sorr.We ain’t so bad,” protested Belfast, in a voice shaken by shivers; “we ain’t...Brr...”—“Again,“ shouted the mate, grabbing at the shadowy form; “again!...Why, you’re in your shirt!What have you done?”—“I’ve put my oilskin and jacket over that half-dead nayggur—and he says he chokes,“ said Belfast, complainingly.—“You wouldn’t call me nigger if I wasn’t half dead, you Irish beggar!” boomed James Wait, vigorously.—“You...brrr...You wouldn’t be white if you were ever so well...I will fight you...brrrr...in fine weather...brrr...with one hand tied behind my back...Brrrrrr...”—“I don’t want your rags—I want air,” gasped out the other faintly, as if suddenly exhausted.The sprays swept over whistling and pattering.Men disturbed in their peaceful torpor by the pain of quarrelsome shouts, moaned, muttering curses.Mr. Baker crawled off a little way to leeward where a water-cask loomed up big, with something white against it.“Is it you, Podmore?” asked Mr. Baker. He had to repeat the question twice before the cook turned, coughing feebly.—“Yes, sir.I’ve been praying in my mind for a quick deliverance; for I am prepared for any call....I———”—“Look here, cook,” interrupted Mr. Baker, “the men are perishing with cold.”—“Cold!” said the cook, mournfully; “they will be warm enough before long.”—“What?” asked Mr. Baker, looking along the deck into the faint sheen of frothing water.—“They are a wicked lot,” continued the cook solemnly, but in an unsteady voice, “about as wicked as any ship’s company in this sinful world!Now, I”—he trembled so that he could hardly speak; his was an exposed place, and in a cotton shirt, a thin pair of trousers, and with his knees under his nose, he received, quaking, the flicks of stinging, salt drops; his voice sounded exhausted—“now.I—any time...My eldest youngster, Mr. Baker.. a clever boy...last Sunday on shore before this voyage he wouldn’t go to church, sir.Says I, ‘You go and clean yourself, or I’ll know the reason why!’What does he do?...Pond, Mr. Baker—fell into the pond in his best rig, sir!...Accident?...‘Nothing will save you, fine scholar though you are!’ says I....Accident!...I whopped him, sir, till I couldn’t lift my arm....” His voice faltered.“I whopped ’im!” he repeated, rattling his teeth; then, after a while, let out a mournful sound that was half a groan, half a snore.Mr. Baker shook him by the shoulders.“Hey! Cook!Hold up, Podmore!Tell me—is there any fresh water in the galley tank?The ship is lying along less, I think; I would try to get forward.A little water would do them good.Hallo!Look out!Look out!” The cook struggled.—“Not you, sir—not you!” He began to scramble to windward.“Galley!...my business!” he shouted.—“Cook’s going crazy now,” said several voices.He yelled:—“Crazy, am I?I am more ready to die than any of you, officers incloosive—there!As long as she swims I will cook!I will get you coffee.”—“Cook, ye are a gentleman!” cried Belfast.But the cook was already going over the weather-ladder.He stopped for a moment to shout back on the poop:—“As long as she swims I will cook!” and disappeared as though he had gone overboard.The men who had heard sent after him a cheer that sounded like a wail of sick children.An hour or more afterwards some one said distinctly: “He’s gone for good.”—“Very likely,” assented the boatswain; “even in fine weather he was as smart about the deck as a milch-cow on her first voyage.We ought to go and see.” Nobody moved.As the hours dragged slowly through the darkness Mr. Baker crawled back and forth along the poop several times.Some men fancied they had heard him exchange murmurs with the master, but at that time the memories were incomparably more vivid than anything actual, and they were not certain whether the murmurs were heard now or many years ago.They did not try to find out.A mutter more or less did not matter.It was too cold for curiosity, and almost for hope.They could not spare a moment or a thought from the great mental occupation of wishing to live.And the desire of life kept them alive, apathetic and enduring, under the cruel persistence of wind and cold; while the bestarred black dome of the sky revolved slowly above the ship, that drifted, bearing their patience and their suffering, through the stormy solitude of the sea.
他们彼此挤在一起，仍然觉着十分孤独。他们听到了连续不断的大声喧嚣，接着是长时间的沉默，再次忍受着生存的痛苦。夜里他们看到了阳光，感到了温暖，可是突然又大吃一惊，心想在这冰冷的世界里，太阳再也不会升起来了。有人听到了笑声，或者侧耳去听歌曲；也有人由于靠近船尾楼尽头，能够听到人的尖叫声，睁开眼睛，仍能听到，尽管那声音很弱很远，心中不由暗暗吃惊。水手长说道：“嗨，我想那是厨子在前面叫喊呢。”　他几乎不相信自己说的话，也认不出自己的声音了。过了好一阵，他旁边的人才有了一丝生命的迹象。他使劲地拍了一下旁边的人，说道：“厨子在叫呢！”许多人不明白怎么一回事，还有一些人根本不理会；更靠近船尾的人多半根本不相信。可是水手长和另一个人却壮着胆子向前爬去，想看个究竟。他们似乎离开了好几个小时，很快就被遗忘了。突然那些陷入绝望听天由命的人们，似乎产生了伤害别人的欲望。他们用拳头彼此乱打起来。黑暗中，只要是附近摸到的软东西，他们就不住地击打；他们激动地小声议论，可那声音比叫喊还大：“他们弄到了些热咖啡……水手长刚喝过……” “不可能!……在哪儿？”“马上就送来！厨子煮的。” 詹姆斯·韦特在呻吟。唐金则不怀好意地爬了过来，根本不理会踢在别人什么地方，唯恐长官们喝完。咖啡送来了一罐，大家轮流喝。滚烫滚烫的，说来让人难以相信，贪婪的嘴都被烧出了燎泡。人们松开咖啡罐时，叹息道：“他到底咋做成的？”　有人用微弱的声音叫道：“你真有能耐呀，大师傅！”不管怎样，他到底是把咖啡煮好了。此后，阿奇老说这事真是“奇迹”。多少天来，大家都想不明白，直到此次航程结束，它都是我们津津乐道的话题。天气转好的时候，我们问过厨子，他看到炉子“倒立”时心里是咋想的。在东北信风带航行期间，每到风平浪静的夜晚，我们都会问厨子，他是不是头朝下把一切给准备停当的。我们暗示他是不是把切面包板当木筏，在上面舒舒服服地烧着火炉；我们尽量用高超的讽刺智慧来掩盖对他的钦佩。他怎么也不肯承认有这样的事，嗔怪我们太轻佻，并庄重而不失活泼地宣称：为救助我们这些不洁的生命，他得到了上帝特别的眷顾。根本上来讲，他自然是对的；但大可不必得罪别人，对自己的功劳如此肯定——不必经常暗示我们，要不是他这位功勋卓著、纯洁无瑕的人在那儿接受了灵感和力量，去干这件慈悲的事情，我们的处境就会变得更加艰难。要是他凭着自己的鲁莽或者机敏救了我们，我们最终也会接受这一事实的；可要我们承认单对某个人的美德和圣洁表示感激的话，我们会像其他人群一样，觉得难以做到。像其他施恩者一样，厨子太把自己当回事了，结果得到的却是别人的不敬。然而我们也并非忘恩负义。他依然是英雄。他的名言——他生命的写照——广为传送，好像是征服者或者是圣人的话语。后来，不管什么时候，我们中要是有人被工作所困扰，别人建议他放弃的时候，他都会借用厨子的话表达自己坚持到胜利的决心：“只要船浮着，我就要做好每一顿饭！” Huddled close to one another, they fancied themselves utterly alone.They heard sustained loud noises, and again bore the pain of existence through long hours of profound silence.In the night they saw sunshine, felt warmth, and suddenly, with a start, thought that the sun would never rise upon a freezing world.Some heard laughter, listened to songs; others, near the end of the poop, could hear loud human shrieks, and opening their eyes, were surprised to hear them still, though very faint, and far away.The boatswain said:—“Why, it’s the cook, hailing from forward, I think.” He hardly believed his own words or recognised his own voice.It was a long time before the man next to him gave a sign of life.He punched hard his other neighbour and said:—“The cook’s shouting!” Many did not understand, others did not care; the majority further aft did not believe.But the boatswain and another man had the pluck to crawl away forward to see.They seemed to have been gone for hours, and were very soon forgotten.Then suddenly men who had been plunged in a hopeless resignation became as if possessed with a desire to hurt.They belaboured one another with fists.In the darkness they struck persistently anything soft they could feel near, and, with a greater effort than for a shout, whispered excitedly:—“They’ve got some hot coffee....Boss’en got it....” “No!...Where?”....“It’s coming!Cook made it.” James Wait moaned.Donkin scrambled viciously, caring not where he kicked, and anxious that the officers should have none of it.It came in a pot, and they drank in turns.It was hot, and while it blistered the greedy palates, it seemed incredible.The men sighed out parting with the mug:—“How ’as he done it?” Some cried weakly:—“Bully for you, doctor!”He had done it somehow.Afterwards Archie declared that the thing was “meeraculous.” For many days we wondered, and it was the one ever-interesting subject of conversation to the end of the voyage.We asked the cook, in fine weather, how he felt when he saw his stove “reared up on end.” We inquired, in the north-east trade and on serene evenings, whether he had to stand on his head to put things right somewhat.We suggested he had used his bread-board for a raft, and from there comfortably had stoked his grate; and we did our best to conceal our admiration under the wit of fine irony.He affirmed not to know anything about it, rebuked our levity, declared himself, with solemn animation, to have been the object of a special mercy for the saving of our unholy lives.Fundamentally he was right, no doubt; but he need not have been so offensively positive about it—he need not have hinted so often that it would have gone hard with us had he not been there, meritorious and pure, to receive the inspiration and the strength for the work of grace.Had we been saved by his recklessness or his agility, we could have at length become reconciled to the fact; but to admit our obligation to anybody’s virtue and holiness alone was as difficult for us as for any other handful of mankind.Like many benefactors of humanity, the cook took himself too seriously, and reaped the reward of irreverence.We were not un-ungrateful, however.He remained heroic.His saying——the— saying of his life—became proverbial in the mouth of men as are the sayings of conquerors or sages.Later, whenever one of us was puzzled by a task and advised to relinquish it, he would express his determination to persevere and to succeed by the words:—“As long as she swims I will cook!”
热咖啡帮助我们度过了黎明前数小时的寒冷时光。靠近海平面的低空呈现出了粉红和淡黄的美丽色彩，仿佛是稀有的贝壳内壁。稍高处，天空闪耀着珍珠般亮光的地方，出现一朵小小的乌云，仿佛是黑夜遗忘的碎片，镶嵌在耀眼的金光边缘。束束阳光在浪尖上跳跃。人们把目光转向东方。阳光沐浴着他们疲惫的面颊。他们完全被疲劳所征服，好像永远抛弃了自己的工作。辛格尔顿黑色的雨衣上，晒干的盐渍闪闪发光，像是下了一层白霜。他瞪着毫无生气的双眼，牢牢把握住舵轮。阿里斯顿船长面对初升的太阳，眼睛一眨不眨。他的双唇抽动了一下，这是二十四小时以来第一次张开，然后用洪亮而坚定的声音叫道：“把船头掉到下风！” The hot drink helped us through the bleak hours that precede the dawn.The sky low by the horizon took on the delicate tints of pink and yellow like the inside of a rare shell.And higher, where it glowed with a pearly sheen, a small black cloud appeared, like a forgotten fragment of the night set in a border of dazzling gold.The beams of light skipped on the crests of waves.The eyes of men turned to the eastward.The sunlight flooded their weary faces.They were giving themselves up to fatigue as though they had done for ever with their work.On Singleton’s black oilskin coat the dried salt glistened like hoar frost.He hung on by the wheel, with open and lifeless eyes.Captain Allistoun, unblinking, faced the rising sun.His lips stirred, opened for the first time in twenty-four hours, and with a fresh firm voice he cried, “Wear ship!”
刺耳的命令声犹如一声突然甩起的响鞭，使得这些麻木的人个个吃了一惊。但是接下来，他们仍躺在原处一动不动，习惯的力量使得其中几位用几乎听不到的声音把命令重复了几遍。船长阿里斯顿扫视了一下他的船员，只见有几位正用麻木的手指和慌乱的动作试图松开他们的身体。他不耐烦地重复道：“把船头掉到下风。喂，贝克先生，快让大家行动。他们都怎么啦？”“把船头掉到下风。你们那边听到了没有？——把船头掉到下风！”水手长突然雷鸣般地吼道。他的声音似乎打破了死一般沉闷的魔咒。大家开始挪动身体，接着便爬着干活去了。“我要你们把前桅支索帆赶快升起来，”船长大声命令道，“要是站着拉不动，那就躺着拉——执行吧。 The commanding sharp tones made all these torpid men start like a sudden flick of a whip.Then again, motionless where they lay, the force of habit made some of them repeat the order in hardly audible murmurs.Captain Allistoun glanced down at his crew, and several, with fumbling fingers and hopeless movements, tried to cast themselves adrift.He repeated impatiently, “Wear ship.Now then, Mr. Baker, get the men along.What’s the matter with them?”—“Wear ship.Do you hear there?—Wear ship!” thundered out the boatswain suddenly.His voice seemed to break through a deadly spell.Men began to stir and crawl.—“I want the fore-top-mast staysail run up smartly,” said the master, very loudly; “if you can’t manage it standing up you must do it lying down—that’s all.
快动手！”“来吧！咱们给这老姑娘一个机会。”水手长催促道。“嗨哟！嗨哟！掉到下风去！”震颤的声音一起呼喊着。这些水手们，带着满脸的不情愿，准备往前去。这时，贝克先生挤上前来，嘴里哼哼着爬着带路，大家跟着他越过船尾楼的陡峭边缘。其他人躺着没动，心里藏着一个卑鄙的愿望：别使唤他们爬来爬去，直到他们得救或者平静地淹死。 Bear a hand!”—“Come along!Let’s give the old girl a chance,” urged the boatswain.—“Aye!aye! Wear ship!” exclaimed quavering voices.The forecastle men, with reluctant faces, prepared to go forward.Mr. Baker pushed ahead, grunting, on all fours to show the way, and they followed him over the break.The others lay still with a vile hope in their hearts of not being required to move till they got saved or drowned in peace.
过了一会儿，只见他们出现在船头楼顶的前部，一个挨一个，晃晃悠悠的样子；紧紧抓住栏杆，从一个锚顶爬到另一个锚顶；有的抱住锚机的十字头，有的揽住前部的绞盘。他们手忙脚乱，动作怪异：挥着胳膊跪下，平身躺倒，晃着立起，似乎要设法跳下海去。突然，一张小小的白帆在他们中间摆动，逐渐变大，在风中飘舞。狭窄的帆顶一窜一跳地往上升——最后呈三角形完全展开在阳光下。“他们成功了！”船尾好几个人叫道。船长阿里斯顿放开绕在手腕上的绳子，伸头快步冲到下风处。只见他把下风的主操帆索从系索栓上解下，这时，回流的波浪打在他身上。“把主帆下桅横桁拉直！”他对我们喊叫——我们惊讶地望着他。我们有些迟疑，没有马上动手。“主桅下横桁转桁索，伙计们。拉呀！使劲拉！仰着身子拉！”他尖叫着，半个身子没在水中。我们根本不相信我们能够拉动主帆下桅横桁，可是那些力气最大和希望尚存的人在一心一意地执行着命令。其他人也三心二意地帮着忙。辛格尔顿再次紧握舵轮柄，眼睛突然变得炯炯有神。阿里斯顿船长艰难地来到上风处。“拉呀，伙计们！使劲拉起横桁！拉呀，帮船一把。”　他那刚毅的脸涨得通红，透着怒气。“她动弹了没有？辛格尔顿？”他喊道。“一点也没动，先生。”老水手用可怕的嘶哑声答道。“掌好舵，辛格尔顿。”船长气急败坏地说道。“拉呀，伙计们！你们难道还不如老鼠力气大？拉呀，干好你们的活。”克赖顿先生仰面躺着，一条腿肿着，脸色像纸一般煞白，他眨巴着眼睛，发青的嘴唇抽搐着。在疯狂的攀爬过程中，有人会扯住他的身体，爬过他那受伤的腿，跪在他的胸膛上。可他纹丝不动，紧咬牙关，不呻吟，不叹气。船长的激情、默默忍受着痛苦的汉子的叫喊激励着我们。我们悬在绳子上使劲拉。我们听到他对卑鄙地趴在那儿的唐金粗暴地训斥道：“如果你不抓紧转桁索，我就用系索栓砸烂你的头。”那位饱受人们欺凌的受气包怯生生地但又无耻地低声抱怨道：“你想谋害我们呀？”同时，猛然拼命地牢牢拽住绳索。人们叹着气，叫嚷着，呻吟着，嘴里叽里咕噜地说着毫无意义的话。帆桁转动了，慢慢地迎风吊平，桁臂周围风声呼叫。“动起来啦，先生，”辛格尔顿叫道，“船开始移动啦。”“把转桁索挽一下。挽一下！”船长叫道。克赖顿先生几乎窒息，不能动弹，他使出了全身力气，左手才勉强捏住了绳子。 After some time they could be seen forward appearing on the forecastle head, one by one in unsafe attitudes; hanging on to the rails, clambering over the anchors; embracing the cross-head of the windlass or hugging the fore-capstan.They were restless with strange exertions, waved their arms, knelt, lay flat down, staggered up, seemed to strive their hardest to go overboard.Suddenly a small white piece of canvas fluttered amongst them, grew larger, beating.Its narrow head rose in jerks—and at last it stood distended and triangular in the sunshine.—“They have done it!” cried the voices aft. Captain Allistoun let go the rope he had round his wrist and rolled to leeward headlong.He could be seen casting the lee main braces off the pins while the backwash of waves splashed over him.—“Square the main yard!” he shouted up to us—who stared at him in wonder.We hesitated to stir.“The main brace, men.Haul! haul anyhow!Lay on your backs and haul!” he screeched, half drowned down there.We did not believe we could move the main yard, but the strongest and the less discouraged tried to execute the order.Others assisted half-heartedly.Singleton’s eyes blazed suddenly as he took a fresh grip of the spokes.Captain Allistoun fought his way up to windward.—“Haul, men!Try to move it!Haul, and help the ship.”His hard face worked suffused and furious.“Is she going off, Singleton?” he cried.—“Not a move yet, sir,” croaked the old seaman in a horribly hoarse voice.—“Watch the helm, Singleton,” spluttered the master.“Haul, men!Have you no more strength than rats?Haul, and earn your salt.” Mr. Creigh-ton, on his back, with a swollen leg and a face as white as a piece of paper, blinked his eyes; his bluish lips twitched.In the wild scramble men grabbed at him, crawled over his hurt leg, knelt on his chest.He kept perfectly still, setting his teeth without a moan, without a sigh.The master’s ardour, the cries of that silent man inspired us.We hauled and hung in bunches on the rope.We heard him say with violence to Donkin, who sprawled abjectly on his stomach,—“I will brain you with this belaying pin if you don’t catch hold of the brace,” and that victim of men’s injustice, cowardly and cheeky, whimpered:—“Are you goin’ to murder us now?” while with sudden desperation he gripped the rope.Men sighed, shouted, hissed meaningless words, groaned.The yards moved, came slowly square against the wind, that hummed loudly on the yard-arms.—“Going off, sir,” shouted Singleton, “she’s just started.”—“Catch a turn with that brace.Catch a turn!” clamoured the master.Mr. Creighton, nearly suffocated and unable to move, made a mighty effort, and with his left hand managed to nip the rope.—
“都结紧啦！”有人叫道。他闭上眼睛，好像要昏厥过去，而我们挤在转桁索周围，面带恐惧，观察着轮船下一步的动静。 “All fast!” cried some one.He closed his eyes as if going off into a swoon, while huddled together about the brace we watched with scared looks what the ship would do now.
她缓慢地移动了，好像和她载着的人们一样疲劳、沮丧。她一点点转向下风，弄得我们气不敢出，几乎要窒息，她一转到正下风处，就开始前移，惊得我们的心脏突突直跳。她带着几乎要倾覆的样子，拖着没在水中的一侧开始加速行驶，看着这样的情景，真叫人恐惧。绳索上的三孔滑车搅动着破碎的浪花。甲板较低的一半到处是疯狂的漩涡；打转的泡沫仿佛雪原一般闪耀着白光，从中不时可以看到下风处长长的乌黑围栏。风在帆桁丛中尖声歌唱；她的每次倾斜，我们都以为她会从我们身后侧着身子滑向海底。面对死亡，她第一次明显地尝试着要保持直立，我们用微弱而杂乱的叫声去鼓励她。一个巨浪直扑船尾，卷曲的浪尖在我们头顶悬挂片刻；然后悄然落下，在船的两侧化成大片飞溅的泡沫。透过海浪凶猛的嘶嘶声，我们听到了辛格尔顿嘶哑的叫喊声：“她转舵了！”他把双脚牢牢地插进格子板里，回舵时，舵轮飞快旋转起来。“让左舷受风，稳住船！”船长叫道，他是我们这群躺着的人中第一个踉踉跄跄站起来的。一两个人兴奋地叫起来：“她浮起来啦！”远处的船头上，只见贝克先生和其他三人笔直地站着，在清澈天空的映衬下，显现出黝黑的身影，他们举着手臂，张着嘴，仿佛一直在齐声呐喊。轮船震颤着，试图把下沉的一侧升上来，可又倾斜着回到了原处，似乎不再挣扎，只得无力地浸在水中，可突然间意想不到的一次猛拉，一下子跃到了上风头，仿佛从死神的掌控中挣脱了出来。甲板掀起的大片海水全部洒向右舷。此时听到一阵巨大的炸裂声。铁质的舷窗口突然断开，发出雷鸣般的撞击声。海水倾覆在右舷的围栏上，宛如决堤的河水奔腾而下。甲板上的海浪和轮船四周的波涛混合在一起，咆哮声震耳欲聋。轮船在剧烈颠簸。我们爬起来，非常无助地东奔西突，从船的一侧跑到另一侧。人们不住地摔倒，狂叫道：“甲板室要冲跑了！”“她在自我清洁呢！”她被一个高高的海浪抬起，并跟着跑了一阵，受伤的两侧每个窗口都喷吐着粗粗的水流。下风处的转桁索不是被风刮跑了就是被水从栓钉上冲掉了，船的每次震动，前桅上所有沉重的帆桁便荡来荡去，速度之快，令人吃惊。只见前面，人们三三两两地蹲着，满怀恐惧地向上注视着头顶上不断摇摆的粗大桅桁。刮烂的帆和扯帆的绳头在风中飘舞，宛如一缕缕头发。在明媚的阳光下，轮船漫无目地行驶在亮光闪闪、汹涌澎湃、吼声如雷的波涛上，她披头散发，猛力向前，似乎在逃命；我们站在船尾楼上晃荡摇摆着，心烦意乱，喋喋不休。我们用微弱的声音抢着说话；个个面容像病人，姿态像疯子。一张张微笑着的脸十分消瘦，像是荡上了一层粉笔灰，而憔悴的大眼睛却闪着亮光。我们跺着脚，拍着手，觉着随时可以跳起来干点什么，而事实上站都站不稳。 She went off slowly as though she had been weary and disheartened like the men she carried.She paid off very gradually, making us hold our breath till we choked, and as soon as she had brought the wind abaft the beam she started to move, and fluttered our hearts.It was awful to see her, nearly overturned, begin to gather way and drag her submerged side through the water.The dead-eyes of the rigging churned the breaking seas.The lower half of the deck was full of mad whirlpools and eddies; and the long line of the lee rail could be seen showing black now and then in the swirls of a field of foam as dazzling and white as a field of snow.The wind sang shrilly amongst the spars; and at every slight lurch we expected her to slip to the bottom sideways from under our backs.When dead before it she made the first distinct attempt to stand up, and we encouraged her with a feeble and discordant howl.A great sea came running up aft and hung for a moment over us with a curling top; then crashed down under the counter and spread out on both sides into a great sheet of bursting froth.Above its fierce hiss we heard Singleton’s croak:—“She is steering!”He had both his feet now planted firmly on the grating, and the wheel spun fast as he eased the helm.—“Bring the wind on the port quarter and steady her!” called out the master, staggering to his feet, the first man up from amongst our prostrate heap.One or two screamed with excitement:—“She rises!” Far away forward, Mr. Baker and three others were seen erect and black on the clear sky, lifting their arms, and with open mouths as though they had been shouting all together.The ship trembled, trying to lift her side, lurched back, seemed to give up with a nerveless dip, and suddenly with an unexpected jerk swung violently to windward, as though she had torn herself out from a deadly grasp.The whole immense volume of water, lifted by her deck, was thrown bodily across to starboard.Loud cracks were heard.Iron ports breaking open thundered with ringing blows.The water topped over the starboard rail with the rush of a river falling over a dam.The sea on deck, and the seas on every side of her, mingled together in a deafening roar.She rolled violently.We got up and were helplessly run or flung about from side to side.Men, rolling over and over, yelled,—“The house will go!”—“She clears herself!” Lifted by a towering sea she ran along with it for a moment, spouting thick streams of water through every opening of her wounded sides.The lee braces having been carried away or washed off the pins, all the ponderous yards on the fore swung from side to side and with appalling rapidity at every roll.The men forward were seen crouching here and there with fearful glances upwards at the enormous spars that whirled about over their heads.The torn canvas and the ends of broken gear streamed in the wind like wisps of hair.Through the clear sunshine, over the flashing turmoil and uproar of the seas, the ship ran blindly, dishevelled and headlong, as if fleeing for her life; and on the poop we spun, we tottered about, distracted and noisy.We all spoke at once in a thin babble; we had the aspect of invalids and the gestures of maniacs.Eyes shone, large and haggard, in smiling, meagre faces that seemed to have been dusted over with powdered chalk.We stamped, clapped our hands, feeling ready to jump and do anything; but in reality hardly able to keep on our feet.
阿里斯顿船长一脸严肃，身体消瘦；他在船尾楼使劲给贝克先生打手势：“稳住前桅帆桁！尽可能稳住！”主甲板上的人为船长的话所鼓舞，溅着水花，到处乱跑，泡沫在他们的腰际打转。老辛格尔顿远离众人，独自一人呆在船尾，紧靠舵轮，悠闲地把白胡须收拢起来，压到他那亮晶晶的大衣最上面的纽扣下。船儿在喧嚣翻滚的波涛之上颠簸；辛格尔顿用他那沉稳的苍老的目光，注视着这条千疮百孔的航船披荆斩棘向前疾驶；他直挺挺地站着，全神贯注，似乎被大家遗忘了。在他直立的身体前面，只有两臂快速或者突然交叉移动，去制止或再次帮助旋转的轮辐快速转动。他小心谨慎地把着舵。 Captain Allistoun, hard and slim, gesticulated madly from the poop at Mr. Baker: “Steady these fore-yards!Steady them the best you can!” On the main deck, men excited by his cries, splashed, dashing aimlessly, here and there with the foam swirling up to their waists.Apart, far aft, and alone by the helm, old Singleton had deliberately tucked his white beard under the top button of his glistening coat.Swaying upon the din and tumult of the seas, with the whole battered length of the ship launched forward in a rolling rush before his steady old eyes, he stood rigidly still, forgotten by all, and with an attentive face.In front of his erect figure only the two arms moved crosswise with a swift and sudden readiness, to check or urge again the rapid stir of circling spokes.He steered with care.
第四章 CHAPTER FOUR
永恒的大海出于鄙夷的同情，暂缓了对人们的惩罚，按照它的公平原则，给了人们充分的特权，让其享受期盼已久的纷争。根据这一体现它慈悲的完美智慧，人们不被允许悠闲地体味生活复杂而辛辣的滋味。他们必须马上证明他们的生活没有辜负这永久的同情，这同情要求他们从日出到日落，从日落到日出去从事那些艰苦的劳动，永不歇息，直到那沉闷的日夜交替——那被祈求极乐幸福和虚幻天堂的圣人们吵闹得不得安宁的日夜交替——最终被那些普通、渺小和坚韧的人们痛苦和劳作的巨大沉寂所赎回，被他们无声的恐惧和勇气所赎回。 On men reprieved by its disdainful mercy, the immortal sea confers in its justice the full privilege of desired unrest.Through the perfect wisdom of its grace they are not permitted to meditate at ease upon the complicated and acrid savour of existence.They must without pause justify their life to the eternal pity that commands toil to be hard and unceasing, from sunrise to sunset, from sunset to sunrise; till the weary succession of nights and days tainted by the obstinate clamour of sages, demanding bliss and an empty heaven, is redeemed at last by the vast silence of pain and labour, by the dumb fear and the dumb courage of men obscure, forgetful, and enduring.
船长和贝克先生碰到了一起，彼此注视片刻，露出紧张和惊奇的神情，好像经过多年磨难之后不期而遇似的。他们的嗓子都不行了，他们彼此尽管使劲，也只能低声交谈。“有没有谁失踪？”船长阿里斯顿问道。“没有。都在这儿。”“有受伤的没有？”“只有二副。”“我马上去看他。我们还算幸运。”“是啊。”贝克先生轻声答道。他抓紧围栏，转动着充血的眼睛。这位头发灰白的小个子，一改沉闷低语的说话方式，使劲提高了嗓门，目光仿佛一把犀利的飞镖，冷冷地注视着大副。“把船上的帆张起来。”他威严地命令道，然后非常果断地闭上了他那薄薄的嘴唇。“你们尽快把帆张起来。现在正是顺风。马上，先生——别让那些人闲下来。他们会瘫倒下去，四肢僵硬，我们再也不能……我们现在必须让船动起来”……由于轮船长时间的剧烈震动，他走起路来趔趔趄趄；船的围栏没入到了闪闪发光，嘶嘶作响的海水中。他抓住了一根桅索，身体不由自主地与大副撞来撞去……“我们终于遇到顺风了——起帆。”他的头在两肩中间使劲晃悠。他的眼皮开始不停地打架。“还有抽水泵——抽水泵，贝克先生。”他凝视着对方，近在咫尺的脸好像在半英里之外。“让大家动起来——让船走起来。”他用昏昏欲睡的声音模糊地说着，好像马上要进入梦乡。他突然振作起来。“不要老站着。那可不行。”他说着，忍痛装出一丝微笑。他把手从横桅索上松开，被下沉的船带得向船尾滑去，他一路小碎步，直到被罗盘箱架挡住才停下来。他呆在那儿，抬起头，漫无目的地打量着辛格尔顿，辛格尔顿没有理会他，焦急地注视着船首斜帆桁的末端——“舵机运转正常吧？”他问道。这位老水手喉咙嗡嗡作响，仿佛话语在这儿互相碰撞后才能发出来。“开起来……就像一条小船。”他末了答道，声音有些沙哑，但充满了温柔，连一眼也没瞥船长——接着，他聚精会神地将舵轮往下旋转，稳定后，又转回原处。阿里斯顿船长悻悻地离开他舒舒服服靠着的罗盘箱，开始在船尾楼甲板上踱步，摇摇晃晃以便保持身体平衡…… The master and Mr. Baker coming face to face stared for a moment, with the intense and amazed looks of men meeting unexpectedly after years of trouble.Their voices were gone, and they whispered desperately at one another.—“Any one missing?” asked Captain Allistoun.—“No.All there.”—“Anybody hurt?”—“Only the second mate.”—“I will look after him directly.We’re lucky.”—“Very,” articulated Mr. Baker, faintly.He gripped the rail and rolled bloodshot eyes.The little grey man made an effort to raise his voice above a dull mutter, and fixed his chief mate with a cold gaze, piercing like a dart.—“Get sail on the ship,” he said, speaking authoritatively and with an inflexible snap of his thin lips.“Get sail on her as soon as you can.This is a fair wind.At once, sir—Don’t give the men time to feel themselves.They will get done up and stiff, and we will never...We must get her along now”...He reeled to a long heavy roll; the rail dipped into the glancing, hissing water.He caught a shroud, swung helplessly against the mate...“now we have a fair wind at last———Make———sail.”His head rolled from shoulder to shoulder.His eyelids began to beat rapidly.“And the pumps———pumps, Mr. Baker.” He peered as though the face within a foot of his eyes had been half a mile off.“Keep the men on the move to———to get her along,” he mumbled in a drowsy tone, like a man going off into a doze.He pulled himself together suddenly.“Mustn’t stand.Won’t do,” he said with a painful attempt at a smile.He let go his hold, and, propelled by the dip of the ship, ran aft unwillingly, with small steps, till he brought up against the binnacle stand.Hanging on there he looked up in an aimless manner at Singleton, who, unheeding him, watched anxiously the end of the jib-boom—“Steering gear works all right?” he asked.There was a noise in the old seaman’s throat, as though the words had been rattling together before they could come out.—“Steers...like a little boat,” he said, at last, with hoarse tenderness, without giving the master as much as half a glance—then, watchfully, spun the wheel down, steadied, flung it back again.Captain Allistoun tore himself away from the delight of leaning against the binnacle, and began to walk the poop, swaying and reeling to preserve his balance....
主桅根部的飞轮平稳地高速旋转，泵杆小幅度上下起伏，叮当作响，带着两组紧紧抓住拉手的、筋疲力尽的船员前后不停地猛烈摇摆。他们不顾自己的安危，臀部以上来回摇动，面部肌肉抽搐，两眼发直。木匠不时地测着水深，嘴里机械地惊叫道：“让船振作起来！让她走呀！”　贝克先生再也不能心平气和地讲话了，他发现自己在吼叫；在他训斥声的鞭策下，人们只得去照看索具，拖出新帆；他们心想，动都动不了啦，可还得把滑轮搬到高处——以便详细检查传动装置。他们哆哆嗦嗦，拼命地攀爬着绳子。手在不住变换着抓绳的部位，脚胡乱地踩在帆桁上，仿佛黑暗中的行人，人人感到头晕眼花；筋疲力尽时，便漫不经心地将身体靠在手边的绳子上。尽管摇摇欲坠，他们的心却没事似的无力地跳着；身下远处海浪的咆哮不绝于耳，但又隐隐约约，仿佛是来自另一世界的模糊声音：风吹得他们满眼流泪，剧烈时，甚至想把他们从摇摆不定的危险之处刮下去。只见他们脸上泪汗纵横，头发东倒西歪，在水天之间上下翻飞；他们有的骑跨在桁臂末端，有的蜷缩在踏脚索上，有的双腿夹紧吊索，腾出双手，还有人紧靠锁链，直起身来。他们的思想模糊地游弋于安息与生存的愿望之间，他们用僵直的手指解开帆顶耳索，笨手笨脚地摸找小刀，或者紧紧抓住绳索，抵御那不断拍打着的帆布的疯狂进攻。他们彼此凶狠地注视着，一只手打着疯狂的手势，另一只手把握住自己的性命，俯视着被淹没的一窄条甲板，顺风叫道：“往前递绳！”…… “把帆拉开！” ……“系紧！”他们的嘴唇鼓动着，眼睛睁得大大的，愤怒而急切，期盼别人明白自己的意思，但话还没等让人听见，就被风吹到沸腾的大海上去了。在难以忍受、无休无止的重压下，他们宛如被无情梦魇驱使着工作的人们，在冰与火的环境里做着苦工。他们一阵发烧，一阵哆嗦。他们的眼球酸痛，好像在大火中受过烟熏一样；每叫喊一声，头颅仿佛就要炸裂开来。似乎有坚硬的魔爪掐住了他们的喉咙。船的每次震动，他们都会想：这次肯定完了。它会把我们全掀到海里去的——他们横七竖八地挂在空中，疯狂地叫道：“小心——抓住绳头。”…… “把绳子穿好”…… “转动这个滑轮”……他们时而拼命地点着头；时而晃着愤怒的脸，“不！不！从下往上穿。”　他们彼此之间似乎恨得咬牙切齿。什么都不愿管的想法啮噬着他们的心，而把事情干好的愿望又像火一般灼烫着他们。他们诅咒着自己的命运，谴责着他们的非人生活，不惜耗费精力彼此咒骂。修帆工裸露着秃头，忙乱地干着活，忘掉了和许多海军上将的亲密友谊。水手长带了几把索针和几根粗捻的细索油麻绳往上爬，或者跪在帆桁上，准备顺便绕到船中部桅顶凸木上去，他脑海里不时闪现出生活在荒野村庄里的老婆和孩子的幻影。贝克先生感到身体十分虚弱，一歪一扭地随处走着，嘴里哼哼唧唧却又非常顽强，像铁人似的。他拦住那些刚从高处下来站着喘气的人们。他对他们或命令，或鼓励，或责备。“喂——现在该收拾主桅上桅帆了！拉紧那根桅顶吊索。别站在那儿晃来晃去！”“难道我们就不能歇一会儿吗？”几个人嘟囔道。他猛然转过身来，很是失望。“不行！活不干完不能歇。干到爬不起来为止。要你们来这儿来就是为了干活。”他肘旁一位弓着腰的海员噗嗤笑了一声。“要么干活，要么死。”他用嘶哑的声音痛苦地说着，然后，向手心里吐口唾沫，扬起他那两条长臂，抓住头顶上的绳子，发出一声哀号，让大家一起拉。一个大浪冲上了后甲板，驱赶着全体人员爬到下风去。帽子，绞盘棒都漂在了水中。透过发出哗哗响声的白色泡沫，可以看到紧握着的手，乱踢乱蹬着的腿，随处可见溅满白色水沫的脸。贝克先生和大家一起摔倒了，他尖叫道：“别松开绳子！抓紧！抓紧！”虽然受到绳子残忍地抽打，他们还是紧紧抓住了它，仿佛它是他们生命的一切。船儿剧烈震动着向前飞奔，高耸的波峰扫过船的左右两弦，峰顶白光闪闪。抽水泵工作完成了。转桁索穿过了滑轮。三面上桅帆和前桅帆张开了。船在水上快速行驶着，把奔腾的海浪甩在了后面。在她身后，远方传来海浪雷鸣般的可怕吼声——这声音引起的巨大震动弥漫在空气中。好像是为追求某种高尚事业的勇气所激励，在饱受蹂躏、折磨和创伤之后，她又劈波斩浪向北疾驶…… The pump-rods, clanking, stamped in short jumps while the fly-wheels turned smoothly, with great speed, at the foot of the mainmast, flinging back and forth with a regular impetuosity two limp clusters of men clinging to the handles.They abandoned themselves, swaying from the hip with twitching faces and stony eyes.The carpenter, sounding from time to time, exclaimed mechanically: “Shake her up!Keep her going!”Mr. Baker could not speak, but found his voice to shout; and under the goad of his objurgations, men looked to the lashings, dragged out new sails; and thinking themselves unable to move, carried heavy blocks aloft—overhauled the gear.They went up the rigging with faltering and desperate efforts.Their heads swam as they shifted their hold, stepped blindly on the yards like men in the dark; or trusted themselves to the first rope at hand with the negligence of exhausted strength.The narrow escapes from falls did not disturb the languid beat of their hearts; the roar of the seas seething far below them sounded continuous and faint like an indistinct noise from another world: the wind filled their eyes with tears, and with heavy gusts tried to push them off from where they swayed in insecure positions.With streaming faces and blowing hair they flew up and down between sky and water, bestriding the ends of yard-arms, crouching on foot-ropes, embracing lifts to have their hands free, or standing up against chain ties.Their thoughts floated vaguely between the desire of rest and the desire of life, while their stiffened fingers cast off head-earrings, fumbled for knives, or held with tenacious grip against the violent shocks of beating canvas.They glared savagely at one another, made frantic signs with one hand while they held their life in the other, looked down on the narrow strip of flooded deck, shouted along to leeward: “Light-to!”...“Haul out!”...“Make fast!” Their lips moved, their eyes started, furious and eager with the desire to be understood, but the wind tossed their words unheard upon the disturbed sea.In an unendurable and unending strain they worked like men driven by a merciless dream to toil in an atmosphere of ice or flame.They burnt and shivered in turns.Their eyeballs smarted as if in the smoke of a conflagration; their heads were ready to burst with every shout.Hard fingers seemed to grip their throats.At every roll they thought:Now I must let go.It will shake us all off—and thrown about aloft they cried wildly: “Look out there—catch the end.”...“Reeve clear”...“Turn this block....”They nodded desperately; shook infuriated faces, “No!No!From down up.” They seemed to hate one another with a deadly hate. The longing to be done with it all gnawed their breasts, and the wish to do things well was a burning pain.They cursed their fate, contemned their life, and wasted their breath in deadly imprecations upon one another.’ The sailmaker, with his bald head bared, worked feverishly, forgetting his intimacy with so many admirals.The boatswain, climbing up with marlinspikes and bunches of spunyarn rovings, or kneeling on the yard and ready to take a turn with the midship-stop, had acute and fleeting visions of his old woman and the youngsters in a moorland village.Mr. Baker, feeling very weak, tottered here and there, grunting and inflexible, like a man of iron.He waylaid those who, coming from aloft, stood gasping for breath.He ordered, encouraged, scolded.“Now then—to the main topsail now!Tally on to that gantline.Don’t stand about there!”—“Is there no rest for us?” muttered voices.He spun round fiercely, with a sinking heart.—“No!No rest till the work is done.Work till you drop.That’s what you’re here for.” A bowed seaman at his elbow gave a short laugh.—“Do or die,” he croaked bitterly, then spat into his broad palms, swung up his long arms, and grasping the rope high above his head sent out a mournful, wailing cry for a pull all together.A sea boarded the quarter-deck and sent the whole lot sprawling to leeward.Caps, handspikes floated.Clenched hands, kicking legs, with here and there a spluttering face, stuck out of the white hiss of foaming water.Mr. Baker, knocked down with the rest, screamed—“Don’t let go that rope!Hold on to it!Hold!” And sorely bruised by the brutal fling, they held on to it, as though it had been the fortune of their life.The ship ran, rolling heavily, and the topping crests glanced past port and starboard flashing their white heads.Pumps were freed.Braces were rove.The three topsails and foresail were set. She spurted faster over the water, outpacing the swift rush of waves.The menacing thunder of distanced seas rose behind her—filled the air with the tremendous vibrations of its voice.And devastated, battered, and wounded she drove foaming to the northward, as though inspired by the courage of a high endeavour....
前甲板水手舱成了一片潮湿的废墟。他们沮丧地望着自己的住处。里面又粘又滑，到处滴水；风吹过时发出空洞的叫声，乱七八糟的残骸随处可见，就像是岩石海滩边一个毫无遮蔽的洞穴，被半潮冲洗过一般。许多人失去了他们世间所有的一切，但右舷值班人员的箱子好歹保存了下来；不过，细细的水流不住地从中渗出。被褥湿透了；毛毯摊开着，被脚下踩扁的钉子挂住没能冲走。他们把一团团湿布从发着怪味的角落里拉出来，将水拧干，这才认出是谁的东西。有人呆呆地傻笑。有人环顾四周，茫然无语。有人对着破背心欢声大叫，有人对着破碎床板残片中找到的破烂东西，发出了痛苦的呻吟。有人发现一盏灯被卡在了斜桅下面。查利抱怨了一阵。诺尔斯转来转去，用鼻子闻闻，检查一下黑暗的角落还有什么没找到的东西。他把一只皮靴中的污水倒掉，关切地为它寻找失主。那些因损失而痛惜不已的人们，坐在舱口，双肘支在膝盖上，手掌托着下巴，抬头望望，露出满脸的鄙夷。他把那只皮靴拿到他们鼻子下面。“这只皮靴蛮不错。是你的吗？”他们怒吼道：“不是——滚。”　有个人厉声说道：“拿走，让它见鬼去吧。”他似乎有些吃惊。“怎么？这皮靴真不错。”可突然想到自己衣服上的衣缝全开了，便扔下靴子，开始诅咒起来。昏暗的灯光里，咒骂声响成了一片。这时，有个人走了进来，只见他垂着双臂，静静地站着，一进门槛，就重复着一句话：“讨厌的老毛病又犯了！讨厌的老毛病又犯了！”有几位急不可耐地一头扎进被水浸过的箱子里找烟草。他们气喘吁吁，低着头直嚷嚷。“看那！”……“哎！糟糕！这可是我上岸时穿的衣服，这下可彻底完了。”　有个人举着一条淌着水的裤子，满眼是泪，咒骂着。没有人理会他。船上的猫不知从哪儿钻了出来。他受到了热烈欢迎。他们争着抱它，轻声呼唤着它的名字，抚摸它。令他们纳闷的是，它是如何“躲过这场灾难的”，大家对此意见不一。于是，一场激烈的争论开始了。两个海员抬进了一桶淡水，大家纷纷围上前；但是汤姆喵喵叫着，根根毛发抖动着，抢先喝了一通。还有几个人到船尾去取油和饼干。 The forecastle was a place of damp desolation.They looked at their dwelling with dismay.It was slimy, dripping; it hummed hollow with the wind, and was strewn with shapeless wreckage like a half-tide cavern in a rocky and exposed coast.Many had lost all they had in the world, but most of the starboard watch had preserved their chests; thin streams of water trickled out of them, however.The beds were soaked; the blankets spread out and saved by some nail squashed under foot.They dragged wet rags from evil-smelling corners, and wringing the water out, recognised their property.Some smiled stiffly.Others looked round blank and mute.There were cries of joy over old waistcoats, and groans of sorrow over shapeless things found among the splinters of smashed bed boards.One lamp was discovered jammed under the bowsprit.Charley whimpered a little.Knowles stumped here and there, sniffing, examining dark places for salvage.He poured dirty water out of a boot, and was concerned to find the owner.Those who, overwhelmed by their losses, sat on the forepeak hatch, remained elbows on knees, and, with a fist against each cheek, disdained to look up.He pushed it under their noses.“Here’s a good boot.Yours?” They snarled, “No—get out.” One snapped at him, “Take it to hell out of this.” He seemed surprised.“Why? It’s a good boot,” but remembering suddenly that he had lost every stitch of his clothing, he dropped his find and began to swear.In the dim light cursing voices clashed.A man came in and, dropping his arms, stood still, repeating from the doorstep, “Here’s a bloomin’ old go!Here’s a bloomin’ old go!” A few rooted anxiously in flooded chests for tobacco.They breathed hard, clamoured with heads down.“Look at that Jack!”...“Here! Sam!Here’s my shore-going rig spoilt for ever.” One blasphemed tearfully, holding up a pair of dripping trousers.No one looked at him.The cat came out from somewhere.He had an ovation.They snatched him from hand to hand, caressed him in a murmur of pet names.They wondered where he had “weathered it out;” disputed about it.A squabbling argument began.Two men brought in a bucket of fresh water, and all crowded round it; but Tom, lean and mewing, came up with every hair astir and had the first drink.A couple of hands went aft for oil and biscuits.
接下来，在昏黄的灯光下，趁着拖擦甲板的空当，他们啃上几口硬面包，心里盘算着“无论多难也要努力挺过去。”　大家像好朋友一样，开始整理床铺。穿皮鞋和雨衣的顺序也排好了。他们用欢快的声音称彼此为“老家伙”和“乖儿子”。友好的互相拍打之声在空中回荡。人们大声地开着玩笑。有一两个人舒展四肢躺在湿漉漉的甲板上，头枕着弯曲的胳膊睡着了，还有几个坐在舱口盖上，抽着烟。透过薄薄青烟，可以看到他们疲惫的脸上洋溢着安详，他们的眼睛闪着亮光。水手长将头从门里探出来。“你们谁去跟舵手换换班”——他在里面叫道——“六点了。说真的，老辛格尔顿在那儿已经呆了三十多个小时了。你们都是好心人。”说罢，他又把门砰地一声关上了。“大副在甲板上值班。”有人提醒道。“喂，唐金，轮到你了！”三四个人一起叫道。他早爬进了一张空床，一动不动地躺在湿漉漉的床板上。“唐金，该你到舵位上值班啦。”他一声不吭。“唐金死了。”有人大笑起来。“把他那破烂衣服卖掉。”另一个人嚷嚷道。“唐金，你要不去掌那该死的舵，他们就卖你的衣服——你听见了没有？”第三个人嘲笑着说道。他在黑洞般的床上呻吟。他抱怨浑身骨头疼，说着可怜兮兮地呜咽起来。“他不会去的，”有人轻蔑地说道，“该你啦，戴维斯。”那年轻的海员忍着痛苦直起身来，耸了耸肩。唐金把头伸了出来，在昏黄的灯光下，显得异常虚弱、可怕。“我愿给你一磅烟草，”他带着哭腔安慰道，“只要我从船尾拿到手。我一定会——帮帮我……”戴维斯将胳膊向后一甩，人便不见了。“我去了，”他说，“但你得为此付钱。”他虽然行走不稳，但毅然决然地向门口走去。“我会的。”在他背后，唐金将头探了出来。“我会的——帮帮我……一英镑……三先令，它们值这么多。”戴维斯拉开了门。“等天气好转了……你得付我这些钱。”他回过头叫道。 “给你，威尔士人——拿去吧，你这贼！”“多谢啦！”黑暗中传来了他的叫喊声，这声音盖过了滚滚海水的嗖嗖声。可以听到他正甩去身上的海水；一个大浪重重地打在船上。“他这次可洗了个痛快澡。”一个尖刻的老水手说道。“哦，哦！”其他人哼哼唧唧附和着。经过长时间的沉默，沃米博发出了奇怪的声音。“喂，咋回事？”有人暴躁地问道。“他说他应该替戴维斯去。”老给芬兰人当翻译的阿奇解释道。“我相信他的话！”几个声音嚷嚷道……“没关系，外国佬……会轮到你的，糊涂蛋……马上轮到你去值班……现在舒舒服服的，倒是不知好歹。”　大家住了嘴，一齐把脸转向门口。辛格尔顿走了进来，往前跨了两步，颤巍巍地站住了。海水嘶嘶作响，咆哮着冲过船头，水手舱在颤动，充满了各种深沉的杂音；灯光闪耀，仿佛钟摆在不停地摇摆。他用梦幻般迷惑的眼神注视着周围，仿佛分辨不清静止的人和晃动的影子。不时有人惊恐地叫喊：“喂，喂”……“外面现在情况怎么样，辛格尔顿？”坐在舱口盖上的人们默默地抬起眼睛，船上年龄仅次于辛格尔顿的老海员（他俩虽然一天也说不上三句话，但彼此十分理解）注视了他的朋友片刻，把他那根短短的土烟斗从嘴里取出，啥话也没说，递了过去。辛格尔顿伸手去接，可没接住，踉踉跄跄，突然身体前倾，直直地摔倒在地，头部朝前，就像一棵连根拔起的大树。大家立刻涌上前去。众人你推我挤，嚷嚷道：“他不行啦！”……“把他的身体翻过来！” ……“站远一点！”一群人脸上带着惊慌神情俯视着他，他仰面朝天躺着，眼睛一眨不眨，样子十分难受。正当大家惊慌失措，屏息不语的时候，他用沙哑的声音说道：“我没事。”接着紧紧攥住自己的双手。他们扶他起来。他沮丧地嘟哝道：“我一天天老了……老了。”“你可一点不老。”贝尔法斯特非常机灵地随口答道。在众人搀扶下，他耷拉着头。“好点了吗？”他们问道。他睫毛下那双黑黑的大眼睛打量着大家，他那浓密的白胡子又长又厚直飘胸前。“老啦！老啦！”他坚定地重复着。在别人的一路搀扶下，他走到了自己的铺前。铺上是一堆滑腻的软东西，闻着就像泥泞的海滩上浅浅的死水发出的气味。这便是他那被水浸过的草床。他猛地使了一下劲，才倒在床上，在这黑暗狭窄的地方，只听他愤怒地嘟囔着，仿佛一头被激怒的野兽困在洞中，十分不安：“一点微风……小事……受不了啦……老啦！”他终于睡着了，脚上穿着皮靴，头上戴着防水帽，当他深深地叹息呻吟着翻身时，身上的油布雨衣沙沙作响。出于关心，人们小声地议论着他。“这回可能把他压垮了”……“过去一直像牛一样健壮”……“唉。现在他可比不得从前啦。”他们难过地谈论着，慢慢话题就岔开了。可是，半夜时分他又去值班了，好像什么事都没发生过；点到他的名字时，他凄楚地应道“在！”他比以前更常想心事，独自一人默不作声的样子令人费解，面部表情极度悲伤。多少年来，他经常听到别人叫他“老辛格尔顿”，也就心安理得地接受了这种称呼，认为这是对他的敬意。因为像他这样的人，半个世纪以来，一直与喜怒无常的大海打交道。可他从来没有为作为凡人的自己考虑过。他曾屈服于种种诱惑，经历过无数风暴，可身上未曾留下任何伤痕，好像是坚不可摧的。他在烈日下喘过气，在严寒中发过抖；忍受过饥渴放荡之苦；经历过无数磨难——早已明白各种狂风暴雨。老啦！在他看来，自己彻底垮了。就像一个熟睡中的人中了奸计，被人捆绑了起来，他醒时发现自己被蹉跎岁月的长链锁住了。他不得不立刻承担起自己生命的全部重担，但却发现担子太重，承担不了。老啦！他甩甩胳膊，摇摇头，晃晃四肢。一天天变老了……以后咋办呢？他头脑清醒了，望着永恒的大海，深深地认识到了它的无情威力；他发现大海不曾改变，在群星永久的注视下，黑沉沉的，泛着泡沫；他听见大海在用焦躁的声音召唤他，那声音来自于巨大的，充满不安、混乱和恐惧的无情世界。他向远方眺望，只见茫茫一片，无边无际，充满了痛苦与愚昧，悲伤与肆虐，这片大海夺去了他顽强生活过的所有日子，而当他生命终结之时，还要夺去他那奴隶般销蚀的躯壳…… Then in the yellow light and in the intervals of mopping the deck they crunched hard bread, arranging to “worry through somehow.” Men chummed as to beds.Turns were settled for wearing boots and having the use of oilskin coats.They called one another “old man” and “sonny” in cheery voices.Friendly slaps resounded.Jokes were shouted.One or two stretched on the wet deck, slept with heads pillowed on their bent arms, and several, sitting on the hatch, smoked.Their weary faces appeared through a thin blue haze, pacified and with sparkling eyes.The boatswain put his head through the door.“Relieve the wheel, one of you”—he shouted inside—“it’s six.Blamme if that old Singleton hasn’t been there more’n thirty hours.You are a fine lot.” He slammed the door again.“Mate’s watch on deck,” said some one.“Hey, Donkin, it’s your relief!” shouted three or four together.He had crawled into an empty bunk and on wet planks lay still.“Donkin, your wheel.” He made no sound.“Donkin’s dead,” guffawed some one, “Sell ’is bloomin’ clothes,” shouted another.“Donkin, if ye don’t go to the bloomin’ wheel they will sell your clothes—d’ye hear?” jeered a third.He groaned from his dark hole.He complained about pains in all his bones, he whimpered pitifully.“He won’t go,” exclaimed a contemptuous voice, “your turn, Davis.” The young seaman rose painfully, squaring his shoulders.Donkin stuck his head out, and it appeared in the yellow light, fragile and ghastly.“I will giv’ yer a pound of tobaccer,” he whined in a conciliating voice, “so soon as I draw it from aft.I will—s’elp me...” Davis swung his arm backhanded and the head vanished.“I’ll go,” he said, “but you will pay for it.” He walked unsteady but resolute to the door.“So I will,” yelped Donkin, popping out behind him.“So I will—s’elp me...a pound...three bob they chawrge.” Davis flung the door open.“You will pay my price...in fine weather,” he shouted over his shoulder.One of the men unbuttoned his wet coat rapidly, threw it at his head.“Here, Taffy—take that, you thief!” “Thank you!” he cried from the darkness above the swish of rolling water.He could be heard splashing; a sea came on board with a thump.“He’s got his bath already,” remarked a grim shellback.“Aye, aye!” grunted others.Then, after a long silence, Wamibo made strange noises.“Hallo, what’s up with you?” said some one grumpily.“He says he would have gone for Davy,” explained Archie, who was the Finn’s interpreter generally.“I believe him!“ cried voices....“Never mind, Dutchy...You’ll do, muddle-head....Your turn will come soon enough...You don’t know when ye’re well off.” They ceased, and all together turned their faces to the door.Singleton stepped in, advanced two paces, and stood swaying slightly.The sea hissed, flowed roaring past the bows, and the forecastle trembled, full of deep murmurs; the lamp flared, swinging like a pendulum.He looked with a dreamy and puzzled stare, as though he could not distinguish the still men from their restless shadows.There were awestruck exclamations:—“Hallo, hallo”...“How does it look outside now, Singleton?” Those who sat on the hatch lifted their eyes in silence, and the next oldest seaman in the ship (those two understood one another, though they hardly exchanged three words in a day) gazed up at his friend attentively for a moment, then taking a short clay pipe out of his mouth, offered it without a word.Singleton put out his arm towards it, missed, staggered, and suddenly fell forward, crashing down, stiff and headlong like an uprooted tree.There was a swift rush.Men pushed, crying:—“He’s done!”...“Turn him over!”...“Stand clear there!” Under a crowd of startled faces bending over him he lay on his back, staring upwards in a continuous and intolerable manner.In the breathless silence of a general consternation, he said in a grating murmur:—“I am all right,” and clutched with his hands.They helped him up.He mumbled despondently:—“I am getting old...old.”—“Not you,” cried Belfast, with ready tact.Supported on all sides, he hung his head.—“Are you better?”they asked.He glared at them from under his eyebrows with large black eyes, spreading over his chest the bushy whiteness of a beard long and thick.—“Old!old!” he repeated sternly.Helped along, he reached his bunk.There was in it a slimy soft heap of something that smelt, as does at dead low water a muddy foreshore.It was his soaked straw bed.With a convulsive effort he pitched himself on it, and in the darkness of the narrow place could be heard growling angrily, like an irritated and savage animal uneasy in its den:—“Bit of breeze...small thing...can’t stand up...old!”He slept at last, high-booted, sou’wester on head, and his oilskin clothes rustled, when with a deep sighing groan he turned over.Men conversed about him in quiet, concerned whispers.“This will break’im up”...“Strong as a horse”...“Aye. But he ain’t what he used to be.”In sad murmurs they gave him up.Yet at midnight he turned out to duty as if nothing had been the matter, and answered to his name with a mournful “Here!”He brooded alone more than ever, in an impenetrable silence and with a saddened face.For many years he had heard himself called “Old Singleton,” and had serenely accepted the qualification, taking it as a tribute of respect due to a man who through half a century had measured his strength against the favours and the rages of the sea.He had never given a thought to his mortal self.He lived unscathed, as though he had been indestructible, surrendering to all the temptations, weathering many gales.He had panted in sunshine, shivered in the cold; suffered hunger, thirst, debauch; passed through many trials—known all the furies.Old! It seemed to him he was broken at last.And like a man bound treacherously while he sleeps, he woke up fettered by the long chain of disregarded years.He had to take up at once the burden of all his existence, and found it almost too heavy for his strength.Old! He moved his arms, shook his head, felt his limbs.Getting old...and then?He looked upon the immortal sea with the awakened and groping perception of its heartless might; he saw it unchanged, black and foaming under the eternal scrutiny of the stars; he heard its impatient voice calling for him out of a pitiless vastness full of unrest, of turmoil, and of terror.He looked afar upon it, and he saw an immensity tormented and blind, moaning and furious, that claimed all the days of his tenacious life, and, when life was over, would claim the worn-out body of its slave....
这是最后一丝微风。风向快速转换，变成了强劲的东南风，顺势推着船儿向北航行，进入到令人欢欣、阳光灿烂的信风带。白色的船儿在蔚蓝的天空下，在风平浪静的碧蓝海面上，沿着笔直的航道向着老家方向快速行驶。她载着辛格尔顿完整的智慧、唐金脆弱的情感和我们所有人的自负与愚昧。无聊打闹的时刻被人们忘在了脑后；这些黑暗的瞬间带来的恐惧和痛苦，在这明媚宁静的日子里再也无人提起。此时此刻，我们似乎开始了新的生活，仿佛死而复生。就像前世留给人们的猜测一样，航行的前一阶段——好望角东侧印度洋里的航行——全部消失在了阴霾之中。这段航程已经结束——然后是一段空白时段：一段灰白色的模糊的时光——而我们再次获得了新生！辛格尔顿收获了残酷的真相；克赖顿先生伤了一条腿；厨子得到了好名声——他竟不知羞耻地滥用荣誉给他带来的种种机会。唐金平添了更多不满。不管到哪儿他都要重复那句话：“他说他要砸烂我的头——你们都听见了吧？他们现在为鸡毛蒜皮的小事就想要我们的命。”最终，我们也觉着这确实可怕。其实，我们也很自负！我们夸耀我们的勇气，工作能力和精力。我们念念不忘那些荣耀的往事：我们的忠诚，我们的坚强意志——以此为荣，好像它们是我们不靠外力、自觉努力的结果。我们牢牢记住我们的危险境遇，我们的艰辛劳作——并顺势忘记了我们有过的巨大恐惧。我们诽谤上司——他们什么事情都不干——却听信唐金信口雌黄。他关心我们的权利，他无私地维护我们的尊严，他从不因我们一贯对他进行言辞上的侮辱和神情上的蔑视而气馁。我们极度鄙视他——可又禁不住怀着极大兴趣听这位艺术家天衣无缝的编造。他告诉我们说我们都是好人——一群“令人讨厌的该受谴责的好人。”谁会感谢我们呢？谁会关注我们受到的不公待遇呢？难道我们不是为了一个月两磅十先令而过着“猪狗不如的生活”吗？　难道我们认为那点少得可怜的工钱就足以补偿我们，让我们去卖命，去丢掉衣服？“我们丢得连一片布都没有啦！”他嚷嚷道。他让我们忘掉了这一点：不管怎么说，他什么也没丢。年轻人们边听边想——唐金这小子真是精明人，虽然也不完全说得上。两个斯堪的纳维亚人对他的狂妄深感惊讶；沃米博不明白他的话；两位老水手若有所思地点点头，晃得他们毛茸茸、厚墩墩耳垂上的金色薄耳环闪闪发光。一副副晒黑的严肃面孔，托在饰有纹身的前臂上沉思着。青筋突出的褐色拳头紧握着黑黢黢的冒着青烟的白黏土烟斗。大家伸着背，曲着肩，一声不吭地静静听着，可咋也听不明白。他激情满怀，滔滔不绝，大家看不起他，却也无法反驳他。他那绘声绘色、猥亵不堪的饶舌，宛如一条流自毒源的泛滥溪流。他那珠子般的小眼睛跳来跳去，左顾右盼，提防着上司的出现。偶尔，贝克先生去查看船头的帆脚索，拖着笨重脚步路过时，大家会突然安静下来；或者，克赖顿先生一瘸一拐地经过，年轻光滑的脸显得比以前更加严厉；清澈的眼睛发出犀利的光芒，一眼就看透了我们短暂的沉默。克赖顿一走，唐金又开始偷偷地斜着眼睛使眼色：“又一个。那天你们几个把他捆得真够结实的。你们得到重谢了吧。他不是把你们赶得更紧了吗……？让他滚到海里去……为什么不呢？这样麻烦不就少多了。为什么不呢？”他秘密地提出建议，博得了大家的赞许；他时而低声细语，时而高声尖叫，挥舞着他那可怜的不比烟袋管粗的胳膊——伸着细细的脖子——喷着唾沫斜着眼。在他激情演讲的间歇，风在高处轻轻叹息，平静得无人注意的大海沿着船边窃窃私语，似乎在警告什么。我们憎恶这家伙，但又不可否认他论点中无可辩驳的事实。这些再清楚不过了。毋庸置疑，我们都是好人；我们功劳显著，而报酬却微乎其微。我们拼着命拯救了这艘船，而船长却得到了全部荣誉。他到底干了些什么？我们倒想请教。唐金问道：“没有我们他能干什么？”谁也无法回答。我们一直受着世间不公正待遇的压迫；在这种负担下这么久竟浑然不知自己的悲惨境遇，这着实令我们吃惊；我们惴惴不安地怀疑自己是否愚蠢到不辨是非的地步，这又给我们平添了一层烦恼。唐金笃定地告诉我们这都是因为我们“心眼太好”，但我们并不能从他这种浅薄的诡辩中得到丝毫安慰。我们这些人有足够的勇气承认我们在智力上存在缺陷。打那时起，我们就不再去踢他，拧他的鼻子，或者有意无意撞他。在经历了好望角风暴之后，最后那项成了大家最喜欢的消遣。戴维斯不再说黑眼珠和塌鼻子这类令他不快的话。风暴过后，查利也收敛许多，不再讥讽他。诺尔斯恭恭敬敬，神情诡谲地提出一些问题，比如：“我们大家能否和大副二副他们吃得一样好？拿不到工钱，我们能不下海吗？要是我们拿到了，下一步该干什么？”对于这些问题，他显得不屑一顾，回答得从容肯定；他志得意满，踱来踱去；他穿的衣服太大，好像故意要显摆自己。这些衣服多半是吉米的——虽然他愿意接受任何人的东西，可除了吉米，谁都没有多余的。他对吉米无限忠诚。他老是躲在那间小屋里照料吉米的起居，迎合他的怪念头，顺从他吹毛求疵的坏脾气，陪他一块儿大笑。谁也阻止不了他探访病人的虔诚行为，尤其是甲板上有繁重的拖拉搬运之类的工作时。贝克先生有两次抓着他的领子把他从那儿使劲揪了出来，激起了我们内心极大的不满。怎么能让一个病人留在那儿无人照顾呢？怎么能虐待一个照看船友的人？“什么？”贝克先生听到这些牢骚后，转过身，恶狠狠地吼道，结果，围着的半圈人像是一个人似的一起向后退了一步。“张中桅辅助帆。爬到高处去，唐金，仔细检查一下滑轮，”大副果断命令道，“把帆拉过来，将落帆索盘好。搭把手。” 等帆张好后，他慢步来到船尾，站在那儿凝视了罗盘好一阵子，只见他疲惫不堪，神情忧郁，呼吸急促，仿佛那弥漫于船上莫名其妙的敌意让他窒息。“他们中间出了什么事？”他想，“真不明白他们干吗闪烁其词，嘀嘀咕咕。这年头，这伙人的表现已经不错了。”　唐金发完牢骚之后过了很久，大家还是会对一些不公而又难以挽回的蠢事——这些事不可否认——咒骂几句，小声议论议论。我们这个小小的世界，载着一个充满不满和抱负的群体向前驶去，道路曲折，但始终不偏离航向。他们无休止地认真分析着自己不被赏识的价值，从中找到了一丝黯淡的慰藉；他们为唐金那充满希望的说辞所鼓舞，狂热地梦想着自己的未来，到时候，航行在宁静大海上的每艘孤独的船，都由他们这些腰缠万贯、吃饱喝足、踌躇满志的船长们驾驶。 This was the last of the breeze.It veered quickly, changed to a black south-easter, and blew itself out, giving the ship a famous shove to the northward into the joyous sunshine of the trade.Rapid and
