“你们就不能多干点吗？不能，这我知道，可我什么也没说过。你们可不要再没事找事啦，不然的话，我可对大家不客气。我候着你们呢！别胡闹了！”他向人群挥着一个指头。“至于那个人，”他把嗓门提得高高的，“至于那个人，要是没有我的同意，他胆敢把鼻子往外面甲板上伸一伸，我就马上给他戴上镣铐。瞧着吧！”厨子听到他在前面讲话，连忙从厨房跑了出来，只见他举着双手，惊恐万分，难以相信这是真的，着实吃了一惊，于是赶紧又跑了回去。人群中出现了片刻寂静，这时，一个罗圈腿海员走到一旁，很有礼貌地将痰吐到排水口里去。“还有一件事。”船长平静地说。他飞快向前跨了一大步，身子一扭，从兜里掏出一个系索栓。“这个！”他的举动如此意外和突然，人群不由向后退去。他死死地盯着他们的脸，有几位脸上立马现出吃惊的神色，好像他们以前从未见过这东西。他把系索栓举起来。“这是我的事。我不打算问你们什么问题，可你们心里都明白；现在这东西必须从哪儿来回哪儿去。”他的双眼怒火直冒。人群中出现了一阵骚动。他们把头扭向一边，不看那铁块，觉着惭愧、尴尬、震惊，好像那东西恐怖、可耻或者不雅；为照顾一般面子，也不应该大白天拿在手里那样挥舞。船长仔细地打量着他们。“唐金。”他大声叫道，声音短促、威严。 “You can do no more?No, I know, and say nothing.But you stop your caper or I will stop it for you.I am ready for you!Stop it!”He shook a finger at the crowd.“As to that man,” he raised his voice very much; “as to that man, if he puts his nose out on deck without my leave I will clap him in irons.There!” The cook heard him forward, ran out of the galley lifting his arms, horrified, unbelieving, amazed, and ran in again.There was a moment of profound silence during which a bow-legged seaman, stepping aside, expectorated decorously into the scupper.“There is another thing,” said the master, calmly.He made a quick stride and with a swing took an iron belaying-pin out of his pocket.“This!” His movement was so unexpected and sudden that the crowd stepped back.He gazed fixedly at their faces, and some at once put on a surprised air as though they had never seen a belay-ing-pin before.He held it up. “This is my affair.I don’t ask you any questions, but you all know it; it has got to go where it came from.”His eyes became angry.The crowd stirred uneasily.They looked away from the piece of iron, they appeared shy, they were embarrassed and shocked as though it had been something horrid, scandalous, or indelicate, that in common decency should not have been flourished like this in broad daylight.The master watched them attentively.“Donkin,” he called out in a short, sharp tone.
唐金在人们身后东躲西藏，可他们回头一看见他，便自动闪到一边去。这样，队伍不断在他前面分开，在他后面又合起来，直到最后他独自一人站在船长面前，仿佛刚从甲板上钻出来似的。船长阿里斯顿朝他走过去。他们俩身材极为相似，在近距离内，船长与那双珠子般的眼睛交换了一下仇视的目光。他们都有点站不稳。“你认得这东西吧？”船长问道。“不，不认识。”对方厚着脸皮惊恐地答道。“你是个胆小鬼。拿着。”船长命令道。唐金的双臂似乎粘到了大腿上；他立着不动，双眼注视前方，仿佛在接受检阅。“拿着。”船长重复了一遍，逼近他；他们彼此能感受到对方的呼吸。“拿着。”阿里斯顿船长又说一遍，打了一个威胁的手势。唐金勉强把一只胳膊从身体一侧抬起。“你为什么这样对我？”他使劲嘟囔着，仿佛口中塞满了面团。“要是你不拿……”船长接着说。唐金一把抓住铁栓，看样子想拿着逃掉，可偏偏像树桩一样站着没动，仿佛手里拿着一支蜡烛。“你从哪里弄来的，还把它放回原处去。”船长阿里斯顿嘴上说着，眼睛狠狠地瞪着他。唐金退后一步，睁大双眼。“滚，你这无赖，不然，看我怎么叫你滚。”船主吼道，威胁着跨上一步，逼着他慢慢后退。他躲闪着，试图用那铁块护住头，避开逼近的拳头。贝克先生这一会儿不再哼哼。“好啊！我的天。”克赖顿先生低声赞许，俨然是鉴赏家的口吻。“别碰我。”唐金惊叫着向后退。“那就滚。快点滚。”“你别打我……我非把你拉到法官跟前……我要告发你。”阿里斯顿船长大跨一步，唐金便马上转身向前跑了几步，停下来，往回看，露出满嘴黄牙。“再往前，前部索具。”船长催促道，用手臂指了指。“你们都站在那儿看我受欺负吗？”唐金向一群默默看热闹的人尖叫。阿里斯顿船长健步朝他走去。唐金纵身向前一跃，直奔前部索具，使劲将铁栓塞入孔中。“我迟早会找你算账的。”他对着整个轮船尖叫，然后，走过前桅便消失了。阿里斯顿船长转过身来，表情自若地走回船尾，似乎忘了刚才的景象。人们给他让开了一条路。他谁也没有看一眼。“这就行啦，贝克先生。派值班员到下面去。”他镇定地命令道。“还有你们这些人，从今以后一定要规规矩矩的。”他用平静的声音补充道。这些怀有深刻印象的人们正在离去，他望着他们的背影，陷入了一阵沉思。“上早点，乘务员。”他冲着舱门叫道，语气十分轻松。“我真不愿意看到你——咳！——亲自把铁栓交给那家伙，先生，”贝克先生说，“他会用它砸——咳！——砸你的头，就像砸蛋壳一样。”“噢！他！”船长漫不经心地低声说。“一群怪物，”他继续低声说，“我想现在该不会有什么问题啦。可这也难说，这年头，同这么一群……多年前；那时，我是年轻船长——一次去中国航行，我遇到了一次暴动；一次真正的暴动，贝克。跟现在这班人不同。我知道他们想要什么：他们想把货箱钻开取酒喝。很简单……我们敲打他们两天，等他们挨够了——就会变得跟绵羊一样温顺。真是一班好水手。真是一次愉快的航行。”他往空中望了望，帆桁拉得紧紧的。“天天都是逆风，”他痛苦地叹着气，“难道这次航行就遇不上顺风啦？”“早点来了，先生。”乘务员叫道，像变魔术一般出现在他们面前，手里还拿着一块旧餐巾。“啊！好了。过来，贝克先生——时间不早了——说了这么多废话。” Donkin dodged behind one, then behind another, but they looked over their shoulders and moved aside.The ranks kept on opening before him, closing behind, till at last he appeared alone before the master as though he had come up through the deck.Captain Allistoun moved close to him.They were much of a size, and at short range the master exchanged a deadly glance with the beady eyes.They wavered.—“You know this?” asked the master.—“No, I don’t,” answered the other, with cheeky trepidation.—“You are a cur.Take it,” ordered the master.Donkin’s arms seemed glued to his thighs; he stood, eyes front, as if drawn on parade.“Take it,” repeated the master, and stepped closer; they breathed on one another.“Take it,” said Captain Allistoun again, making a menacing gesture.Donkin tore away one arm from his side.—“Vy are yer down on me?” he mumbled with effort and as if his mouth had been full of dough.—“If you don’t...” began the master.Donkin snatched at the pin as though his intention had been to run away with it, and remained stock still holding it like a candle.“Put it back where you took it from,” said Captain Allistoun, looking at him fiercely.Donkin stepped back opening wide eyes.“Go, you blackguard, or I will make you,” cried the master, driving him slowly backwards by a menacing advance.He dodged, and with the dangerous iron tried to guard his head from a threatening fist.Mr. Baker ceased grunting for a moment.—“Good!By Jove,” murmured appreciatively Mr. Creighton in the tone of a connoisseur.—“Don’t tech me,” snarled Donkin, backing away.—“Then go. Go faster.”—“Don’t yer ‘it me....I will pull yer up afore the magistryt....I’ll show yer up.” Captain Allistoun made a long stride, and Donkin, turning his back fairly, ran off a little, then stopped and over his shoulder showed yellow teeth.—“Further on, fore-rigging,” urged the master, pointing with his arm.—“Are yer goin’ to stand by and see me bullied?” screamed Donkin at the silent crowd that watched him.Captain Allistoun walked at him smartly.He started off again with a leap, dashed at the fore-rigging, rammed the pin into its hole violently.“I’ll be even with yer yet,” he screamed at the ship at large and vanished beyond the foremast.Captain Allistoun spun round and walked back aft with a composed face, as though he had already forgotten the scene.Men moved out of his way.He looked at no one.—“That will do, Mr. Baker.Send the watch below,” he said, quietly.“And you men try to walk straight for the future,” he added in a calm voice.He looked pensively for a while at the backs of the impressed and retreating crowd.“Breakfast, steward,” he called in a tone of relief through the cabin door.—“I didn’t like to see you—Ough!—give that pin to that chap, sir,” observed Mr. Baker; “he could have bust—Ough!—bust your head like an eggshell with it.”—“O! he!” muttered the master, absently.“Queer lot,” he went on in a low voice，“I suppose it’s all right now.Can never tell tho‘ nowadays, with such a...Years ago; I was a young master then—one China voyage I had a mutiny; real mutiny, Baker.Different men tho’.I knew what they wanted: they wanted to broach the cargo and get at the liquor.Very simple....We knocked them about for two days, and when they had enough—gentle as lambs.Good crew.And a smart trip I made.” He glanced aloft at the yards braced sharp up.“Head wind day after day,” he exclaimed, bitterly.“Shall we never get a decent slant this passage?”—“Ready, sir,” said the steward, appearing before them as if by magic and with a stained napkin in his hand.—“Ah!All right.Come along, Mr. Baker—it’s late—with all this nonsense.”
第五章 CHAPTER FIVE
阴沉寂静的氛围笼罩着轮船，令人压抑。下午人们忙着洗衣服，洗完后挂在微风中晾干，人人都像初醒的哲学家，沉思冥想，无精打采。很少听到有人说话。对于范围狭窄的人类语言来说，生命问题似乎过于浩瀚，于是人们普遍认为，自开天辟地以来，这个问题就被抛给了无垠的大海，大海早已把它纳入了自己巨大的掌控之中；大海无所不知，迟早要准确无误地揭开朦胧的面纱，让每个人看到隐藏在一切谬误中的智慧，潜伏在疑问中的真相，以及跨越悲伤与恐惧边界的安全与和平的领域。混杂在一起的各种胡思乱想，在人们体内不停地四处横流，这时，吉米浮出了水面，十分惹人注意，仿佛一个系在污浊河底的黑色浮标。虚伪取得了胜利。它的获胜凭借的是怀疑、愚昧、怜悯和感伤。出于同情、轻率和取乐，我们主动支持了他的这种虚伪。吉米在不可改变的事实面前表现出的不屈不挠的虚伪态度，简直是惊天之谜——宏大、费解，有时甚至能够引起人们的好奇与敬畏；同时，在许多人看来，对他这种极致的虚伪打趣逗乐也妙不可言。人们为了避免看他死去带来的痛苦，对这个遭受病痛折磨的人更加温柔体贴，其实，这是潜在的利己主义。我们眼睁睁地看着他的大限这一惟一事实一天天临近，可他却极为倔强，偏不承认，这正如大自然某条规律失灵一样，着实令人不安。他在自己的事情上错得这么严重，人们不禁怀疑他是否有超自然的本能。他甚至荒谬到认为自己受了神的启示。他是那样的独一无二，只有超人的事物才能像他一样让人神魂颠倒；他似乎已经超越了那可怕的边界，在另一边疾呼他的反对意见。他正在脱离形骸，变得幽灵一般；他的颧骨高高凸起，额头更加瘦削；面部肌肉凹陷，形成块块阴影；消瘦的脑袋酷似地下挖出的黑骷髅，眼眶中装着两颗不停打转的银灰色眼球。他令人泄气。有了他，我们变得更加慈悲、温和、圆滑，而且也极度消沉：我们懂得了他恐惧的微妙，理解了他所有的厌恶、畏缩、逃避和虚幻——仿佛我们过于世故、堕落，丝毫不懂生活的意义。我们的神态像是被传授了一些龌龊的秘密；我们扮着同谋者之间寓意深刻的怪相，交换着意味深长的眼神和简短而重要的话语。我们卑鄙得无法言表，可内心却乐不可支。我们使用甜言蜜语，郑重其事、感情真挚地对他撒谎，仿佛运用这种道德欺诈手段，是为了获取永恒报酬似的。对他的狂言，我们齐声附和，好像他是百万富翁、政客，或者改革者——而我们是一群野心勃勃的笨蛋。有时我们壮着胆子对他的话语提出质疑，可我们总是摆出阿谀奉承之人的谄媚姿态，结果，这种异议却成了奉承，反而进一步增加了他的荣耀。他左右着我们这个世界的道德观，仿佛他掌控着给我们分配荣誉、财富或痛苦的权力；事实上，他除了蔑视，什么也不曾给过我们。他的蔑视无边无际；我们眼看着他的身体一天天萎缩，而这种蔑视似乎却在逐渐壮大。这是关于他——也是属于他——的惟一能给人留下持久与活力印象的东西。它扎根于他内心深处，具有顽强的生命。它通过那永远撅起的黑唇表达自我；它通过那呆滞硕大的眼睛流露的无礼悲凄注地视着我们。我们专心致志地守护着他。他似乎不愿活动，好像怀疑自己身体的健康。极其轻微的动作也能表现出他身体的虚弱（当然他也绝不会做出其他的动作），由此引起一阵精神痛苦。他对自己的举动非常小心。他躺在床上，四肢打开，下巴贴在毛毯上，一动不动，显得诡秘、谨慎。只有他的目光在人们的面部游弋：鄙夷、锐利、忧伤。 A heavy atmosphere of oppressive quietude pervaded the ship.In the afternoon men went about washing clothes and hanging them out to dry in the unprosperous breeze with the meditative languor of disenchanted philosophers.Very little was said.The problem of life seemed too voluminous for the narrow limits of human speech, and by common consent it was abandoned to the great sea that had from the beginning enfolded it in its immense grip; to the sea that knew all, and would in time infallibly unveil to each the wisdom hidden in all the errors, the certitude that lurks in doubts, the realm of safety and peace beyond the frontiers of sorrow and fear.And in the confused current of impotent thoughts that set unceasingly this way and that through bodies of men, Jimmy bobbed up upon the surface, compelling attention, like a black buoy chained to the bottom of a muddy stream.Falsehood triumphed.It triumphed through doubt, through stupidity, through pity, through sentimentalism.We set ourselves to bolster it up from compassion, from recklessness, from a sense of fun. Jimmy’s steadfastness to his untruthful attitude in the face of the inevitable truth had the proportions of a colossal enigma—of a manifestation grand and incomprehensible that at times inspired a wondering awe; and there was also, to many, something exquisitely droll in fooling him thus to the top of his bent.The latent egoism of tenderness to suffering appeared in the developing anxiety not to see him die. His obstinate non-recognition of the only certitude whose approach we could watch from day to day was as disquieting as the failure of some law of nature.He was so utterly wrong about himself that one could not but suspect him of having access to some source of supernatural knowledge.He was absurd to the point of inspiration.He was unique, and as fascinating as only something inhuman could be; he seemed to shout his denials already from beyond the awful border.He was becoming immaterial like an apparition; his cheekbones rose, the forehead slanted more; the face was all hollows, patches of shade; and the fleshless head resembled a disinterred black skull, fitted with two restless globes of silver in the sockets of eyes.He was demoralising.Through him we were becoming highly humanised, tender, complex, excessively decadent: we understood the subtlety of his fear, sympathised with all his repulsions, shrinkings, evasions, delusions—as though we had been over-civilised, and rotten, and without any knowledge of the meaning of life.We had the air of being initiated in some infamous mysteries; we had the profound grimaces of conspirators, exchanged meaning glances, significant short words.We were inexpressibly vile and very much pleased with ourselves.We lied to him with gravity, with emotion, with unction, as if performing some moral trick with a view to an eternal reward.We made a chorus of affirmation to his wildest assertions, as though he had been a millionaire, a politician, or a reformer—and we a crowd of ambitious lubbers.When we ventured to question his statements we did it after the manner of obsequious sycophants, to the end that his glory should be augmented by the flattery of our dissent.He influenced the moral tone of our world as though he had it in his power to distribute honours, treasures, or pain; and he could give us nothing but his contempt.It was immense; it seemed to grow gradually larger, as his body day by day shrank a little more, while we looked.It was the only thing about him—of him—that gave the impression of durability and vigour.It lived within him with an unquenchable life.It spoke through the eternal pout of his black lips; it looked at us through the impertinent mournfulness of his languid and enormous stare.We watched him intently.He seemed unwilling to move, as if distrustful of his own solidity.The slightest gesture must have disclosed to him (it could not surely be otherwise) his bodily weakness, and caused a pang of mental suffering.He was chary of movements.He lay stretched out, chin on blanket, in a kind of sly, cautious immobility.Only his eyes roamed over faces: his eyes disdainful, penetrating and sad.
就是在这个时候，贝尔法斯特的忠诚——还有他的好斗——赢得了普遍尊重。他把所有的空闲时间都花在了吉米的小屋内。他照顾吉米，和吉米聊天；像女人一样温柔，像老慈善家一样体贴热情，像模范奴隶主一样，从感情上关心他的黑奴。可一到舱外，他就变得十分暴躁，像火药一样一触即炸，他阴沉忧郁，疑神疑鬼，悲伤之时比其他任何时候都更加凶残。他有的只是眼泪和拳头：眼泪为吉米而流，而拳头任何人都有份——只要他在吉米的问题上不够谨慎，未能持有正统的观点。我们别的事情一概不谈。就连那两位斯堪的纳维亚人也议论起了当时的情形——可无法了解他们是什么看法，因为他们用自己的语言争论。贝尔法斯特怀疑他二人中间有一人不敬，可无法确定，既然别无选择，干脆就一起攻击。他们被他的野蛮态度吓坏了，自此，呆在我们中间垂头丧气，活像一对哑巴。沃米博什么时候讲话都让人费解，脸上没有一丝笑容，活像一头畜牲——似乎不比那只猫懂得多——结果倒是很安全。再说，他属于挑选出来营救吉米的那帮人中的一员，因此无需质疑。阿奇平时不爱说话，可他经常带着主人的姿态，和吉米静静聊上个把小时。白天无论什么时候，甚至夜里也经常见到有人坐在吉米的箱子上。傍晚，六点到八点之间，小屋里挤得水泄不通，门口还围着一群兴趣盎然的人。个个都盯着那黑人。 It was at that time that Belfast’s devotion—and also his pugnacity—secured universal respect.He spent every moment of his spare time in Jimmy’s cabin.He tended him, talked to him; was as gentle as a woman, as tenderly gay as an old philanthropist, as sentimentally careful of his nigger as a model slave-owner.But outside he was irritable, explosive as gunpowder, sombre, suspicious, and never more brutal than when most sorrowful.With him it was a tear and a blow: a tear for Jimmy, a blow for any one who did not seem to take a scrupulously orthodox view of Jimmy’s case.We talked about nothing else.The two Scandinavians, even, discussed the situation—but it was impossible to know in what spirit, because they quarrelled in their own language.Belfast suspected one of them of irreverence, and in this incertitude thought that there was no option but to fight them both.They became very much terrified by his truculence, and henceforth lived amongst us, dejected, like a pair of mutes.Wamibo never spoke intelligibly, but he was as smileless as an animal—seemed to know much less about it all than the cat—and consequently was safe.Moreover, he had belonged to the chosen band of Jimmy’s rescuers, and was above suspicion.Archie was silent generally, but often spent an hour or so talking to Jimmy quietly with an air of proprietorship.At any time of the day and often through the night some man could be seen sitting on Jimmy’s box.In the evening, between six and eight, the cabin was crowded, and there was an interested group at the door.Every one stared at the nigger.
他沉浸在我们的兴趣形成的温暖氛围中。他的眼睛里闪烁着讥讽的光芒，他用微弱的声音责备我们怯懦。他说：“要是你们这帮人肯为我坚持到底的话，我这会儿也许就在甲板上了。”我们都低下了头。“是的，你们是不是以为我会让他们给我戴上脚镣手铐，这样好让你们取乐……嗨，不会的……老这样躺着，身体会毁的，真的。反正你们也不关心。”我们非常尴尬，仿佛他说的都是事实。他这种非同寻常的厚颜无耻所向无敌。我们压根就不敢反抗。说真的，我们也不想。我们希望他活着回到家——也就是能够活到航行结束。 He basked in the warmth of our interest.His eye gleamed ironically, and in a weak voice he reproached us with our cowardice.He would say, “If you fellows had stuck out for me I would be now on deck.” We hung our heads.“Yes, but if you think I am going; to let them put me in irons just to show you sport....Well, no....It ruins my health, this lying-up, it does.You don’t care.” We were as abashed as if it had been true.His superb impudence carried all before it.We would not have dared to revolt.We didn’t want to, really.We wanted to keep him alive till home—to the end of the voyage.
辛格尔顿超然如常，似乎对行将逝去生命的琐碎小事不屑一顾。只有一回，他走上前来，却又出人意料地在门口停下了。他默默地凝视着吉米，似乎要把这黑鬼加入到盘踞在他久远记忆中的那群幽灵中去。我们安静异常，辛格尔顿站了好一阵子，仿佛带有使命，专程来召唤某人或者查看某一重大事件似的。詹姆斯·韦特一动不动地躺着，显然没有觉察到别人在仔细端详他，这端详充满了期待。此时，空气中弥漫着一场争斗。我们感到了观看争斗的人们内心的紧张。末了，吉米带着明显的恐惧，在枕头上转动着头。“晚上好。”他用温顺的声音问候道。“嗯。”老海员粗鲁地答道。他威严地定睛打量了吉米一阵，突然走开了。过了好久，船舱内才有人开口讲话，此时我们大家呼吸似乎更加自由，像刚从危险中逃出的人们一样。我们都清楚这位老人对吉米的看法，可谁也不敢反驳。这些看法令人不安，它们会带来痛苦；更糟的是，据我们了解，这些看法都是正确的。只有一次，他屈尊向我们充分解释了他这些看法，于是给大家留下了永久的印象。他说吉米是导致逆风的根源。绝症病人——他说——会捱到看见陆地，一见陆地，就会死去；吉米也明白，第一片陆地将会夺去他的生命。哪条船都是如此。难道我们不知道？他用鄙视的语气严厉地问我们：我们知道些什么？我们下一步还会怀疑什么？吉米的愿望赢得了我们的支持，得到了沃米博的（他是个芬兰人——不是吗？很好！）咒语的帮助，使得这条船在大海上踯躅不前。 Singleton as usual held aloof, appearing to scorn the insignificant events of an ended life.Once only he came along, and unexpectedly stopped in the doorway.He peered at Jimmy in profound silence, as if desirous to add that black image to the crowd of Shades that peopled his old memory.We kept very quiet, and for a long time Singleton stood there as though he had come by appointment to call for some one, or to see some important event.James Wait lay perfectly still, and apparently not aware of the gaze scrutinising him with a steadiness full of expectation.There was a sense of a contest in the air.We felt the inward strain of men watching a wrestling bout.At last Jimmy with perceptible apprehension turned his head on the pillow.—“Good evening,” he said in a conciliating tone.—“H’m,” answered the old seaman, grumpily.For a moment longer he looked at Jimmy with severe fixity, then suddenly went away.It was a long time before any one spoke in the little cabin, though we all breathed more freely as men do after an escape from some dangerous situation.We all knew the old man’s ideas about Jimmy, and nobody dared to combat them.They were unsettling, they caused pain; and, what was worse, they might have been true for all we knew.Only once did he condescend to explain them fully, but the impression was lasting.He said that Jimmy was the cause of head winds.Mortally sick men—he maintained—linger till the first sight of land, and then die; and Jimmy knew that the very first land would draw his life from him.It is so in every ship.Didn’t we know it?He asked us with austere contempt: what did we know?What would we doubt next?Jimmy’s desire encouraged by us and aided by Wamibo’s (he was a Finn—wasn’t he?Very well!) by Wamibo’s spells delayed the ship in the open sea.
这种情况只有傻瓜才看不出来。有谁听说过海上要么持续风平浪静要么总刮逆风？这不符合自然规律……我们无可否认这有点离奇。我们深感不安。俗话所谓“天数越多，挣钱越多”并不能像平常一样给我们一丝安慰，因为我们储备的粮食越来越少。在过好望角时，不少粮食损毁了，我们的饼干供应量也减少了一半。豌豆、糖和茶早就用光了。腌肉也快没有了。我们虽然有足够的咖啡，可煮咖啡的淡水却剩的很少。我们勒紧腰带，从早到晚不停地给船洗刷、打磨和上漆。不久她就看起来焕然一新；可饥饿却缠住了她。不是完全的饿死，而是一步步逼近你的活生生的饥饿，它悄悄地在甲板上溜达，睡在水手舱中；醒着的时候折磨你，睡梦中折腾你。我们望望上风头，看有无改变风向的迹象。不管白天黑夜，每隔几小时，我们就给她调次头，希望她最终能够顺风前进！可她却不。她似乎忘记了回家的路；左冲右突，一会儿头朝西北，一会儿头朝东方；她前后奔波，心烦意乱，活像一只墙根下胆怯的动物撞来撞去。有时，她仿佛累得要死，于是便会一整天都在宁静大海的平滑微波里少气无力地打转。摇摆的桅杆上挂着的帆篷在平静海面上闷热的空气中使劲地拍打着。我们疲惫不堪，又饥又渴，开始相信辛格尔顿所说的话，可我们对吉米忠诚不二，所以在他面前只得把自己的想法隐藏起来。我们用诙谐的隐喻和他说话，我们仿佛是巧妙计策中快乐的同谋；可我们不忘用渴求的眼光，越过船栏向西眺望，希望看到一种迹象，一种顺风的迹象；即使这第一丝风给不情愿的吉米带来死讯。可这是徒劳！宇宙与詹姆斯·韦特早已串通好了。轻快的微风再次从北边吹来；天空依然晴空万里；此时我们倍感困乏，可闪光的大海在微风吹拂下，却纵情地享受着明媚的阳光，似乎忘记了我们的生命与困苦。 Only lubberly fools couldn’t see it.Whoever heard of such a run of calms and head winds?It wasn’t natural....We could not deny that it was strange.We felt uneasy.The common saying, “More days, more dollars,” did not give the usual comfort because the stores were running short.Much had been spoiled off the Cape, and we were on half allowance of biscuit.Peas, sugar and tea had been finished long ago.Salt meat was giving out.We had plenty of coffee but very little water to make it with.We took up another hole in our belts and went on scraping, polishing, painting the ship from morning to night.And soon she looked as though she had come out of a band-box; but hunger lived on board of her.Not dead starvation, but steady, living hunger that stalked about the decks, slept in the forecastle; the tormentor of waking moments, the disturber of dreams.We looked to windward for signs of change.Every few hours of night and day we put her round with the hope that she would come up on that tack at last!She didn’t.She seemed to have forgotten the way home; she rushed to and fro, heading northwest, heading east; she ran backwards and forwards, distracted, like a timid creature at the foot of a wall.Sometimes, as if tired to death, she would wallow languidly for a day in the smooth swell of an unruffled sea.All up the swinging masts the sails thrashed furiously through the hot stillness of the calm.We were weary, hungry, thirsty; we commenced to believe Singleton, but with unshaken fidelity dissembled to Jimmy.We spoke to him with jocose allusiveness, like cheerful accomplices in a clever plot; but we looked to the westward over the rail with longing eyes for a sign of hope, for a sign of fair wind; even if its first breath should bring death to our reluctant Jimmy.In vain!The universe conspired with James Wait.Light airs from the northward sprang up again; the sky remained clear; and round our weariness the glittering sea, touched by the breeze, basked voluptuously in the great sunshine, as though it had forgotten our life and trouble.
唐金和大家一起观望着，看有无顺风到来。现在，没人知道他正在动什么坏心思。他默不作声，显得更加消瘦，众人和命运对他的不公令他内心极为愤恨，而这愤恨仿佛在慢慢地侵蚀着他的身体。没人理会他，他也不跟谁说话，但他鬼鬼祟祟的眼睛里却深藏着对每个人的仇视。他只与厨子搭讪，莫名其妙哄得这个好人相信他——唐金——遭受了极大的污蔑与迫害。他们一起为船上这帮人的不道德而叹息。我们罪大恶极，编造谎言，串通一气，陷害一个可怜无辜的黑人，要把他那毫无准备的灵魂送到万劫不复的地狱中去。波德莫尔就现有的材料备餐，心里十分懊悔，一直觉得为这样一群罪人做饭，必定会影响自己赎罪。至于船长——他已经跟他航海七年了，可现在，他说，怎么也不会相信这么一个人竟会……“算啦。算啦……事已至此……无可挽回。一贯的判断顷刻颠覆……自尊心深受打击……更像一场飞来横祸。”唐金阴着脸，高高地坐在煤箱上，晃着双腿，不住附和。他以假惺惺的同意这微小的付出，换得了坐进厨房的特权；他无比沮丧与愤慨；他赞同厨子所说，而又找不到足够严厉的字眼批评我们的行径；猛烈谴责时，他会诅咒我们，而波德莫尔假装没有听见，其实，要不是碍于信仰，他也会诅咒我们的。唐金见无人怪他，索性大骂起来，骂得足够二人受用，他向厨子要火柴，借烟丝，像在自己家里一样，能在炉前盘桓好几个小时。从他那儿，他可以听到我们在舱壁另一侧和吉米说话。厨子连续敲打着铁锅，砰地关上炉门，嘴里咕哝着诅咒全体船员的预言；从不承认来世的（除非为了亵渎神灵）的唐金，却聚精会神地听着，义愤填膺，异常得意地幻想着脑海中建立起来的无限痛苦的形象——正像人们幻想残暴与复仇、贪婪与强权的形象一样…… Donkin looked out for a fair wind along with the rest.No one knew the venom of his thoughts now.He was silent, and appeared thinner, as if consumed slowly by an inward rage at the injustice of men and of fate.He was ignored by all and spoke to no one, but his hate for every man dwelt in his furtive eyes.He talked with the cook only, having somehow persuaded the good man that he—Donkin—was a much calumniated and persecuted person.Together they bewailed the immorality of the ship’s company.There could be no greater criminals than we, who by our lies conspired to send the unprepared soul of a poor ignorant black man to everlasting perdition.Podmore cooked what there was to cook, remorsefully, and felt all the time that by preparing the food of such sinners he imperilled his own salvation.As to the Captain—he had sailed with him for seven years, now, he said, and would not have believed it possible that such a man...“Well. Well...There it was...Can’t get out of it.Judgment capsized all in a minute...Struck in all his pride...More like a sudden visitation than anything else.” Donkin, perched sullenly on the coal-locker, swung his legs and concurred.He paid in the coin of spurious assent for the privilege to sit in the galley; he was disheartened and scandalised; he agreed with the cook; could find no words severe enough to criticise our conduct; and when in the heat of reprobation he swore at us, Podmore, who would have liked to swear also if it hadn’t been for his principles, pretended not to hear.So Donkin, unrebuked, cursed enough for two, cadged for matches, borrowed tobacco, and loafed for hours, very much at home, before the stove.From there he could hear us on the other side of the bulkhead, talking to Jimmy.The cook knocked the saucepans about, slammed the oven door, muttered prophesies of damnation for all the ship’s company; and Donkin, who did not admit of any hereafter (except for purposes of blasphemy) listened, concentrated and angry, gloating fiercely over a called-up image of infinite torment—as men gloat over the accursed images of cruelty and revenge, of greed, and of power....
晴朗的夜晚，月亮无精打采，清冷的月光下，沉默的轮船一改平日芳容，变得死气沉沉、昏昏欲睡，就像陆地上的严冬。她身下一条金色的长带点缀着黑盘似的大海。寂静的甲板上回荡着脚步声。月光犹如一层冰冷的雾，紧紧地包围着她，帆蓬高高地挂在空中，锥形的顶部仿佛是洁白的雪，发出耀眼的光芒。在这由虚幻的光芒构成的壮丽图景中，船儿显得如此纯洁，仿佛是一位理想的美人，她又是那样朦胧，宛如温柔祥和的梦。船上一切都显得不真实，模模糊糊，毫无立体感，甲板上充斥着深暗的影子，这些影子伴随着那默无声息、一刻不停的骚动：它们比夜更黑暗，比人的思想更不安。 On clear evenings the silent ship, under the cold sheen of the dead moon, took on a false aspect of passionless repose resembling the winter of the earth.Under her a long band of gold barred the black disc of the sea. Footsteps echoed on her quiet decks.The moonlight clung to her like a frosted mist, and the white sails stood out in dazzling cones as of stainless snow.In the magnificence of the phantom rays the ship appeared pure like a vision of ideal beauty, illusive like a tender dream of serene peace.And nothing in her was real, nothing was distinct and solid but the heavy shadows that filled her decks with their unceasing and noiseless stir: the shadows darker than the night and more restless than the thoughts of men.
唐金心怀怨恨，独自一人蹑手蹑脚穿行在阴影中，心里思忖着吉米拖了这么久还没有死去。那天晚上，高处的人们报告说看到了陆地，船长一边调试望远镜的长筒，一边凄楚地和贝克先生聊天，经过一步步艰难的航行，我们终于到了西部群岛，现在别无他求，只要近期风平浪静就行。天气晴朗，气压表升得很高。轻柔的微风随着太阳的西沉变得更加微弱，无边无际的沉静笼罩着晒热的海水，预示着夜里不会有风。只要白昼没有消逝，水手们便聚在船头楼最前部观看东方的天空，那里，弗洛里斯群岛屹立在无边无际的海面上，轮廓曲折断续，仿佛辽阔荒凉的平原上一座死气沉沉的废墟。这是四个月以来见到的首块陆地。查利十分兴奋，在大家的怂恿下，竟然对上司放肆起来。大家莫名其妙地兴高采烈，三五成群地交谈着，不时挥舞着光光的臂膀。在这毋庸置疑的现实面前，吉米亦真亦幻的存在似乎暂时被人们遗忘，这还是此次航行的头一回。我们总算走过了漫长的路程。贝尔法斯特高谈阔论，引用凭空想象的事例，说明从这片群岛回家的路有多么便捷。“那些运水果的快速帆船只需五天，”他肯定地说道，“你需要什么？——仅仅是一点风。” 阿奇却坚持说，航海记录上说要走七天，于是他们用侮辱的话语亲切地争论着。诺尔斯嚷嚷道，他从那儿已经闻得见家乡的气息啦，他把重量倚在那只短腿上，放声大笑，几乎笑折了腰。一群头发灰白的老水手默默注视了片刻，脸上露出严峻凝重的表情。有位突然说道：“现在离伦敦不太远了。”“上岸的头一晚上，我非得好好吃上一顿牛排加洋葱……再喝上一品脱苦啤酒。”另一位说。“你是说一桶。”有人喊道。“一日三餐，光吃火腿和鸡蛋。我就这样过！”一个激动的声音嚷嚷道。接下来是一阵叽叽喳喳，这是赞同的低语声；人们的眼睛开始闪亮；嘴嚼得咂咂作响；急促开怀的笑声不时传来。阿奇克制住自己暗自微笑。辛格尔顿走了过来，漠不关心地瞥了一眼，一言未发，便走了下去，他那漠然的样子，仿佛见过弗洛里斯无数次了。东方赶来的夜从清澈的空中降下，遮住了布满紫色斑点的高地。“没有一丝风浪。”有人静静地说。兴致勃勃的低声交谈突然松懈下来，逐渐消失；聚集的人群四散开来，人们一个个飘然离去，他们慢慢走下梯子，神情严肃，仿佛有关他们冥冥中的主宰的那句话惊醒了他们。硕大橙黄的月亮从清澈的地平线薄薄的边缘升了起来，它发现轮船正沉醉在屏息般的沉默中；一艘无畏的似乎酣睡着的无梦之船，正躺在沉睡着的可怕的大海怀中。 Donkin prowled spiteful and alone amongst the shadows, thinking that Jimmy too long delayed to die. That evening land had been reported from aloft, and the master, while adjusting the tubes of the long glass, had observed with quiet bitterness to Mr. Baker that, after fighting our way inch by inch to the Western Islands, there was nothing to expect now but a spell of calm.The sky was clear and the barometer high.The light breeze dropped with the sun, and an enormous stillness, forerunner of a night without wind, descended upon the heated waters of the ocean.As long as daylight lasted, the hands collected on the forecastle-head watched on the eastern sky the island of Flores, that rose above the level expanse of the sea with irregular and broken outlines like a sombre ruin upon a vast and deserted plain.It was the first land seen for nearly four months.Charley was excited, and in the midst of general indulgence took liberties with his betters.Men strangely elated without knowing why, talked in groups, and pointed with bared arms.For the first time that voyage Jimmy’s sham existence seemed for a moment forgotten in the face of a solid reality.We had got so far anyhow.Belfast discoursed, quoting imaginary examples of short homeward runs from the Islands.“Them smart fruit schooners do it in five days,” he affirmed.“What do you want?—only a good little breeze.” Archie maintained that seven days was the record passage, and they disputed amicably with insulting words.Knowles declared he could already smell home from there, and with a heavy list on his short leg laughed fit to split his sides.A group of grizzled sea-dogs looked out for a time in silence and with grim absorbed faces.One said suddenly—“’Tain’t far to London now.”—“My first night ashore, blamme if I haven’t steak and onions for supper...and a pint of bitter,” said another.—“A barrel ye mean,” shouted someone.—“Ham an’ eggs three times a day. That’s the way I live!” cried an excited voice.There was a stir, appreciative murmurs; eyes began to shine; jaws champed; short, nervous laughs were heard.Archie smiled with reserve all to himself.Singleton came up, gave a careless glance, and went down again without saying a word, indifferent, like a man who had seen Flores an incalculable number of times.The night travelling from the East blotted out of the limpid sky the purple stain of the high land.“Dead calm,” said somebody quietly.The murmur of lively talk suddenly wavered, died out; the clusters broke up; men began to drift away one by one, descending the ladders slowly and with serious faces as if sobered by that reminder of their dependence upon the invisible.And when the big yellow moon ascended gently above the sharp rim of the clear horizon it found the ship wrapped up in a breathless silence; a fearless ship that seemed to sleep profoundly, dreamlessly on the bosom of the sleeping and terrible sea.
唐金痛恨这宁静——痛恨这艘船——痛恨这向四周延伸的大海，它把上帝创造的一切都消融在无边的沉默中。他觉得自己受到了无名怨忿的猛烈打击。他虽然身体上受到了威胁，可受伤的自尊心却不屈不挠，什么也医治不了他的心灵创伤。陆地近在咫尺——马上就到家了——发工钱的日子也拿不到几个——缺衣少穿——干不完的重活。这一切真是恼人。还有陆地。这陆地将夺去患病水手的生命。那个黑鬼有钱——有衣服——过着悠闲的日子；他才不愿死呢。陆地夺去生命……他禁不住想看个究竟。也许已经……那可真够运气的。黑鬼的箱子里有钱呢。他快步从阴影中走到月光下，刹那间，他那贪婪饥渴的脸由黄色变成了青灰色。他打开舱门，不禁大吃一惊。毫无疑问，吉米死啦！他横躺着，一动不动，双手紧握，仿佛石棺盖上雕刻的人像。唐金用贪婪的目光注视着一切。吉米躺着没动，只是眨了眨眼睑，这可又把唐金吓了一跳。他那双眼睛着实吓人。他轻轻关上身后的舱门，凝神注视了吉米一会儿，好像他冒了巨大风险来到这儿，就是为了告诉他令人吃惊的重要秘密似的。吉米仍然没有动，只是用眼角疲惫地瞥了一眼。“大海平静吧？”他问道。“是呀。”唐金回答，同时十分失望地坐在箱子上。 Donkin chafed at the peace—at the ship—at the sea that stretching away on all sides merged into the illimitable silence of all creation.He felt himself pulled up sharp by unrecognised grievances.He had been physically cowed, but his injured dignity remained indomitable, and nothing could heal his lacerated feelings.Here was land already—home very soon—a bad pay-day—no clothes—more hard work.How offensive all this was.Land. The land that draws away life from sick sailors.That nigger there had money—clothes—easy times; and would not die. Land draws life away....He felt tempted to go and see whether it did.Perhaps already.. It would be a bit of luck.There was money in the beggar’s chest.He stepped briskly out of the shadows into the moonlight, and, instantly, his craving, hungry face from sallow became livid.He opened the door of the cabin and had a shock.Sure enough, Jimmy was dead!He moved no more than a recumbent figure with clasped hands, carved on the lid of a stone coffin.Donkin glared with avidity.Then Jimmy, without stirring, blinked his eyelids, and Donkin had another shock.Those eyes were rather startling.He shut the door behind his back with gentle care, looking intently the while at James Wait as though he had come in there at a great risk to tell some secret of startling im-portance.Jimmy did not move but glanced languidly out of the corners of his eyes.—“Calm?” he asked.—“Yuss,” said Donkin, very disappointed, and sat down on the box.
这种访问不管白天黑夜，什么时候都有，吉米已经习以为常。这个去那个来。他们讲话声音清晰，用词欢快，重复着老笑话，倾听着他讲话；每人离开时，似乎都留下了一丝生气，献出了些许力量，反复交代要保重生命——生命不可毁灭！他不喜欢独自呆在自己的舱房里，因为，独自一人时，觉得自己根本不存在。一切都没有。没有痛苦。至少现在没有。一点不错——可他享受不了健康带给他的安静，除非有人在旁边看着。这家伙会跟任何人做得一样好。唐金悄悄地观察着他。“马上就到家了。”韦特说。“你干嘛说话声这么小？”唐金饶有兴趣地问道，“怎么不大声讲？”吉米显得有些生气，好一阵子没言语；然后，用低低的声音闷闷地说道：“我干嘛要叫嚷？你又不是聋子，这我还不知道。”“噢！我听得再清楚不过了。”唐金低低的声音答道，眼睛朝下看。他正悲伤得想着出去时，吉米又开腔了：“我们真正到家时，弄点什么像样的东西吃……我老觉得饿。”唐金突然怒气冲天。“我呢，”他声音沙哑地叫道，“我也饿得难受，可还得去干活。而你，还说饿！”“你干的活又不会要你的命，”韦特有气无力地说，“这儿下铺有几块饼干——你拿一块吃吧。我吃不下去。”　唐金猛冲过去，在角落里乱摸起来，当他走回来时，嘴里已经塞得满满的。他充满热情地咀嚼着。吉米似乎睁着眼睛打瞌睡。唐金吃罢硬饼干站起身。“你还不走？”吉米问道，眼睛死死盯着天花板。“不走。”唐金脱口答道，于是不但没有走，反而将背靠在了关着的门上。他打量着詹姆斯·韦特，只见他又高又瘦，形容枯槁，好像在白色炉子的烘烤下，肌肉都皱缩在了骨头上；一只手枯瘦的五指在床铺边沿轻轻来回移动，弹奏着没完没了的曲子。打量他真是让人既恼火又厌倦，他这样子可能还会捱上几天。他真吓人——完全处在不死不活的境地，正是对生死的无知，他才不会受到任何伤害。唐金饶有兴趣地去开导他。“你心里在想些什么呢？”他直率地问道。詹姆斯·韦特那瘦骨嶙峋、死人般冷漠的脸上浮现出一丝怪异的笑容，这笑容令人惊恐，仿佛梦中死人的突然微笑。 Jimmy was used to such visits at all times of night of day. Men succeeded one another.They spoke in clear voices, pronounced cheerful words, repeated old jokes, listened to him; and each, going out, seemed to leave behind a little of his own vitality, surrender some of his own strength, renew the assurance of life—the indestructible thing!He did not like to be alone in his cabin, because, when he was alone, it seemed to him as if he hadn’t been there at all.There was nothing.No pain.Not now. Perfectly right—but he couldn’t enjoy his healthful repose unless some one was by to see it.This man would do as well as anybody.Donkin watched him stealthily:—“Soon home now,” observed Wait.—“Vy d’yer whisper?” asked Donkin with interest, “can’t yer speak up?” Jimmy looked annoyed and said nothing for a while; then in a lifeless, unringing voice:—“Why should I shout?You ain’t deaf that I know.”—“Oh!I can ’ear right enough,” answered Donkin in a low tone, and looked down.He was thinking sadly of going out when Jimmy spoke again.—“Time we did get home...to get something decent to eat...I am always hungry.” Donkin felt angry all of a sudden.—“What about me,” he hissed, “I am ’ungry too an’ got ter work.You, ’ungry!”—“Your work won’t kill you,” commented Wait, feebly; “there’s a couple of biscuits in the lower bunk there—you may have one.I can’t eat them.” Donkin dived in, groped in the corner and when he came up again his mouth was full.He munched with ardour.Jimmy seemed to doze with open eyes.Donkin finished his hard bread and got up.—“You’re not going?” asked Jimmy, staring at the ceiling.—“No,” said Donkin, impulsively, and instead of going out leaned his back against the closed door.He looked at James Wait, and saw him long, lean, dried up, as though all his flesh had shrivelled on his bones in the heat of a white furnace; the meagre fingers of one hand moved lightly upon the edge of the bunk playing an endless tune.To look at him was irritating and fatiguing; he could last like this for days; he was outrageous—belonging wholly neither to death nor life, and perfectly invulnerable in his apparent ignorance of both.Donkin felt tempted to enlighten him.—“What are yer thinkin’ of?” he asked, surlily.James Wait had a grimacing smile that passed over the deathlike impassiveness of his bony face, incredible and frightful as would, in a dream, have been the sudden smile of a corpse.
“有一个姑娘，”韦特低声说，“广东街上的姑娘。她甩了伦尼船上的三管轮——全是为了我。她烹的牡蛎正合我的口味……她说——她会丢开——任何有钱人——粗俗的人。” “There is a girl,” whispered Wait....“Canton Street girl.———She chucked a third engineer of a Rennie boat———for me. Cooks oysters just as I like...She says———she would chuck———any toff———louder.”
唐金简直不敢相信自己的耳朵。他深感震惊：“她会吗？你对她没什么好处。”他说着，厌恶之情溢于言表。韦特根本没听他说些什么。他正幻想着昂首阔步走在东印度码头路上；爽快地说：“来喝上一杯。”于是，推开旋转的玻璃门，信心十足地站在煤气灯下面的红木柜台前。“你觉得你还能上得了岸吗？”唐金愤怒地问道。韦特猛然从幻觉中惊醒。“还有十天。”他随口答道，然后又立即回到了那对时间一无所知的记忆王国里。他毫无倦意，镇定自若，安全退回到自己的内心深处，任何重大不安的事情都与他无关。在他悠闲度过的极度宁静的时光里，存在着永恒不变的东西。他安静惬意地生活在美好的回忆里，把这种回忆错误地当作将来的真实景象而兴奋不已。他不理会任何人。唐金模模糊糊地觉察到了这一点，这正如盲人在黑暗中感到来自周围一切的致命敌意，这一切无法接近，不能看见，却又令人羡慕。他有一种愿望，想显示自己的非凡，打烂一切，压碎一切；在所有事上可与任何人一比高低；撕掉面纱，扯去面具，不留任何遮掩，彻底把自己暴露在世人面前——好一个坦率的背信弃义的愿望！他喷着唾沫，哈哈大笑，同时不无讥讽地说道： Donkin could hardly believe his ears.He was scandalised—“Would she?Yer wouldn’t be any good to ’er,” he said with unrestrained disgust.Wait was not there to hear him.He was swaggering up the East India Dock Road; saying kindly, “Come along for a treat,” pushing glass swing-doors, posing with superb assurance in the gaslight above a mahogany counter.—“D’yer think yer will ever get ashore?” asked Donkin, angrily.Wait came back with a start.—“Ten days,” he said, promptly, and returned at once to the regions of memory that know nothing of time.He felt untired, calm, and safely withdrawn within himself beyond the reach of every grave incertitude.There was something of the immutable quality of eternity in the slow moments of his complete rest-fulness.He was very quiet and easy amongst his vivid reminiscences which he mistook joyfully for images of an undoubted future.He cared for no one. Donkin felt this vaguely like a blind man feeling in his darkness the fatal antagonism of all the surrounding existences, that to him shall for ever remain irrealisable, unseen and enviable.He had a desire to assert his importance, to break, to crush; to be even with everybody for everything; to tear the veil, unmask, expose, leave no refuge—a perfidious desire of truthfulness!He laughed in a mocking splutter and said:
“十天。打死我也不信！……也许明天这个时候你已经死啦。 还十天呢！”他等了一会儿。“你听见我的话了吗？看你现在的样子，和死人没什么两样。” “Ten days.Strike me blind if lever!...You will be dead by this time to-morrow p’r’aps.Ten days!” He waited for a while.“D’ye ’ear me?Blamme if yer don’t look dead already.”
韦特一定是憋足了劲，因为他几乎说出了声：“你这个卑鄙的乞丐、骗子。谁还不了解你。”出人意料的是，他竟然坐了起来，这下可吓坏了他的客人。可不一会儿，唐金又恢复了镇定。他气势汹汹地问道：“什么？什么？谁是骗子？你是——大伙儿是——船长是——都是。而我不是！还摆什么架子！你是什么人？”他气得几乎话都说不出来了。“你是什么人，还摆臭架子。”他重复了一遍，浑身直哆嗦。“拿一块——拿一块，他说——他自己不能吃。现在，我要拿两块。上帝呀——我偏拿！你算什么东西！” Wait must have been collecting his strength, for he said almost aloud—“You’re a stinking, cadging liar.Every one knows you.” And sitting up, against all probability, startled his visitor horribly.But very soon Donkin recovered himself.He blustered, “What?What?Who’s a liar?You are—the crowd are—the skipper—everybody.I ain’t!Putting on airs!Who’s yer?” He nearly choked himself with indignation.“Who’s yer to put on airs,” he repeated, trembling.“’Ave one—’ave one, says ’ee—an’ cawn’t eat ’em ’isself.Now I’ll ’ave both.By Gawd—I will!Yer nobody!”
他钻到下铺，在那儿翻来翻去，终于又找到一块脏兮兮的饼干。他把饼干举到吉米面前——然后，挑衅似地猛咬一口。 He plunged into the lower bunk, rooted in there and brought to light another dusty biscuit.He held it up before Jimmy—then took a bite defiantly.
“现在怎么样？”他像疯了一样，厚颜无耻地问道。“你可以拿一块——你说的。为什么不把两块都给我？不给。我是一条肮脏的狗。一块留给这肮脏的狗。我两块都要。你拦得住吗？试试看。来呀。试试看。” “What now?” he asked with feverish impudence.“Yer may take one—says yer.Why not giv’ me both?No. I’m a mangy dorg.One fur a mangy dorg.I’ll tyke both.Can yer stop me?Try. Come on. Try.”
吉米抱着自己的双腿，脸紧贴在膝盖上。衬衫紧紧裹着身子。根根肋骨清晰可见。阵阵喘息使他瘦弱的脊背不住地前后颤动。 Jimmy was clasping his legs and hiding his face on the knees.His shirt clung to him.Every rib was visible.His emaciated back was shaken in repeated jerks by the panting catches of his breath.
“你不过来呀？你做不到！我说什么来着？”唐金继续恶狠狠地说。他又匆忙使劲地吞了一大口饼干。另一位默默无语，无可奈何，他那软弱、畏缩的态度进一步激怒了唐金。“你快完蛋啦！”他叫道，“你是谁，用得着对你撒谎，把你当成该死的皇帝伺候着。你什么都不是。什么都不是！”他飞沫四溅，句句切中要害；由于用劲过大，结果，全身摇摆，仿佛松开的细绳不住地颤抖。 “Yer won’t?Yer can’t!What did I say?” went on Donkin, fiercely.He swallowed another dry mouthful with a hasty effort.The other’s silent helplessness, his weakness, his shrinking attitude exasperated him.“Ye’re done!” he cried.“Who’s yer to be lied to; to be waited on ’and an’ foot like a bloomin’ ymperor.Yer nobody.Yer no one at all!” he spluttered with such a strength of unerring conviction that it shook him from head to foot in coming out, and left him vibrating like a released string.
詹姆斯·韦特重新振作起来。他抬起头，勇敢地转向唐金，于是唐金看到了一张奇怪、陌生的脸，一副非同寻常的怪面具，充满了失望与愤怒。面具上的嘴唇快速地噏动着；小屋里回荡着空洞、悲切的呼啸声，夹杂着模糊的充满恐吓、抱怨和忧伤的低语声，仿佛远处起风时发出的呜呜声。韦特摇摇头，翻翻眼；他否认，咒骂，威胁——可没有一个字有力量越过他那悲伤的撅起的两片黑唇。这番话难以理解，令人不安；是情感的模糊流露，是狂乱的哑剧，为不可能的事情辩护，表达了一种渺茫的复仇愿望。这使唐金清醒起来，更加仔细地观察起他来。 James Wait rallied again.He lifted his head and turned bravely at Donkin, who saw a strange face, an unknown face, a fantastic and grimacing mask of despair and fury.Its lips moved rapidly; and hollow, moaning, whistling sounds filled the cabin with a vague mutter full of menace, complaint and desolation, like the far-off murmur of a rising wind.Wait shook his head; rolled his eyes; he denied, cursed, threatened—and not a word had the strength to pass beyond the sorrowful pout of those black lips.It was incomprehensible and disturbing; a gibberish of emotions, a frantic dumb show of speech pleading for impossible things, promising a shadowy vengeance.It sobered Donkin into a scrutinising watchfulness.
“你叫不出声啦。是吧？我说什么来着？”他专注地观察了一阵后，慢慢说道。对方不停地说着，一句也听不清，还不住激烈地点着头，张着嘴，雪白的大牙发出奇异骇人的光。唐金仿佛被这无声的口才和黑色幽灵的愤怒所吸引，走上前去，带着几分怀疑和好奇，伸长了脖颈；突然间，他感到自己看到的只是一个人的影子，高高地蜷曲在与他眼睛同一水平面上的铺位上。“什么？什么？”他说。在那连续不断的呼呼喘声中，他似乎听清了几个词。“你要告诉贝尔法斯特！是吧？难道你还是啥都不懂的孩子？”他哆嗦着，既吃惊又愤怒，“告诉你姥姥也行！你害怕了！你算什么东西，想让别人怕你？”他那强烈的自命不凡感连同他最后的一丝谨慎一起消失得无影无踪。 “Yer can’t oller.See? What did I tell yer?” he said, slowly, after a moment of attentive examination.The other kept on headlong and unheard, nodding passionately, grinning with grotesque and appalling flashes of big white teeth.Donkin, as if fascinated by the dumb eloquence and anger of that black phantom, approached, stretching his neck out with distrustful curiosity; and it seemed to him suddenly that he was looking only at the shadow of a man crouching high in the bunk on the level with his eyes.—“What?What?” he said.He seemed to catch the shape of some words in the continuous panting hiss.“Yer will tell Belfast!Will yer?Are yer a bloomin’ kid?” He trembled with alarm and rage, “Tell yer gran’mother!Yer afeard!Who’s yer ter be afeard more’n any one?” His passionate sense of his own importance ran away with a last remnant of caution.
“告诉他们，你这王八蛋！有本事的话，去告诉呀！”他大叫道，“你们这些背后使坏的王八蛋，对我还没有对狗好。他们唆使我，纯粹是为了陷害我。这里就我一个好人。他们打我，踢我——而你却哈哈大笑——你这黑鬼，你这腐烂无用的东西，你！你要为此赔我。他们把自己的食物、水送给你——你要把这东西赔我，上帝呀！有谁请我喝过一杯水？那天夜里，他们把自己的破被给你盖，可他们给了我什么——一个大嘴巴——去他们的……谁来帮我呀……你要赔我钱。只要你一断气，我就叫你赔，你这个该死的无用骗子。我就是这样的人。而你不是人——你这个该死的东西。嗨——你这死尸！”　他把一直紧紧攥在手里的饼干嗖的一声向吉米头部砸去，可它只是擦身而过，啪的一声打在了搁板上，像手榴弹一样，炸成了碎片。詹姆斯·韦特仿佛受到了致命一击，向后一仰，倒在了枕头上。他的双唇不再动弹，转动的眼睛安静了下来，聚精会神，死死地盯着上方。唐金大吃一惊；一下子瘫坐在箱子上，低着头，显得疲惫、沮丧。过了一会儿，他开始自言自语道：“死吧，你这乞丐——死吧。也许有人要进来……我希望我喝醉了酒……十天……牡蛎……”他扬起脸大声说。“不…… 不再有你的啦……再没有什么姑娘给你烹牡蛎吃啦……你是什么东西？现在轮到我啦……但愿我醉了；我马上给你一脚送你上天。那是你早晚要去的地方。脚朝前，越过船舷……扑通！从此再也见不到你啦。葬身大海！对你够好了吧。”　吉米的头微微动了动，把目光转向唐金的脸；那怀疑、凄凉、恐惧的目光，仿佛小孩听到威胁——要把自己一个人关在黑暗中——时的害怕眼神。唐金坐在箱子上望着他，目光中充满希望；然后，干脆坐着，去试着打开箱盖。箱子锁了。“真希望我喝醉了。”他喃喃地自语，接着站起身，不安地倾听着远处甲板上的脚步声。那声音越来越近——又停了下来。有人在门外打着长长的呵欠，拖着懒洋洋的脚步走开了。唐金惊跳的心脏恢复了平静，他再次望望床上的吉米，只见吉米和刚才一样注视着白色的横梁。“你这会儿感觉怎么样？”他问。“难受。”吉米喘着气答道。 “Tell an’ be damned!Tell, if yer can!” he cried.“I’ve been treated worser’n a dorg by your blooming back-lickers.They ’as set me on, only to turn aginst me.I am the only man ’ere.They clouted me, kicked me—an’ yer laffed—yer black, rotten incumbrance, you!You will pay fur it.They giv’ yer their grub, their water—yer will pay fur it to me, by Gawd!Who axed me ter ’ave a drink of water?They put their bloomin’ rags on yer that night, an’ what did they giv’ ter me—a clout on the bloomin’ mouth—blast their...S’elp me!...Yer will pay fur it with yer money.I’m goin’ ter ’ave it in a minyte; as soon has ye’re dead, yer bloomin’ useless fraud.That’s the man I am.An’ ye’re a thing—a bloody thing.Yah—you corpse!” He flung at Jimmy’s head the biscuit he had been all the time clutching hard, but it only grazed, and striking with a loud crack the bulkhead beyond burst like a hand-grenade into flying pieces.James Wait, as if wounded mortally, fell back on the pillow.His lips ceased to move and the rolling eyes became quiet and stared upwards with an intense and steady persistence.Donkin was surprised; he sat suddenly on the chest, and looked down, exhausted and gloomy.After a moment, he began to mutter to himself, “Die, you beggar—die.Somebody’ll come in...I wish I was drunk...Ten days...oysters...”He looked up and spoke louder.“No...No more for yer...no more bloomin’ gals that cook oysters...Who’s yer?It’s my turn now...I wish I was drunk; I would soon giv’ you a leg up.That’s where yer bound to go. Feet fust, through a port...Splash! Never see yer any more.Overboard! Good ’nuff fur yer.” Jimmy’s head moved slightly and he turned his eyes to Donkin’s face; a gaze unbelieving, desolated and appealing, of a child frightened by the menace of being shut up alone in the dark.Donkin observed him from the chest with hopeful eyes; then, without rising, tried the lid.Locked. “I wish I was drunk,” he muttered and getting up listened anxiously to the distant sound of footsteps on the deck.They approached—ceased.Some one yawned interminably just outside the door, and the footsteps went away shuffling lazily.Donkin’s fluttering heart eased its pace, and when he looked towards the bunk again Jimmy was staring as before at the white beam.—“’Ow d’yer feel now?” he asked.—“Bad,” breathed out Jimmy.
唐金怀着自己不可告人的目的，耐着性子坐了下来。每隔半小时，钟声沿着船身从船头到船尾应和一次。吉米的呼吸骤然加剧，难以计数，可又微弱得几乎听不到。他的眼神惊恐万分，仿佛看到了难以名状的可怕景象；从他脸上，可以看出他正在想着这些讨厌的东西。突然，他哽咽着说起话来，那超乎想象的响亮声音充满了悲伤： Donkin sat down patient and purposeful.Every half-hour the bells spoke to one another ringing along the whole length of the ship.Jimmy’s respiration was so rapid that it couldn’t be counted, so faint that it couldn’t be heard.His eyes were terrified as though he had been looking at unspeakable horrors; and by his face one could see that he was thinking of abominable things.Suddenly with an incredibly strong and heartbreaking voice he sobbed out:
“抛入大海！……我！……我的主呀！”唐金坐在箱子上，身子扭了一下。他看起来很不情愿。詹姆斯·韦特不再说话。他那两只骨瘦如柴的长手把毛毯向上拉平，似乎希望把整条毯子全掖到下巴下。一滴眼泪，一滴大大的孤独眼泪，从眼角流出，不曾碰到凹陷的面颊，直接落到了枕头上。他的喉咙发出微弱的咯咯声。 “Overboard!...I!...My God!”Donkin writhed a little on the box.He looked unwillingly.James Wait was mute.His two long bony hands smoothed the blanket upwards, as though he had wished to gather it all up under his chin.A tear, a big solitary tear, escaped from the corner of his eye and, without touching the hollow cheek, fell on the pillow.His throat rattled faintly.
唐金望着这个可恨的黑鬼的弥留场面，感到巨大的悲伤带来的痛苦紧紧揪住了他的心；因为，他想到了他自己有一天也会经历这一切——就像现在的情况——很可能！他的眼睛湿润了。“可怜的黑鬼。”他咕哝着说。那个夜晚似乎一闪而过；他似乎听到了宝贵的时光在飞速流逝，一去不返。这件讨厌的事到底会拖多长时间？真是太长了。倒霉透了。他再也按捺不住自己。他站起身，走到床边。韦特一动不动。只有他的眼睛透出一丝活力，两只手继续不知疲倦地拉着毛毯，样子十分可怕。唐金俯下身子。 And Donkin, watching the end of that hateful nigger, felt the anguishing grasp of a great sorrow on his heart at the thought that he himself, some day, would have to go through it all—just like this—perhaps!His eyes became moist.“Poor beggar,” he murmured.The night seemed to go by in a flash; it seemed to him he could hear the irremediable rush of precious minutes.How long would this blooming affair last?Too long surely.No luck.He could not restrain himself.He got up and approached the bunk.Wait did not stir.Only his eyes appeared alive and his hands continued their smoothing movement with a horrible and tireless industry.Donkin bent over.
“吉米。”他轻声叫道。没有回音，但是喉咙里的咯咯声消失了。“你看得见我吗？”他哆哆嗦嗦地问道。吉米的胸口不住起伏。唐金扭头望着别处，将耳朵凑近唐金的嘴唇，能听到沙沙的声音，仿佛海滨平整的沙滩上刮起的一片枯叶。声音逐渐清晰起来。 “Jimmy,” he called low.There was no answer, but the rattle stopped.“D’yer see me?” he asked, trembling.Jimmy’s chest heaved.Donkin, looking away, bent his ear to Jimmy’s lips, and heard a sound like the rustle of a single dry leaf driven along the smooth sand of a beach.It shaped itself.
“点上……灯……就……走开。”韦特气喘吁吁地说。 “Light...the lamp...and...go,” breathed out Wait.
唐金本能地回头望了一眼那闪耀的灯光；然后仍将目光转向一边，在枕头下摸到一把钥匙。他立刻攥在手中，接下来的几分钟，他哆哆嗦嗦地跪在地上，慌乱地翻着箱子。站起来的时候，他脸上——生平第一次——闪现着淡淡的红晕——也许这是胜利的象征吧。 Donkin, instinctively, glanced over his shoulder at the brilliant flame; then, still looking away, felt under the pillow for a key. He got it at once and for the next few minutes remained on his knees shakily but swiftly busy inside the box. When he got up, his face—for the first time in his life—had a pink flush—perhaps of triumph.
他又把钥匙迅速塞回枕头下，一眼也不愿多看躺在床上一动不动的吉米。他毅然从床边转过头，大步走向房门，仿佛有一英里的路要赶似的。他刚迈出第二步，鼻子就碰到了房门。他小心翼翼地紧紧抓住门拉手，就在此时，脑海中产生了一种挥之不去的印象：身后发生了什么事。仿佛肩膀被人拍了一下，他连忙转过身。此时赶得正巧，他看到了韦特的眼睛突然一亮，然后立刻暗淡下去，好像一阵强风一下子吹翻了两盏灯。一根红线似的东西从嘴角挂到下巴——他已经停止了呼吸。 He slipped the key under the pillow again, avoiding to glance at Jimmy, who had not moved.He turned his back squarely from the bunk, and started to the door as though he were going to walk a mile.At his second stride he had his nose against it. He clutched the handle cautiously, but at that moment he received the irresistible impression of something happening behind his back.He spun round as though he had been tapped on the shoulder.He was just in time to see Wait’s eyes blaze up and go out at once, like two lamps overturned together by a sweeping blow.Something resembling a scarlet thread hung down his chin out of the corner of his lips—and he had ceased to breathe.
唐金蹑手蹑脚镇定自若地带上身后的房门。沉睡的人们，和衣挤在一起，在灯光照亮的甲板上，形成了一个个奇形怪状的黑色土丘，那外形仿佛是一座座无人看管的坟墓。虽然一整夜他什么活也没干，可也不曾引起别人的注意。他纹丝不动地站着，异常吃惊地发现舱外的世界和他离开前一模一样；依然是大海，轮船——船上睡眠的人；他对这种现象产生了荒诞的想法，仿佛他原想人都死光了，一切熟悉的东西都消失得无影无踪：正如流浪者多年后回到故里，预计会见到种种令人惊诧的变化。一阵清新的空气袭来，他禁不住瑟瑟发抖，于是只得孤零零地抱紧双臂。悲伤的月亮在西边天际缓缓落下，仿佛是触到了清冷的淡淡晨光而变得萎靡不振。轮船进入了梦乡。不朽的大海延伸开来，朦朦胧胧，无边无际，就像人生的形象，闪闪发光的外表下面隐藏着黑暗的深渊。唐金仿佛受到了威严肃穆威力无限的大海的审判后，被放逐了，他鄙夷地瞥了大海一眼，便悄无声息地溜走了。 Donkin closed the door behind him gently but firmly.Sleeping men, huddled under jackets, made on the lighted deck shapeless dark mounds that had the appearance of neglected graves.Nothing had been done all through the night and he hadn’t been missed.He stood motionless and perfectly astounded to find the world outside as he had left it; there was the sea, the ship—sleeping men; and he wondered absurdly at it, as though he had expected to find the men dead, familiar things gone for ever: as though, like a wanderer returning after many years, he had expected to see bewildering changes.He shuddered a little in the penetrating freshness of the air, and hugged himself forlornly.The declining moon drooped sadly in the western board as if withered by the cold touch of a pale dawn.The ship slept.And the immortal sea stretched away immense and hazy, like the image of life, with a glittering surface and lightless depths.Donkin gave it a defiant glance and slunk off noiselessly as if judged and cast out by the august silence of its might.
吉米的死毕竟太让人吃惊了。直到那时我们才明白我们多么相信他那虚幻的念头。关于他能否生存下去，我们的看法完全源于他自己的估计，结果，他一死，就像一个古老信仰的破灭一样，撼动了我们这个群体的基础。一根共同的纽带消失了；这个纽带由多愁善感的吉米编织而成：牢固、有效、可信。一整天，我们干活时都无精打采，满脸疑惑，如梦方醒。我们心想，在他去世这件事上，他表现得太反常，不够意思。他未能像真正的船友那样给予我们应有的帮助。他去了，带走了他那忧郁严肃的影子；在这影子中，我们的愚念，曾经扮演过命运的温和裁判，并为自己表现出的人情味而沾沾自喜。可现在我们明白了，根本不是那么回事。那不过是寻常的愚蠢行为；是对重大问题愚昧而徒然的干涉——换言之，假设波德莫尔的话是对的。也许他说的还真不错吧？吉米死了，疑问还没得到解决；就像一伙受到一点点恩惠就四分五裂的罪犯，我们彼此无情地诽谤对方。有些人对自己最要好的朋友也说起了难听话。还有人干脆什么也不说。只有辛格尔顿毫不吃惊。“死啦——是吧？当然了。”他说，顺手指着横在面前的岛屿：虽然弗洛斯岛就在眼前，可平静的大海却依然用咒语镇住了轮船。死了——当然了。他——一点都不吃惊。这边是陆地，那边，前舱口盖板上停着死尸，等待修帆工的到来。这就是因果关系。在这次航行中，这位老海员第一次变得兴奋起来，唠唠叨叨说个不停，根据他的丰富经验，举例说明病人看到岛屿（即便是小岛）往往比看到大陆更危险。可他无法解释其中的原因。 Jimmy’s death, after all, came as a tremendous surprise.We did not know till then how much faith we had put in his delusions.We had taken his chances of life so much at his own valuation that his death, like the death of an old belief, shook the foundations of our society.A common bond was gone; the strong, effective and respectable bond of a sentimental he.All that day we mooned at our work, with suspicious looks and a disabused air.In our hearts we thought that in the matter of his departure Jimmy had acted in a perverse and unfriendly manner.He didn’t back us up, as a shipmate should.In going he took away with himself the gloomy and solemn shadow in which our folly had posed, with humane satisfaction, as a tender arbiter of fate.And now we saw it was no such thing.It was just common foolishness; a silly and ineffectual meddling with issues of majestic import—that is, if Podmore was right.Perhaps he was?Doubt survived Jimmy; and, like a community of banded criminals disintegrated by a touch of grace, we were profoundly scandalised with each other.Men spoke unkindly to their best chums.Others refused to speak at all.Singleton only was not surprised.“Dead—is he?Of course,” he said, pointing at the island right abeam: for the calm still held the ship spell-bound within sight of Flores.Dead—of course.—He— wasn’t surprised.Here was the land, and there, on the fore-hatch and waiting for the sailmaker—there was that corpse.Cause and effect.And for the first time that voyage, the old seaman became quite cheery and garrulous, explaining and illustrating from the stores of experience how, in sickness, the sight of an island (even a very small one) is generally more fatal than the view of a continent.But he couldn’t explain why.
吉米下午五点下葬，此前的时间这么漫长——弄得一整天心神不宁，甚至身体也跟着难受。我们无心干活，自然受到了责骂。在我们时刻遭受饥饿困扰的情况下，这更易于激怒大家。唐金干活时额头上缠着一缕破布，脸色苍白，贝克先生看到他忍着病痛干活，深受感动，不禁产生了怜悯之心。“咳！你，唐金！放下活计，这个班你不用值了，躺下休息休息。看起来你像是病了。”“我不舒服，先生——是头部。”他声音低低地说，话音刚落，人就不见了。这下可惹恼了不少人，他们认为大副“今天真他妈的心软。”这时，大家看见阿里斯顿船长正在船尾楼上观望西南方的天空，很快甲板上的人们都知道，气压计昨天夜里已经开始下降，预计不久便会起风。这事经过奇妙联想，又引发了激烈争论：吉米到底是几时死的。是在“玻璃计开始下降”之前还是之后？这已经无从考证，于是大家不屑地对着对方大声嚷嚷。突然前面一阵骚动。性情温和的诺尔斯和好脾气的戴维斯为这事竟动了手。下面的值班人员也精神抖擞地加入到了这场混战，人们在舱口盖板周围吵吵闹闹，扭扭打打，一直闹腾了十来分钟。舱盖上面，飘忽不定的帆影下，停放着吉米的遗体，周身裹着白色的被单，悲痛的贝尔法斯特在旁边守候；他内心充满了忧伤，对周围的吵闹嗤之以鼻。当吵闹声停止，盛怒归于阴郁的沉默时，他站在裹着的遗体头部，高高举起双臂，痛苦愤恨地叫道：“你们应该为自己的行为感到耻辱！”事实的确如此。 Jimmy was to be buried at five, and it was a long day till then—a day of mental disquiet and even of physical disturbance.We took no interest in our work and, very properly, were rebuked for it.This, in our constant state of hungry irritation, was exasperating.Donkin worked with his brow bound in a dirty rag, and looked so ghastly that Mr. Baker was touched with compassion at the sight of this plucky suffering.—“Ough!You, Donkin!Put down your work and go lay-up this watch.You look ill.”—“I am bad, sir—in my ’ead,” he said in a subdued voice, and vanished speedily.This annoyed many, and they thought the mate “bloomin’ soft to-day.” Captain Allistoun could be seen on the poop watching the sky to the southwest, and it soon got to be known about the decks that the barometer had begun to fall in the night, and that a breeze might be expected before long.This, by a subtle association of ideas, led to violent quarrelling as to the exact moment of Jimmy’s death.Was it before or after “that ’ere glass started down?” It was impossible to know, and it caused much contemptuous growling at one another.All of a sudden there was a great tumult forward.Pacific Knowles and good-tempered Davis had come to blows over it.The watch below interfered with spirit, and for ten minutes there was a noisy scrimmage round the hatch, where, in the balancing shade of the sails, Jimmy’s body, wrapped up in a white blanket, was watched over by the sorrowful Belfast, who, in his desolation, disdained the fray.When the noise had ceased, and the passions had calmed into surly silence, he stood up at the head of the swathed body, lifting both arms on high, cried with pained indignation:—“You ought to be ashamed of yourselves!...” We were.
贝尔法斯特对朋友的去世深表痛心。他有充分证据证明他那不可磨灭的忠心。只有他，别无他人，帮助修帆工为吉米遗体的庄重海葬仪式做准备，把他葬入贪得无厌的大海。他在吉米脚边精心准备了一些重物：两块甲板磨石，一个废旧的没有穿轴的锚船链扣，几节断裂磨破的尾锚链。他把这些东西这样摆摆，那样放放。“上帝保佑！你不怕磨破了他的脚跟？”一向讨厌自己工作的修帆工说。他一边缝，一边狠命地抽烟，整个脑袋笼罩在烟雾中；他把垂下的布边翻过来，把针脚拉平，展开帆布。“提起他的肩膀……往你那边拉一点儿……好——好——好。稳住。”贝尔法斯特遵照吩咐，拉呀，提呀，痛苦得难以支撑，泪珠噼里啪啦滴到了涂过焦油的麻线上。“可别把他可怜的脸上的帆布缝得太紧了，修帆工。”他泪眼汪汪地恳求道。“你为什么操这么多心？他够舒服的了。”修帆工向他保证道，他在吉米额头的中间缝完最后一针，便截断了线。他把余下的帆布卷起来，把针收好。“啥事使你这样伤心？”他问。贝尔法斯特低头看看灰色帆布缝成的长袋子。“是我把他拖出来的，”他小声说道，“他并不愿走。要是我昨天夜里不睡觉，一直陪他的话，由我照顾，他也许现在还活着呢……可我干活太累了。”　修帆工使劲地吸着烟，嘴里喃喃地说道：“当我在……西印度驻地……布兰奇——巡洋舰……黄热病流行……一周给二十个人缝过……有朴茨茅斯—德文港的人——城里人——我认识他们的父母、姐妹、家里所有的人。可我并没有多想什么。像他这样的黑人——你连他打哪儿来都不知道。没有一个亲属。谁也用不着他。谁会想他呢？”“我会——我把他拖了出来。”贝尔法斯特忧伤地说。 Belfast took his bereavement very hard.He gave proofs of unextinguishable devotion.It was he, and no other man, who would help the sailmaker to prepare what was left of Jimmy for a solemn surrender to the insatiable sea.He arranged the weights carefully at the feet: two holystones, an old anchor-shackle without its pin, some broken links of a worn-out stream cable.He arranged them this way, then that.“Bless my soul! you aren’t afraid he will chafe his heel?” said the sailmaker, who hated the job.He pushed the needle, purring furiously, with his head in a cloud of tobacco smoke; he turned the flaps over, pulled at the stitches, stretched at the canvas.—“Lift his shoulders....Pull to you a bit....So—o—o. Steady.”Belfast obeyed, pulled, lifted, overcome with sorrow, dropping tears on the tarred twine.—.“Don’t you drag the canvas too taut over his poor face, Sails,” he entreated, tearfully.—“What are you fashing yourself for?He will be comfortable enough,” assured the sailmaker, cutting the thread after the last stitch, which came about the middle of Jimmy’s forehead.He rolled up the remaining canvas, put away the needles.“What makes you take on so?” he asked.Belfast looked down at the long package of grey sailcloth.—“I pulled him out,” he whispered, “and he did not want to go. If I had sat up with him last night he would have kept alive for me...but something made me tired.” The sailmaker took vigorous draws at his pipe and mumbled:—“When I...West India Station...In the —Blanche— frigate...Yellow Jack...sewed in twenty men a week...Portsmouth-Devon-port men—townies—knew their fathers, mothers, sisters—the whole boiling of ’em.Thought nothing of it.And these niggers like this one—you don’t know where it comes from.Got nobody.No use to nobody.Who will miss him?”—“I do—I pulled him out,” mourned Belfast dismally.
詹姆斯·韦特服服帖帖地躺在两块钉子钉起来的木板上，身上盖着折了几折的带有白边的英国国旗，被四个人抬到了船尾，慢慢放下去，双脚对着一个开着的舷孔。一个巨浪从西面打来，随着轮船的剧烈震动，挂在半桅处的红船旗，在灰色天空的映衬下，突然展开，然后又垂了下来，仿佛是一片闪烁的火舌；查利敲响了丧钟；每次船身向右舷倾斜时，就能看到那边完整的半圆形的威猛巨浪，直扑舷孔边沿，似乎在迫不及待地将吉米夺走。除了唐金病得厉害，没有到场外，其他人都到齐了；船长和克赖顿先生光着头站在船尾楼边沿；船长郑重地告诉贝克先生：“你比我更熟悉祈祷书。”他听后，迅速走出船舱，显得有些尴尬。所有人都将帽子脱下。贝克仍用他平日无恶意却有点骇人的语气，开始低声诵读，似乎最后一次对脚边这位已故海员进行推心置腹的劝诫。 On two planks nailed together and apparently resigned and still under the folds of the Union Jack with a white border, James Wait, carried aft by four men, was deposited slowly, with his feet pointing at an open port.A swell had set in from the westward, and following on the roll of the ship, the red ensign, at half-mast, darted out and collapsed again on the grey sky, like a tongue of flickering fire; Charley tolled the bell; and at every swing to starboard the whole vast semi-circle of steely waters visible on that side seemed to come up with a rush to the edge of the port, as if impatient to get at our Jimmy.Every one was there but Donkin, who was too ill to come; the Captain and Mr. Creighton stood bareheaded on the break of the poop; Mr. Baker, directed by the master, who had said to him gravely:—“You know more about the prayer book than I do,” came out of the cabin door quickly and a little embarrassed.All the caps went off. He began to read in a low tone, and with his usual harmlessly menacing utterance, as though he had been for the last time reproving confidentially that dead seaman at his feet.
人们三五成群地站在那里听着；有人倚在卷帆索座台上，凝视着甲板；有人托着下巴，若有所思，或者，双臂交叉，单腿微曲地站着，低着头，似乎正在冥想什么。沃米博似在做梦。贝克先生仍在诵读，每次翻页都会恭敬地咕哝一声。这些祈祷词并未进入人们不安的心灵，而是飘散开来，漫无目的地徘徊在无情的大海上；詹姆斯·韦特永远沉默了，躺在这低沉的充满绝望与希望的嘶哑声中，听之任之，毫无怨言。 The men listened in scattered groups; they leaned on the fife rail, gazing on the deck; they held their chins in their hands thoughtfully, or, with crossed arms and one knee slightly bent, hung their heads in an attitude of upright meditation.Wamibo dreamed.Mr. Baker read on, grunting reverently at the turn of every page.The words, missing the unsteady hearts of men, rolled out to wander without a home upon the heartless sea; and James Wait, silenced for ever, lay uncritical and passive under the hoarse murmur of despair and hopes.
两名水手做好准备，只等曾经送别我们许许多多兄弟最后一跳的祈祷词。贝克先生开始诵读这一段落。“准备。”水手长低声命令。贝克先生读道：“交给大海。”然后停了一下。水手们提起木板的内侧，水手长伸手揭去国旗，詹姆斯·韦特一动不动。“抬高点。”水手长愤怒地嘟囔道。所有人都抬起了头，显得局促不安，可詹姆斯·韦特却没有一点离去的迹象。死后裹了起来，要被送往永恒的世界，可他似乎仍怀着无限的恐惧，紧紧抓住轮船不愿离去。“再高些！抬呀！”水手长用低低的声音凶狠地命令道。“他不愿走。”其中一人结结巴巴地说，浑身都在哆嗦，二人似乎都恨不得扔下不管。 Two men made ready and waited for those words that send so many of our brothers to their last plunge.Mr. Baker began the passage.“Stand by,” muttered the boatswain.Mr. Baker read out: “To the deep,” and paused.The men lifted the inboard end of the planks, the boatswain snatched off the Union Jack, and James Wait did not move.—“Higher,” muttered the boatswain angrily.All the heads were raised; every man stirred uneasily, but James Wait gave no sign of going.In death and swathed up for all eternity, he yet seemed to cling to the ship with the grip of an undying fear.“Higher!Lift!” whispered the boatswain, fiercely.—“He won’t go,” stammered one of the men, shakily, and both appeared ready to drop everything.
贝克先生把脸埋在书中等候，焦躁地蹭着双脚。人们个个显得十分不安，人群中传出了一阵嗡嗡声——声音越来越大……“吉米！”贝尔法斯特哭着叫道，人群中顿时一片惊恐。 Mr. Baker waited, burying his face in the book, and shuffling his feet nervously.All the men looked profoundly disturbed; from their midst a faint humming noise spread out—growing louder....“Jimmy!” cried Belfast in a wailing tone, and there was a second of shuddering dismay.
“吉米，做个男子汉！”他动情地尖叫道。人人都把嘴张得大大的，眼睛一眨不眨。他发疯似地直勾勾地盯着眼前的一切，浑身哆嗦；他弯腰前倾，像在仔细观察一个恐惧场面。“去吧！”他叫道，伸着一只胳膊，跳出人群。“去吧，吉米！——吉米，去吧！去吧！”他用手指点了一下尸体的头，于是，那灰色的包裹便发出不情愿的嘶嘶声，一下子从抬起的木板上滑落下去，像闪电一样突然。人群齐刷刷地涌上前，只听一声深沉的“啊——”震颤着从一个个宽阔的胸中奔腾而出。轮船晃动着，仿佛卸掉了本不该承载的负担；帆蓬不住地拍打着。贝尔法斯特在阿奇的搀扶下，歇斯底里地喘着气；查利慌着要看吉米最后一次潜水，猛然冲向船栏，结果还是迟了，除了正在消失的一圈圈波纹，什么也没看到。 “Jimmy, be a man!” he shrieked, passionately.Every mouth was wide open, not an eyelid winked.He stared wildly, twitching all over; he bent his body forward like a man peering at an horror.“Go!” he shouted, and sprang out of the crowd with his arm extended.“Go, Jimmy!—Jimmy, go!Go!” His fingers touched the head of the body, and the grey package started reluctantly to whizz off the lifted planks all at once, with the suddenness of a flash of lightning.The crowd stepped forward like one man; a deep Ah—h—h! came out vibrating from the broad chests.The ship rolled as if relieved of an unfair burden; the sails flapped.Belfast, supported by Archie, gasped hysterically; and Charley, who anxious to see Jimmy’s last dive, leaped headlong on the rail, was too late to see anything but the faint circle of a vanishing ripple.
贝克先生汗流浃背，在兴奋的人们和不停拍打着的帆蓬的窃窃私语中，读完了最后一句祈祷词。“阿门！”他用颤抖的声音大声念道，然后合上了书。 Mr. Baker, perspiring abundantly, read out the last prayer in a deep rumour of excited men and fluttering sails.“Amen!” he said in an unsteady growl, and closed the book.
“把帆桁扯平！”他头顶上响起了雷鸣般的吼声。人人都大吃一惊；有一两个连帽子都吓掉了；贝克先生惊恐地抬头张望。只见船长站在船尾楼边沿，指着西方。“快起风啦，”他说，“快拉上风转桁索。”贝克先生连忙把书塞进口袋。“喂，向前——解开前下角索！”他兴奋地吩咐着，头上没戴帽子，动作十分敏捷，“拉平前桅下帆桁，你们几位左舷值班员！”“顺风——顺风。”人们念叨着奔向转桁索。“我是怎么说的？”辛格尔顿咕哝着，赶忙使劲把一盘盘绳子扔下来，“我早就知道——他一死，风就来了。” “Square the yards!” thundered a voice above his head.All hands gave a jump; one or two dropped their caps; Mr. Baker looked up surprised.The master, standing on the break of the poop, pointed to the westward.“Breeze coming,” he said, “Man the weather braces.” Mr. Baker crammed the book hurriedly into his pocket.“Forward, there—let go the foretack!” he hailed joyfully, bareheaded and brisk; “Square the foreyard, you port-watch!”—“Fair wind—fair wind,” muttered the men going to the braces.—“What did I tell you?” mumbled old Singleton, flinging down coil after coil with hasty energy; “I knowed it—he’s gone, and here it comes.”
海风刮起来了，带着高傲、雄浑的嗟叹。帆蓬吹得鼓鼓的，轮船加速前进，苏醒的大海睡眼惺忪地在人们耳边呢喃着家乡的故事。 It came with the sound of a lofty and powerful sigh.The sails filled, the ship gathered way, and the waking sea began to murmur sleepily of home to the ears of men.
那天夜里，轮船在越刮越猛的狂风吹拂下，荡着白沫，疾速向北行驶，水手长面对着普通船员的床铺，向大伙倾诉了自己的心事：“那家伙真是个累赘，”他说，“从他上船那一刻起——你们还记得吧——在孟买那天夜里？老欺负一帮好说话的哥们儿——顶撞老头子——我们还得在沉没一半的船上跑来跑去救他。妈的，为了他差点出了大乱子——可现在大副却怪我，好像我是扒手，忘了在两块木板上涂油。不错，我是给忘了，可你们不该糊涂呀，上面怎能竖着钉子——喂，木匠？” That night, while the ship rushed foaming to the Northward before a freshening gale, the boatswain unbosomed himself to the petty officers’ berth:—“The chap was nothing but trouble,” he said, “from the moment he came aboard—d’ye remember—that night in Bombay?Been bullying all that softy crowd—cheeked the old man—we had to go fooling all over a half-drowned ship to save him. Dam’ nigh a mutiny all for him—and now the mate abused me like a pickpocket for forgetting to dab a lump of grease on them planks.So I did, but you ought to have known better, too, than to leave a nail sticking up—hey, Chips?”
“你们也不该犯混呀，为了他，怎能将我所有的工具都扔到海里去，真像一个无用的傻瓜。”郁闷的木匠反唇相讥。“唉——他现在也随它们去了。”他接着说，语气里毫无宽恕的意思，“在中国驻地，我记得有一次，海军上将对我说……”修帆工打开了话匣子。 “And you ought to have known better than to chuck all my tools overboard for ’im, like a skeary greenhorn,” retorted the morose carpenter.“Well—he’s gone after ’em now,” he added in an unforgiving tone.—“On the China Station, I remember once, the Admiral he says to me...” began the sailmaker.
一周后，“水仙号”驶进了英吉利海峡的入口。 A week afterwards the—Narcissus—entered the chops of the Channel.
在白色的翅膀下，她低低地掠过蔚蓝的大海，仿佛一只飞累的巨鸟快速飞回自己的巢中。云团在和桅顶赛跑；它们在船尾升起，白白的，硕大无比，骤然飞到天空顶端，越过轮船，在辽阔的苍穹中飘落，似乎猛然冲向大海——白云比轮船更快，更逍遥，可它们却无家可归。欢迎她的海岸走上前来，迈进阳光中。巍峨的海岬威严地步入大海；宽阔的海湾在阳光下绽放微笑；无家可归的白云的阴影，沿着阳光普照的原野在飞奔，越过山谷，轻松地跨上山岗，滚下斜坡；阳光发出片片飞奔的亮光追赶它们。阴沉的峭壁边缘，白色的灯塔光柱般闪耀。英吉利海峡光彩熠熠，仿佛蓝色的斗篷织入了金色丝线，点缀着竞相比高的银色海浪。“水仙号”疾速行驶，越过了一个个海岬和港湾。出海的船只横穿她走过的尚未消失的痕迹，撤掉帆蓬的桅杆在与猛烈的西南风进行着一场殊死搏斗。岸边，一连串冒着烟的汽船摇摇摆摆，紧贴海岸，好像要迁徙的两栖怪兽，对躁动不安的波浪充满了疑惑。 Under white wings she skimmed low over the blue sea like a great tired bird speeding to its nest.The clouds raced with her mastheads; they rose astern enormous and white, soared to the zenith, flew past, and falling down the wide curve of the sky, seemed to dash headlong into the sea—the clouds swifter than the ship, more free, but without a home.The coast to welcome her stepped out of space into the sunshine.The lofty headlands trod masterfully into the sea; the wide bays smiled in the light; the shadows of homeless clouds ran along the sunny plains, leaped over valleys, without a check darted up the hills, rolled down the slopes; and the sunshine pursued them with patches of running brightness.On the brows of dark cliffs white lighthouses shone in pillars of light.The Channel glittered like a blue mantle shot with gold and starred by the silver of the capping seas.The—Narcissus—rushed past the headlands and the bays.Outward-bound vessels crossed her track, lying over, and with their masts stripped for a slogging fight with the hard sou’wester.And, inshore, a string of smoking steamboats waddled, hugging the coast, like migrating and amphibious monsters, distrustful of the restless waves.
夜里，海岬隐去了，港湾延伸开来，形成了一条完整朦胧的长线。地上的灯光与天上的星光交相辉映；捕鱼船队摇摇晃晃的灯笼上方，雄伟的灯塔一眨不眨地闪耀着，仿佛一盏巨大的锚泊灯燃烧在一艘奇大无比的轮船上。在灯塔始终如一的强光照射下，暗淡的海岸笔直地伸展开来，犹如一艘牢不可破的巨轮凸起的一侧，停泊在这永恒不变、永无安宁的大海上。深色的大陆孤零零地躺在大海中间，像一艘雄伟的巨轮，点缀着无数的警灯——一艘承载着千万个生命的船——一艘装有糟粕与珠宝、黄金与钢铁的船。这艘船巍然屹立，无边无际，坚强有力，守卫着有趣的传说和无限的痛苦，庇护着光荣的记忆和低劣的疏忽、卑贱的品行以及辉煌的罪恶。一艘多么了不起的船！多少年来，大海一直磨损不了她那坚韧的边缘；当世界更辽阔更黑暗的时候，当浩瀚神秘的大海把荣誉的奖品送给勇敢的人类的时候，她已经矗立在那里了。她不愧为舰队和民族的母舰！人类伟大的旗舰，比暴风雨还坚强！停泊在茫茫的大海之中。 At night the headlands retreated, the bays advanced into one unbroken line of gloom.The lights of the earth mingled with the lights of heaven; and above the tossing lanterns of a trawling fleet a great lighthouse shone steadily, like an enormous riding light burning above a vessel of fabulous dimensions.Below its steady glow, the coast, stretching away straight and black, resembled the high side of an indestructible craft riding motionless upon the immortal and unresting sea.The dark land lay alone in the midst of waters, like a mighty ship bestarred with vigilant lights—a ship carrying the burden of millions of lives—a ship freighted with dross and with jewels, with gold and with steel.She towered up immense and strong, guarding priceless traditions and untold suffering, sheltering glorious memories and base forgetfulness, ignoble virtues and splendid transgressions.A great ship!For ages had the ocean battered in vain her enduring sides; she was there when the world was vaster and darker, when the sea was great and mysterious, and ready to surrender the prize of fame to audacious men. A ship mother of fleets and nations!The great flagship of the race; stronger than the storms! and anchored in the open sea.
“水仙号”迎着海岸狂风，侧着身子绕过南弗兰德，穿过唐斯，被拖船拖着，驶入泰晤士河。她卸去荣耀的白色翅膀，顺从地跟在拖船后面，左拐右绕，行驶在弯弯曲曲迷宫般的河道上。当她迤逦前行时，固定在停泊处的漆得通红的灯船摇曳着，似乎在潮水的冲击下，一下子要飞行起来，然后又绝望地被抛在后面。系在河岸突出位置上的巨大浮标低低滑过她的两侧，在她的尾流中起伏，仿佛一只凶猛的看门狗被锁链拉着。河面越来越窄；两边陆地离船越来越近。她稳步逆流而上。在河畔斜坡上，出现了一簇簇房子——它们似乎要顺着斜坡猛冲下来观看她的通过，可被前滩的污泥挡住了去路，只得聚集在岸边。再往前走，高耸林立的工厂烟囱露出了傲慢的面孔，注视着她从面前驶过，它们仿佛一群掉队的高个巨人，大摇大摆，昂首挺胸，骑士般侧身站在直如铅锤的漆黑浓烟下。她沿着弯曲的河道，拂掠着两岸徐行；一股浊风在光光的桅杆和陆地之间奏响了一支刺耳的欢迎曲；陆地越来越近，最终走到了船和海的中间。 The—Narcissus—, heeling over to off-shore gusts, rounded the South Foreland, passed through the Downs, and, in tow, entered the river.Shorn of the glory of her white wings, she wound obediently after the tug through the maze of invisible channels.As she passed them the red-painted light-vessels, swung at their moorings, seemed for an instant to sail with great speed in the rush of tide, and the next moment were left hopelessly behind.The big buoys on the tails of banks slipped past her sides very low, and, dropping in her wake, tugged at their chains like fierce watchdogs.The reach narrowed; from both sides the land approached the ship.She went steadily up the river.On the riverside slopes the houses appeared in groups—seemed to stream down the declivities at a run to see her pass, and, checked by the mud of the foreshore, crowded on the banks.Further on, the tall factory chimneys appeared in insolent bands and watched her go by, like a straggling crowd of slim giants, swaggering and upright under the black plummets of smoke, cavalierly aslant.She swept round the bends; an impure breeze shrieked a welcome between her stripped spars; and the land, closing in, stepped between the ship and the sea.
一片云朵低低地挂在她的面前——这片浮动着的乳白色云朵，似乎是从千百万个冒着热气的额头上升腾起来的。不断升起的烟雾漂浮在空中，给那片云朵染上了一条青灰色尾巴；云儿随着千万颗心脏的跳动而飘荡，从中传来了无边无际的哀伤低语——这低语是千万人在祈祷，在诅咒，在叹息，在嘲弄——这低语经久不息，是焦躁不安的尘世在表达愚念、遗憾和希望。“水仙号”驶进了云中；阴影变得更深；四周充满着钢铁的叮当声、有力的锤击声、尖叫声、呐喊声。乌黑的驳船悄无声息地漂泊在朦胧的河上。横七竖八，污秽不堪的一堵堵墙壁，在迷蒙的烟雾中隐约可见，仿佛一副灾后景象，令人迷茫和悲伤。多条拖船后退，聚集于河道，以便在船坞闸门口保持这艘船船身稳定；船头处，两条绳子嗖的一声从空中飞过，恶狠狠地落在地上，活像两条蛇。她前面的一座桥，仿佛中了魔法，从中间截然断开；几个大型水力绞盘自动地旋转起来，好像是神秘的邪咒给了它们活力。她在狭窄的水道里穿行，两边是低矮的花岗岩石壁，人们手里拽着防松索，与她同步走在宽阔的石板上。一群人焦急地等待在隐去的桥两侧：几个戴无边帽的粗笨男子，一些戴高顶礼帽脸色发黄的男子，两位没戴帽子的妇人，几个衣衫褴褛的孩子。他们出于好奇，个个都把眼睛睁得大大的。一辆二轮马车颠簸着跑了过来，猛然停下。两个妇人中的一个对着静静的船儿突然尖声叫道——“喂，杰克！”她并未专注地看谁，而前甲板上的水手们却都在看她。“站远点！不要踩住绳子！”带缆工俯身在石柱上叫道。人群喁喁细语，站在那里直跺脚。“松开后甲板防松索！松呀！”码头上一个红脸老汉大声嚷嚷。只听“砰”的一声，绳子重重地落入水中，于是，“水仙号”驶入了船坞。 A low cloud hung before her—a great opalescent and tremulous cloud, that seemed to rise from the steaming brows of millions of men.Long drifts of smoky vapours soiled it with livid trails; it throbbed to the beat of millions of hearts, and from it came an immense and lamentable murmur—the murmur of millions of lips praying, cursing, sighing, jeering—the undying murmur of folly, regret, and hope exhaled by the crowds of the anxious earth.The—Narcissus—entered the cloud; the shadows deepened; on all sides there was the clang of iron, the sound of mighty blows, shrieks, yells.Black barges drifted stealthily on the murky stream.A mad jumble of begrimed walls loomed up vaguely in the smoke, bewildering and mournful, like a vision of disaster.The tugs backed and filled in the stream, to hold the ship steady at the dock-gates; from her bows two lines went through the air whistling, and struck at the land viciously, like a pair of snakes.A bridge broke in two before her, as if by enchantment; big hydraulic capstans began to turn all by themselves, as though animated by a mysterious and unholy spell.She moved through a narrow lane of water between two low walls of granite, and men with check-ropes in their hands kept pace with her, walking on the broad flagstones.A group waited impatiently on each side of the vanished bridge: rough heavy men in caps; sallow-faced men in high hats; two bareheaded women; ragged children, fascinated, and with wide eyes.A cart coming at a jerky trot pulled up sharply.One of the women screamed at the silent ship—“Hallo, Jack!” without looking at any one in particular, and all hands looked at her from the forecastle head.—“Stand clear!Stand clear of that rope!” cried the dockmen, bending over stone posts.The crowd murmured, stamped where they stood.—“Let go your quarter-checks!Let go!” sang out a ruddy-faced old man on the quay.The ropes splashed heavily falling in the water, and the—Narcissus—entered the dock.
石岸成直线左右分开，围成一个昏暗的矩形池子。砖墙高耸在水面之上！毫无生气的墙上开着数百个窗口，仿佛饱餐后野兽的眼睛，显得不安和呆滞。墙根处，蹲伏着钢筋铁骨般的巨型起重机，长长的铁臂上垂着锁链，外观狰狞的吊钩稳稳当当地悬在死气沉沉的轮船甲板上。轮盘在石头上转动的嗡嗡声，重物落下的咚咚声，发烧的卷扬机的哼哼声，拉紧的铁链的嘎吱声一齐在空中震响。高大建筑物之间，来自各大洲的尘土争相飞扬；香水与灰尘，香料与皮革，贵重的货物与低劣的商品，各种东西的气味混杂一起，弥漫空中，刺鼻难闻，使得这里的氛围极其难得又令人讨厌。“水仙号”悠然来到了她的停泊处；无精打采的墙的阴影笼罩着她，各大洲的尘土落在她的甲板上，一群陌生人爬上她的两弦，以肮脏陆地的名义占有了她。她的生命到此结束。 The stony shores ran away right and left in straight lines, enclosing a sombre and rectangular pool.Brick walls rose high above the water!—soulless walls, staring through hundreds of windows as troubled and dull as the eyes of over-fed brutes.At their base monstrous iron cranes crouched, with chains hanging from their long necks, balancing cruel-looking hooks over the decks of lifeless ships.A noise of wheels rolling over stones, the thump of heavy things falling, the racket of feverish winches, the grinding of strained chains, floated on the air. Between high buildings the dust of all the continents soared in short flights; and a penetrating smell of perfumes and dirt, of spices and hides, of things costly and of things filthy, pervaded the space, made for it an atmosphere precious and disgusting.The—Narcissus—came gently into her berth; the shadows of soulless walls fell upon her, the dust of all the continents leaped upon her deck, and a swarm of strange men, clambering up her sides, took possession of her in the name of the sordid earth.She had ceased to live.
一个身着黑大衣，头戴高顶礼帽的人敏捷地登上船，来到二副面前与他握手，说道：“你好，赫伯特。”　这是他哥哥。突然，一位贵妇出现了。一位真正的贵妇，穿着黑色礼服，打着一把阳伞。她站在我们中间，显得极其优雅，如此超凡脱俗，仿佛是刚从天上下凡。贝克先生轻拍帽子向她致意。原来这是船长夫人。不一会儿，船长打扮得漂漂亮亮，穿着白衬衣，和她一道跨过船舷。我们简直认不出他了，直到他走上码头，才回头招呼贝克先生：“明天早上别忘了给经线仪上满劲。”一群偷偷摸摸衣衫褴褛的家伙在水手舱内外探头探脑，转来转去，想找点活干——他们这样说。“更像是想偷点东西。”诺尔斯兴奋地议论道。可怜的乞丐。谁还操心呢？反正我们已经到家啦！可贝克先生却好好收拾了其中一个，因为他对贝克先生无礼，我们觉得非常开心。一切事情都让我们开心。“我把船尾的活干完了，先生。”克赖顿先生大声说道。“井里没水了，先生。”木匠最后一次报告，手里还拿着测深杆。贝克先生顺着甲板扫视了一眼这群满怀期望的水手，又向空中望了一眼帆桁。“咳！好了，伙计们。”他哼哼道。于是，大家解散。航程结束了。 A toff in a black coat and high hat scrambled with agility, came up to the second mate, shook hands, and said:—“Hallo, Herbert.”It was his brother.A lady appeared suddenly.A real lady, in a black dress and with a parasol.She looked extremely elegant in the midst of us, and as strange as if she had fallen there from the sky.Mr. Baker touched his cap to her.It was the master’s wife.And very soon the Captain, dressed very smartly and in a white shirt, went with her over the side.We didn’t recognise him at all till, turning on the quay, he called to Mr. Baker:—“Don’t forget to wind up the chronometers to-morrow morning.” An underhand lot of seedy-looking chaps with shifty eyes wandered in and out of the forecastle looking for a job—they said.—“More likely for something to steal,” commented Knowles, cheerfully.Poor beggars.Who cared?Weren’t we home!But Mr. Baker went for one of them who had given him some cheek, and we were delighted.Everything was delightful.—“I’ve finished aft, sir,” called out Mr. Creighton.—“No water in the well, sir,” reported for the last time the carpenter, sounding-rod in hand.Mr. Baker glanced along the decks at the expectant group of sailors, glanced aloft at the yards.—“Ough!That will do, men,” he grunted.The group broke up.The voyage was ended.
卷好的铺盖飞过船栏；捆好的箱子从舷梯滑下——此时，这两样东西都所剩无几了。“其余部分还在好望角附近的海上飘着呢。”诺尔斯神秘地对码头流浪汉解释说，这是他刚刚结识的朋友。人们奔跑着，彼此呼喊着，欢快地招呼陌生人“搭把手提提行李”，然后一下子变得彬彬有礼，走到大副面前同他握手后，方才登岸。“再见，先生。”大家用不同的语调重复着这句话。贝克先生握住一只只粗糙的手，哼哼着亲热地同大家一一道别，眼睛闪烁着喜悦的光芒。“诺尔斯，当心你的钱。咳！这样很快就能娶个漂亮太太。”那瘸腿男子被逗得心花怒放。“再见啦，先生。”贝尔法斯特激动地说，他紧紧握着大副的手，泪汪汪地抬头望了一眼。“原以为我会把他带上岸的。”他伤心地接着说道。贝克先生不解其意，只是亲切地说道：“多保重，克雷克。”于是，痛失挚友的贝尔法斯特，孤孤单单地跨过船栏。 Rolled-up beds went flying over the rail; lashed chests went sliding down the gangway—mighty few of both at that.“The rest is having a cruise off the Cape,” explained Knowles enigmatically to a dock-loafer with whom he had struck a sudden friendship.Men ran, calling to one another, hailing utter strangers to “lend a hand with the dunnage,” then with sudden decorum approached the mate to shake hands before going ashore.—“Good-bye, sir,” they repeated in various tones.Mr. Baker grasped hard palms, grunted in a friendly manner at every one, his eyes twinkled.—“Take care of your money, Knowles.Ough! Soon get a nice wife if you do.” The lame man was delighted.—“Good-bye, sir,” said Belfast, with emotion, wringing the mate’s hand, and looked up with swimming eyes.“I thought I would take ’im ashore with me,” he went on, plaintively.Mr. Baker did not understand, but said kindly:—“Take care of yourself, Craik,” and the bereaved Belfast went over the rail mourning and alone.
轮船一下子安静了下来，贝克先生嘴里哼哼着，独自一人踱来踱去，一个个门把手转动转动，一个个黑暗部位察看察看，一直忙个不停——真不愧是模范大副！岸上没人等他。母亲已经过世；父亲和两个兄弟都是雅茅斯渔民，同时在多格滩淹死了；姐姐嫁人了，和他也不怎么亲近。完全是一副贵妇人派头。她嫁给了一个小镇上的裁缝头，也是镇上的主要政客，他觉得他那当海员的内弟有辱于他的门风。真是一个贵妇人，真是一个贵妇人，他心里想着，顺便坐到后甲板升降口盖板上休息片刻。时间还早，待会儿到岸上弄点吃的喝的，再找个住处。他舍不得离开船。他没有人可以想了。雾蒙蒙的黑夜降临了，空荡荡的甲板上阴冷潮湿；贝克先生坐在那里抽烟，回想着多年来他尽心尽力照顾过的每一艘船。可从来也没当过船长。一次也没有！“我大概没有当船长的风度吧。”他平静地想，此时，看船人（他已经占据了船上的厨房），一个眼睛昏花的干瘪老头儿在低声骂他“瞎转什么”。“嗯，克赖顿，”他继续想着心事，毫无嫉妒之意，“真是个绅士……广交朋友……会越来越有出息。真是好小伙子……就差一点经验。”他站起身，抖了抖身子。“我明天回来做的头件事就是取走舱口盖板。看船的，我来之前，可别叫他们动任何东西。”他大声交代着。交代完毕，他也上了岸——真是一位模范大副！ Mr. Baker, in the sudden peace of the ship, moved about solitary and grunting, trying door-handles, peering into dark places, never done—a model chief mate!No one waited for him ashore.Mother dead; father and two brothers, Yarmouth fishermen, drowned together on the Dogger Bank; sister married and unfriendly.Quite a lady.Married to the leading tailor of a little town, and its leading politician, who did not think his sailor brother-in-law quite respectable enough for him.Quite a lady, quite a lady, he thought, sitting down for a moment’s rest on the quarter-hatch.Time enough to go ashore and get a bite and sup, and a bed somewhere.He didn’t like to part with a ship.No one to think about then.The darkness of a misty evening fell, cold and damp, upon the deserted deck; and Mr. Baker sat smoking, thinking of all the successive ships to whom through many long years he had given the best of a seaman’s care.And never a command in sight.Not once!—“I haven’t somehow the cut of a skipper about me,” he meditated, placidly, while the shipkeeper (who had taken possession of the galley), a wizened old man with bleared eyes, cursed him in whispers for “hanging about so.”—“Now, Creighton,” he pursued the unenvious train of thought, “quite a gentleman...swell friends...will get on.Fine young fellow...a little more experience.” He got up and shook himself.“I’ll be back first thing to-morrow morning for the hatches.Don’t you let them touch anything before I come, shipkeeper,” he called out.Then, at last, he also went ashore—a model chief mate!
一踏上陆地就四散的人们，再次在港务局相聚。“‘水仙号’发工钱啦。”玻璃门外一个老头在叫喊，他身穿黄铜纽扣制服，头戴印有王冠和大写“贸易部”字样的帽子。于是，马上就有一批人涌了进去，可也有不少人姗姗来迟。 The men scattered by the dissolving contact of the land came together once more in the shipping office.—“The—Narcissus—pays off,” shouted outside a glazed door a brass-bound old fellow with a crown and the capitals B. T. on his cap. A lot trooped in at once but many were late.
这是一个宽敞的房间，墙壁刷得雪白，里面空荡荡的；柜台上装有铜丝隔离栏，把布满灰尘的空间划去三分之一。隔离栏后面坐着一位脸色苍白的书记员，头发中分，眼睛闪亮灵活，动作敏捷，仿佛一只笼中鸟。可怜的阿里斯顿船长也在里面，他坐在一张小桌前，上面摆着一摞摞金币和钞票；关在里面，他显得有些抑郁。贸易部的另一只鸟栖息在靠近门的一张高凳子上：那是一只老鸟，对兴高采烈的水手们的打趣逗乐无动于衷。“水仙号”的职员们三三两两挤在屋角。个个穿着岸上的新衣服，漂亮的上衣看着像是斧头削过的，光滑的裤子犹如折起的铁板做成，贴身穿的是法兰绒无领衬衣，脚上蹬着锃亮的新皮靴。他们轻拍肩膀，你拉我扯地问道：“昨晚在哪儿睡的？”他们愉快地低声交谈着，时而猛拍大腿，尽管压低了声音，还是忍不住哄堂大笑。多数人干干净净，容光焕发；只有一两位蓬头垢面，满脸愁容；两个年轻的挪威人看起来干净整齐，温和谦恭，完全有希望得到那些惠顾斯堪的纳维亚之家的好心女子的青睐。沃米博仍然穿着工作服，挺着壮实的身躯站在房间中央，像在做梦，阿奇进来时，才如梦方醒，微微一笑。而两个完全清醒的书记员这时大声叫喊名字，于是，发放工钱开始了。 The room was large, white-washed, and bare; a counter surmounted by a brass-wire grating fenced off a third of the dusty space, and behind the grating a pasty-faced clerk, with his hair parted in the middle, had the quick, glittering eyes and the vivacious, jerky movements of a caged bird.Poor Captain Allistoun also in there, and sitting before a little table with piles of gold and notes on it, appeared subdued by his captivity.Another Board of Trade bird was perching on a high stool near the door: an old bird that did not mind the chaff of elated sailors.The crew of the—Narcissus—, broken up into knots, pushed in the corners.They had new shore togs, smart jackets that looked as if they had been shaped with an axe, glossy trousers that seemed made of crumpled sheet-iron, collarless flannel shirts, shiny new boots.They tapped on shoulders, button-holed one another, asked:—“Where did you sleep last night?” whispered gaily, slapped their thighs with bursts of subdued laughter.Most had clean, radiant faces; only one or two turned up dishevelled and sad; the two-young Norwegians looked tidy, meek, and altogether of a promising material for the kind ladies who patronise the Scandinavian Home.Wamibo, still in his working clothes, dreamed, upright and burly in the middle of the room, and, when Archie came in, woke up for a smile.But the wide-awake clerk called out a name, and the paying-off business began.
