他们一个接一个来到柜台前，领取他们那份荣耀而卑微的苦工报酬。他们小心翼翼地将钱一把抓在宽大的手中，深信不疑地塞进裤兜，或转过身背对柜台，吃力地在僵硬的手心里一一清点。“数目对吧？那就在解雇文件上签字吧。那边——那边。”书记员不耐烦地重复着。“这群水手真够笨的！”他想。辛格尔顿庄严地走上前来——白昼的亮光照得他晕头转向；一滴滴棕色烟汁挂在他的白胡子上；他那双在苍茫大海的亮光中从不颤抖的手，竟会在这滨海极度的阴暗中，差点找不到那一小堆金币。“不会写字吗？”书记员问道，心里颇感吃惊。“那就画个记号吧。”于是，辛格尔顿异常费劲地画了一个粗大的十字，把整张纸都给弄脏了。“讨厌的老东西。”那个书记员嘟囔着。有人为他打开门，这位德高望重的老海员跌跌撞撞地走了出去，不曾看我们中间任何人一眼。 One by one they came up to the pay-table to get the wages of their glorious and obscure toil.They swept the money with care into broad palms, rammed it trustfully into trousers’ pockets, or, turning their backs on the table, reckoned with difficulty in the hollow of their stiff hands.—“Money right?Sign the release.There—there,” repeated the clerk, impatiently.“How stupid those sailors are!” he thought.Singleton came up, venerable—and uncertain as to daylight; brown drops of tobacco juice hung in his white beard; his hands, that never hesitated in the great light of the open sea, could hardly find the small pile of gold in the profound darkness of the shore.“Can’t write?” said the clerk, shocked.“Make a mark, then.” Singleton painfully sketched in a heavy cross, blotted the page.“What a disgusting old brute,” muttered the clerk.Somebody opened the door for him, and the patriarchal seaman passed through unsteadily, without as much as a glance at any of us.
阿奇提着一个女式提包。大家同他开玩笑。贝尔法斯特一副放荡不羁的样子，仿佛在一两家旅店里出过风头，此时有些激动，想与船长单独谈谈。船长大吃一惊。他们隔着铜线隔离栏交谈，我们听见船长说：“我把东西都交给贸易部了。”　“我想留点他的什么。”贝尔法斯特嘟囔道。“可是不行啊，伙计。全都交给了海事局，上了锁，贴了封条。”船长规劝道；贝尔法斯特退后几步，嘴角耷拉着，眼睛掠过一丝不安。在发工钱的间歇，我们听到船长和书记员的对话。我们隐约听见：“詹姆斯·韦特——死亡——找不到任何文件——没有亲属——毫无线索——海事局必须代管他的工钱。”　这时，唐金走了进来。他似乎有点喘不过来气，一脸严肃，事务繁忙。他径直走到柜台前，同书记员侃侃而谈，书记员认为他是个精明人。他们讨论着账目，你一言，我一语，仿佛在为赌注的多少而争执不休——其实彼此很友好。阿里斯顿付清了款。“我给你开了一张不太好的解雇证明。”他平静地说。唐金提高嗓门说道：“我才不稀罕你的鬼证明哩——留着自己用吧。我想在岸上找份工作。”他转向我们。“我再也不会到那倒霉的海上去啦。”他大声说道。所有人都注视着他。他穿着稍微讲究的衣服，神情自若，似乎比我们谁都过得更舒心；他满怀自信地注视着大家，尽情享受着他的声明带来的效果。“没错。我有不少有钱朋友。比你们有钱。我是个人。而你们只配当船员。谁愿跟我喝上一杯？“ Archie displayed a pocket-book.He was chaffed.Belfast, who looked wild, as though he had already luffed up through a public-house or two, gave signs of emotion and wanted to speak to the Captain privately.The master was surprised.They spoke through the wires, and we could hear the Captain saying:—“I’ve given it up to the Board of Trade.” “I should ’ve liked to get something of his,” mumbled Belfast.“But you can’t, my man. It’s given up, locked and sealed, to the Marine Office,” expostulated the master; and Belfast stood back, with drooping mouth and troubled eyes.In a pause of the business we heard the master and the clerk talking.We caught: “James Wait—deceased—found no papers of any kind—no relations—no trace—the Office must hold his wages then.” Donkin entered.He seemed out of breath, was grave, full of business.He went straight to the desk, talked with animation to the clerk, who thought him an intelligent man. They discussed the account, dropping h’s against one another as if for a wager—very friendly.Captain Allistoun paid.“I give you a bad discharge,” he said, quietly.Donkin raised his voice:—“I don’t want your bloomin’ discharge—keep it.I’m goin’ ter ’ave a job ashore.” He turned to us.“No more bloomin’ sea fur me,” he said, aloud.All looked at him.He had better clothes, had an easy air, appeared more at home than any of us; he stared with assurance, enjoying the effect of his declaration.“Yuss. I ’ave friends well off.That’s more’n you got.But I am a man. Yer shipmates for all that.Who’s comin fur a drink?”
谁都没动。大家都沉默了，个个一脸茫然，神情呆滞。他等了片刻，苦笑一下，向门口走去。从那儿再次回过头。“都不乐意吗？你们这群伪君子。不是吗？我什么地方得罪过你们？我欺负过你们吗？我伤害过你们吗？有吗？……你们不想喝？……根本不是这回事！……那就等着渴死吧，你们这群混蛋！连臭虫的勇气都没有。尽是世间垃圾。只配干活饿死！” No one moved.There was a silence; a silence of blank faces and stony looks.He waited a moment, smiled bitterly, and went to the door.There he faced round once more.“You won’t?You bloomin’ lot of yrpocrits.No? What ’ave I done to yer?Did I bully yer?Did I ’urt yer?Did I?...You won’t drink?...No!...Then may ye die of thirst, every mother’s son of yer!Not one of yer ’as the sperrit of a bug.Ye’re the scum of the world.Work and starve!”
他走了出去，使劲将门带上，那巨响差点使贸易部的老鸟从栖木上跌落下去。 He went out, and slammed the door with such violence that the old Board of Trade bird nearly fell off his perch.
“他疯啦。”阿奇说。“不！不！他醉了。”贝尔法斯特摇摇晃晃，用伤感的语气坚持道。阿里斯顿船长坐在清空的账桌旁，若有所思地微笑着。 “He’s mad,” declared Archie.“No! No!He’s drunk,” insisted Belfast, lurching about, and in a maudlin tone.Captain Allistoun sat smiling thoughtfully at the cleared pay-table.
到了外面，登上塔山，他们的眼睛不住地眨，脚步也变得迟缓拖沓，仿佛被这陌生的雾光刺花了眼，又像被这人山人海的景象扰乱了心；这些能在吼叫的狂风中听清彼此说话的人们，当面对这纷繁尘世的喧嚣时似乎耳朵聋了，思想乱了。“到黑马饭店去！到黑马饭店去！”有人嚷嚷道，“大家分手前好好喝上一杯。”他们穿过马路，一个跟着一个。只有贝尔法斯特和查利各自走开了。当我走近时，看见一个面色黑红的女人正搂着查利的脖子，只见她系着一条灰色围巾，蓬乱的头发上满是灰尘。那是他母亲。她亲昵地说道：“啊，我的孩子！我的孩子！”“放开我，”查利说，“放开我，妈妈！”　当时我从他身旁经过，他越过那老泪纵横的老太太的脏头，对我滑稽地笑了笑，然后瞥了我一眼，这深沉的目光充满了讥讽和鼓励，使我关于人生的全部认识变得不值一提。我冲他点点头走了过去，可又听到他愉快地说：“要是你这会儿放开我——我会从工钱中拿出一先令给你买酒喝。”又走了几步，我碰见了贝尔法斯特。他热情地拉住我的胳膊，身子有点哆嗦。“我可不能跟他们一块儿去。”他吞吞吐吐地说，点头暗示正热热闹闹漫步在街对面人行道上的那帮人。“我一想起吉米……可怜的吉姆！我一想起他，就再也没有心思喝酒了。你也是他的好朋友……是我把他拖出来的……是吧？他的头发那样短…… 确实。是我偷了那该死的饼……他不愿走……谁拖他都不愿走。”他说着泪流满面。“我从没有惹过他——从没有——从没有！”他呜咽着说，“他倒要揍我像……像……只绵羊。” Outside, on Tower Hill, they blinked, hesitated clumsily, as if blinded by the strange quality of the hazy light, as if discomposed by the view of so many men; and they who could hear one another in the howl of gales seemed deafened and distracted by the dull roar of the busy earth.—“To the Black Horse!To the Black Horse!” cried some.“Let us have a drink together before we part.” They crossed the road, clinging to one another.Only Charley and Belfast wandered off alone.As I came up I saw a red-faced, blowsy woman, in a grey shawl, and with dusty, fluffy hair, fall on Charley’s neck.It was his mother.She slobbered over him:—“O, my boy!My boy!”—“Leggo of me,” said Charley, “Leggo, mother!” I was passing him at the time, and over the untidy head of the blubbering woman he gave me a humorous smile and a glance ironic, courageous, and profound, that seemed to put all my knowledge of life to shame.I nodded and passed on, but heard him say again, good-naturedly:—“If you leggo of me this minyt—ye shall ’ave a bob for a drink out of my pay.” In the next few steps I came upon Belfast.He caught my arm with tremulous enthusiasm.—“I couldn’t go wi’ ’em,” he stammered, indicating by a nod our noisy crowd, that drifted slowly along the other sidewalk.“When I think of Jimmy...Poor Jim!When I think of him I have no heart for drink.You were his chum, too...but I pulled him out...didn’t I?Short wool he had....Yes. And I stole the blooming pie....He wouldn’t go....He wouldn’t go for nobody.” He burst into tears.“I never touched him—never—never!” he sobbed.“He went for me like...like...a lamb.”
我巧妙地脱了身。贝尔法斯特每次哭闹发作，结果总是同别人打上一架，他宣泄难以排解的痛苦时，我可不愿当靶子。再说，我们旁边还站着两名身材高大的警察，正用怀疑的目光盯着我们，眼睛里流露着对职责的无限忠诚。“再见啦！”我说罢便径自走开了。 I disengaged myself gently.Belfast’s crying fits generally ended in a fight with some one, and I wasn’t anxious to stand the brunt of his inconsolable sorrow.Moreover, two bulky policemen stood near by, looking at us with a disapproving and incorruptible gaze.—“So long!” I said, and went on my way.
在街道拐弯处，我收住脚步，向“水仙号”的水手们投去了最后一眼。他们正摇摇摆摆慢慢吞吞地走在铸币厂外面宽阔的石板路上，一路上有说有笑，热热闹闹。他们正向黑马饭店走去；那里的店员长着凶神恶煞的面孔，戴着皮帽子，不穿外套，只穿衬衣。他们从漆过的桶里取出力量、欢乐和幸福的幻影；人生的辉煌与诗歌的幻影，分给这些南行船上发过工钱的水手们。远远地，我看见他们高谈阔论，打着笨拙的手势，目光中闪烁着欢乐的光芒，而生命之海却在他们耳畔不住震响，可没人留意。他们在白色石路上晃荡，周围是匆忙喧嚣的人群，他们似乎变成了另类——迷茫、孤独、健忘、在劫难逃；他们像流浪汉，像不顾后果，只图欢乐的流浪汉，像疯狂的流浪汉，在暴风雨中还坐在危险的礁石上寻欢作乐。城市的喧嚣犹如高耸海浪的轰鸣，无情、强劲，声音洪大，目的残忍；头顶上的云散开了；普照的阳光顺着污秽房屋的墙壁泻了下来。一群黑压压的海员便徜徉在阳光里。他们的左侧，塔园里的树木在叹息，塔石熠熠发光，似乎在光的嬉戏中动了起来，仿佛突然记起了过去一切巨大的欢乐与痛苦，这正是这些人斗争的原型；抓壮丁队；暴动的叫声；妇女河边的哭声；人们庆祝胜利的欢呼声。天上的阳光仿佛是上帝赐予的礼物，照在地上的烂泥中，照在深藏记忆而又沉默不语的石头上，照在贪婪自私上，照在健忘之人焦急的脸上。黑压压的人群右侧，铸币厂漆过的正面被阳光洗得干干净净，顷刻变得雪白耀眼，仿佛成了童话故事中的大理石宫殿。“水仙号”上的水手们逐渐漂出了视线。 But at the corner I stopped to take my last look at the crew of the—Narcissus—.They were swaying irresolute and noisy on the broad flagstones before the Mint.They were bound for the Black Horse, where men, in fur caps with brutal faces and in shirt sleeves, dispense out of varnished barrels the illusions of strength, mirth, happiness; the illusion of splendour and poetry of life, to the paid-off crews of southern-going ships.From afar I saw them discoursing, with jovial eyes and clumsy gestures, while the sea of life thundered into their ears ceaseless and unheeded.And swaying about there on the white stones, surrounded by the hurry and clamour of men, they appeared to be creatures of another kind—lost, alone, forgetful, and doomed; they were like castaways, like reckless and joyous castaways, like mad castaways making merry in the storm and upon an insecure ledge of a treacherous rock.The roar of the town resembled the roar of topping breakers, merciless and strong, with a loud voice and cruel purpose; but overhead the clouds broke; a flood of sunshine streamed down the walls of grimy houses.The dark knot of seamen drifted in sunshine.To the left of them the trees in Tower Gardens sighed, the stones of the Tower gleaming, seemed to stir in the play of light, as if remembering suddenly all the great joys and sorrows of the past, the fighting prototypes of these men; press-gangs; mutinous cries; the wailing of women by the riverside, and the shouts of men welcoming victories.The sunshine of heaven fell like a gift of grace on the mud of the earth, on the remembering and mute stones, on greed, selfishness; on the anxious faces of forgetful men.And to the right of the dark group the stained front of the Mint, cleansed by the flood of light, stood out for a moment dazzling and white like a marble palace in a fairy tale.The crew of the—Narcissus—drifted out of sight.
自此以后，我再也没有见过他们。大海带走了一些，轮船接收了几个，陆地上的坟场会说明其余人的去向。毫无疑问，辛格尔顿会把他忠实工作的漫长记录，带入那热情大海的平静深渊中去。至于唐金，他一辈子也没干过一天体面活，不用说，他要谋生，只得靠他那污言秽语的雄辩，去为劳工争取生存权利。这样也好！陆地和大海各得其所。 I never saw them again.The sea took some, the steamers took others, the graveyards of the earth will account for the rest.Singleton has no doubt taken with him the long record of his faithful work into the peaceful depths of an hospitable sea.And Donkin, who never did a decent day’s work in his life, no doubt earns his living by discoursing with filthy eloquence upon the right of labour to live.So be it!Let the earth and the sea each have its own.
一个离去的船友，和其他任何人一样，永远离去了；我从未再见过他们其中任何一个。然而有时，记忆的春汛会猛力涌入黑暗的九曲河。于是，孤独的河上便会漂浮着一条船——一条阴影似的船，由一班幽灵似的水手驾驶着。他们朦朦胧胧地打了个招呼就一闪而过。难道我们不是一起在永恒的大海上，从我们罪孽的生命中挤出了一点意义吗？再见了，兄弟们！你们是一帮好人。不比任何一帮水手逊色：你们曾经狂吼着去操纵那不住拍打着的沉重的前桅帆；或者在漆黑的夜色中，攀在高空摇荡着，对着狂怒的西风高声呐喊。 A gone shipmate, like any other man, is gone for ever; and I never met one of them again.But at times the spring-flood of memory sets with force up the dark River of the Nine Bends.Then on the waters of the forlorn stream drifts a ship—a shadowy ship manned by a crew of Shades.They pass and make a sign, in a shadowy hail.Haven’t we, together and upon the immortal sea, wrung out a meaning from our sinful lives?Good-bye, brothers!You were a good crowd.As good a crowd as ever fisted with wild cries the beating canvas of a heavy foresail; or tossing aloft, invisible in the night, gave back yell for yell to a westerly gale.
全文终 THE END
